THE RED PAGE.
Stephen Phillips's ‘ Christ in Hades and Other
Poems ’ dates from 1896, so a feverish modern
reader might well deny its claims to be classed
with recent verse. Yet feverish modern readers
must surely sometimes envy the serene aplomb
of British quarterly magazines, which scruple
not to be three, or live, or ten years belated, and
tp discuss far-backs like ‘ Robert Elsmere ’ or
Heavenly twins as showing the trend of current
feminine fiction. And, on the other hand,
quarterly magazine-men must occasionally feel a
lurking qualn; when the literary trader-in-
novelties, having last night assimilated yester-
day’s book, is this afternoon afloat on a full sea
of rapturous or enraged quotations. This, at our
antipodes. Here, at their antipodes, distance
spirts the difference suggestively, so far as the
foreigner is concerned. And we can still better
the pace of local publishers’ presses, though sad
times are coming for our children, and for our
children’s children. The fault, however, will be
theirs entirely ;—they must write before they
read ;—and, for the misfortune, they maun juist
thole.
• * • *
If Bacon were alive nowadays, with the wisdom
attributed to him, he would realise that it isn’t
worth while taking all knowledge for one’s pro-
vince-even if all knowledge permitted itself to
be taken. We have reached a truer conception of
the relation of finite to infinite than Bacon had ;
though our shortcomings and our circumstances
keep lives so far short of conceptions. Nothing
profits the man who loses his own world, his only
world - for we can’t see life sub specie ceternitatis
any longer. The happy hunting-grounds are
Here and Now—
Ah, make the most of what we yet may spend,
Before we too into the Dust descend ;
Dust into Dust, and under Dust to lie,
Sans Wine, sans Song, sans Singer, and—sans End.
The relevancy of this? Well, Literature isn’t Life
—merely Life’s adjunct; that’s all. And a well-
spent life should get the fullest value out of every
hour, every minute, every moment. The present
writer, for example, doesn’t in the least desire to
be discussing Stephen Phillips just at present. It
is a beautiful morning, with a zephyr-softened
sun, and he wants to Bask out on a Beach. With
a Girl. One of Roderic Quinn’s beaches and girls,
for choice—
Pink feet and white ankles
On beaches of gold.
(Omar’s bread and wine and verses might be stowed
under a shady rock, each awaiting its mood). But
the sun-and-zephyr morning is for the present
writer dying, dying—the unreturning hours spelled
by Habit, whose trickling stream of accumulated
impressions continually petrifies more and more
brain-cells into reflex life-in-death ; or mort-
gaged for bread and cheese and kisses, books and
pictures, the hundred cravings which Thoreau
repressed after the method of the alleged savage
who chopped his toes to get his boots on. Yet
Thoreau tired of repression. Hermit-life at Walden
was heroically beautiful ; —and the hermit-hero
gladly came back home again after a couple of years.
Why ? ‘I do not think that I can tell. . . .” Per-
haps I wanted change. There ivas a little stagna-
tionit may be , about two o’clock in the afternoon.’
And, may lie, in spite of the morning and the
mood, that bask would turn out less pleasant than
this task. Stephen Phillips is not always so
attractive as the ideal <4uinn girl, but he has fewer
complications. Even Goethe found his bask-task
rhythm of life developing surprisingly unexpected
irregularities.
* * * *
Stephen Phillips’s book is a very little book
—a pamphlet. So far as here known, it is his only
one at date ; though several later poems have
been printed in ‘The Vellow Book’ and else-
where. Study of the available material brings a
suspicion that ‘The Question’ (here reprinted
July 24, ’97) comes near expressing a personal
emotion. It is the complaint of an enfevered
decadent—
Father, beneath the moonless night,
This heavy stillness without light.
There corne-i a thought which I must speak;
Why is my body then so weak?
Why do 1 falter in the race,
And flag behind this mighty pace?
Why is my strength so quickly flown?
And hark! My mother sobs alone !
There is a suggestion of physical weakness—lack
of vitality—throughout all Phillips’s work : his
phrases flicker where they ought to blaze. This
is the prettiest of the “ Other Poems ”
O thou art put to many uses, sweet!
Thy blood will urge the rose, and surge in Spring ;
But yet! . . .
And all the blue of thee will go to the sky,
And all thy laughter to the rivers run ;
But yeti t . .
Thy tumbling hair will in the West be seen,
And all thy trembling bosom in the dawn ;
But yet 1 . . .
Thy briefness in the dewdrop shall be hung,
And all the frailness of thee on the foam;
But yet! . . .
Thy soul shall be upon the moonlight spent,
Thv mystery spread upon the evening mere.
And yet ! . . .
fr'or the most part this is an extension or fresh
oresentation of old images (perhaps an analogue
night be found even for the lovely line
And all thy trembling bosom in the dawn),
and the ribbon binding the garland develops an old-
enough idea; but the combination is exquisite.
(Mem.—To consider whether it is more meritorious
iu a poet to announce a new beauty—if there be
new beauties—-than to delicately deck an old one;
whether the memory of old beauties is curse or
blessing to the reader, and whether it should be a
curse to the poet.)
* • * •
‘ Christ in Hades ’ is built more massively.
Christ comes to Hades ; and for a moment there
is a sense of Life and Spring in the horrid place
of torment—
Over the head of Jesus the whole sky
Of pain began to drive: old punishments
Diswreathing drooped, and legendary dooms
Dispersing hung, and lurid history streamed.
And in many fine phrases, very sadly sweet, yet
frequently wanting vital pulsations, a dozen
heroic pages pass. The business of Hades is
momentarily suspended, and dim sad ghosts drift
up to Christ enquiringly.
Then stole a woman up to him, and said:
‘ Although I know thee not, yet I can tell
That only a great love hath brought thee hither.
Didst thou so ail in brightness, and couldst not rest
For thinking of some woman ? Was thy bed
So empty, cold thy hearth, and aimless glides
Thy wife amidst us? Whom then dost thou seek?
For see, we are so changed : thou wouldst not know
The busy form that moved about thy fire.
She has no occupation, and no care,
No little tasks. Owe hud pleasant homes.
And often we remember husbands dear,
That were most kind, and wonder after them.
My little children ! Who sings to them now?
Return then to the earth I Thou canst not fetota
Thy drooping listless woman to the air.
Thou’lt have no comfort out of her at all.
* » * *
Then follows a beautiful passage, the most fas-
cinating in the poem—
But one broke in on her with eager words.
‘ See how we live along exhausted streams,
Eluding forests, and dispersing hills ;
O but I gloried and drank and wept and laughed
Give me again great life 1 To dare, to enjoy,
To explore, never to tire, to be alive,
And full of blood, and young, to risk, to love 1
The bright glory of after battle wine,
The flushed recounting faoes, the stern hum
Of burnished armies, thrill of unknown seas 1'
As he was speaking, slowly all the dead
The melancholy attraction of Jesus felt;
And millions, like a sea, wave upon wave,
Heaved dreaming to that moonlight face, or ran
In wonderful long ripples, sorrow-charmed.
Toward him in faded purple, pacing came
Dead emperors, and sad unflattered kings;
Unlucky captains listless armies led ;
Poets with musio frozen on their lips,
Toward the pale Brilliance sighed ; until at last
Antiquity, like evening gathering,
With mild and starry faoes, gradually
Had stolen up.
* * * •
Occasionally the poet moves with Milton’s
majesty—
The dead, caught in intolerable hope,
Hither and hither burning rushed, or fell
Imploring him to leave them cold ; but Christ
Came through them : leading irresistibly
Not Western spirits alone : but all that world
Was up! and after him in passion swept
Dead Asia, murmuring, and the buried North !
Presently Christ comes to Prometheus on his crag,
still fixed in torment, though ‘all these ancient
prisoners released.’ Says unredeemed Prome-
theus—
Tlry hands are too like mine to undo these bonds,-
Brother, although the dead world follow thee,
Deep-fascinated : love hath marred us both,
And one yearning, as wide as is the world.
O how thy power leaves thee at this cross!
And Christ
knew that for a time the great advance
He must delay, postponing our desire.
* » * *
The motive of the whole might be Matthew
Arnold’s
Wandering between two worlds, one dead,
The other powerless to be born,
With nowhere yet to rest my head,
Like these, on earth I wait, forlorn.
The Christ-faith sufficed to save the ancient
world ; the modern world, of which Prometheus,
the re volt-spirit, is exemplar, finds it a consola-
tion transient, nugatory. The conception is finely
treated, and the result remains (in spite of many
eclioes) on the high poetic level. A certain want
of savage force, a certain academic attenuation of
style, are hardly felt as drawbacks; theme and
scene seem to exact half-lights and melancholy
greys.
* » » *
One notes Phillips’s heroic-verse developments
with perplexity. Quit the canons, if you will ;
abolish the five accents even ; but surely you must
permit the musio to remain. And is there heroic
music in lines like—
Smells m the dark the cold odour of earth.
Milton wrote—
Rose like a steam of rich distilled perfumes.
Would it improve his music to write—
Rose like a gleam of rich dlst'lled perfumes—
With stress on the ‘dis,’ and not on the ‘ tilled ’
after the Phillips manner. Here is another
Phillips line —
The bright glory of after-battle wine.
On the same plan Milton would have written—
To men mortal, he, with his horrid orew,
instead of—
To mortal men, he, with his horrid orew.
And when Milton knew better, did he not know
better ?
—♦
J. Le Gay Brereton is out again with another
batch of_ verses to the girl who has undone him.
“ Let him marry the girl ! ” unfortunately can-
not apply here, for the girl has already
married another fellow and all the verses
(three books of ’em) are so many stones heaped
on the grave of a passion which won’t lie quiet.
It is to be feared this reads as it is not meant —
unkindly ; and the point of view is certainly most
unfair. When a great poet loses his mistress,
and wails her in immortal song, we stand with
bared heads in the sanctuary of his grief ; and
when a small poet loses his mistress, and pro-
cesses funerally behind the hearse, we can only
note that the horses are lame and the trappings
tarnished. Yet one should be able to see the
small poet’s great grief through all his inadequa-
cies of expression, and share it as we share a
Dante’s.
. . . the poor beetle that we tread upon
In corporal sufferance finds a pang as great
As when a giant dies.
But imperfect human nature can’t sympathise
with beetles—even with that interesting member
of the species, the sexton beetle, necrophorus
vespillo, which finds an appropriate nidus for its
eggs in dead bodies. The worst of it is that
Brereton turns out to have been the author of
’ Perdita, a Sonnet Record ’ —which, as he shows
by quotation at the end of the new book, was
anathematised on this page as ‘. . . an abomina-
tion of bloodlessness . . . flat batch of rhymes.’
Does it not look brutal to so characterise the
work of a tender-hearted poet (for Brereton in
most essentials is a poet; he has much sym-
pathy and some fancy, and a cultured ap-
preciation of the beautiful and of the true,
and a fine hatred of cant and injustice—and
these things always underlie his verses and
hardly ever are seen in them). But what is a
critic to do? You must uphold standards avid
ideals; and when a poetical skull has to be hit,
it has to be hit —not tapped—more particularly if
it is none of your Keats-eggshell variety, but a
good old Donnybrnoker that merely shakes off a
fly wherever the blackthorn lights.
* • * *
If anything, the new book, ‘Sweetheart Mine,’
tastes a little better than ‘Perdita’ (Brere-
ton’s first book, ‘The Song of Brotherhood,’ con-
tains his best work—presumably it was written
when he was a little younger, and his pulse beat a
little faster, and the girl was a nearer, diviner
inspiration). But the fault of ‘Perdita’ is
4 Sweetheart’s ’ _ fault—in the bulk she is
flat and unoriginal. Occasionally comes a
sentiment with a little fibre in it—as in ‘ Comi-
tatus,’ with its refrain
For I’m loved by all the children of the town.
And in “ Nightfall ’ you get a shadow of what
Brereton might do— would do —and cannot do.
Let it come soothingly, by way of grace after
tough meat, or benediction after a bad sermon.
been treated will appear at price' in the treatment
of Arthur Symons. Symons has poetically lost a
girl, just like Brereton ; and there is nothing but
praise for the way in which he has lost her. At
all events, for the first way ; he lost her once
nobly, in 1 Mundi Victima ’ (wliich appeared first
in ‘The Savoy’ magazine), and once ig-
nobly in the series of 14-lilie stanzas
printed together with ‘ Mundi ’ in his new
book, ‘ Amoris Victima.’ Comparison of the two
ways is curiously interesting. it looks as if
Symons wrote ‘Mundi’ (his masterpiece—on a
level with his previous best, ami better than his
best because fuller, more sustained) while still
shivering and burning with the ague-fits of
passion. Then the idea seized him to em-
body in a book the preliminaries of the
passion, and he sat down deliberately
to write up his dead emotions—and failed,
because no man can deliberately resuscitate
dead emotions. So all these 14-line preliminaries
are among the stiffest, dullest things Symons has
done—entirely lacking the lyrism, fervor, fluidity
for which we ordinarily praise him. Probably
Rossetti’s ‘ House of Life ’ suggested such a
casket for the ashes of old flame; but even
Rossetti -failed for the most part, failed in
the sequence and in many items of the sequence,
and Symons has failed more obviously than
Rossetti—because, for one thing, Symons’s seven-
couplet stanza has not the melody and distinction
of sonnet-form ; and because, for another thing,
Rossetti was the greater master of phrase and
sound, and labored his images more. ‘ Sequences 1
of sonnets or such, in fact, can only be effective
when each sonnet in the sequence culminates from
a wave of emotion ; when you try to build heart-
chambers in accordance with a prearranged archi-
tectural plan the result is uniformly bad.
* * * *
‘ Mundi Vietima ’ may speak a little, brokenly,
for her magnificent self. (After all, only the primal
impulses can heat a bard to white-hot poetry.
Eating and drinking are mostly voted too-base
stimuli ; civilised man doesn’t fight more than ha
can help; what but Love is left to inspire us?)
Love, to the world, is the forbidden thing ;
And rightly, for the world is to the strong,
And the world’s honor and increase must belong
To the few mighty triumphing through hate
And to the many meek who humbly wait
The gi udging wage of doily drudgery.
The world is made for hale, for apathy,
For laboring greed that mines the earth for gold,
And sweats to gather dust into its hold :
Is not the world bought for a little dust?
Kingdoms are shaken from their ancient trust,
And kingdoms stablished upon treacheries;
Under the temple-roof of the same skies
The stones of altars older than their gods
Are beaten down, and in the old abodes
The smoke of a new incense blinds the stars)
The rind of earth is eaten up by wars,
As a rat, gnawing, leaves a mouldering heap ;
And the world drowses in a downy sleep,
The world being sworn confederate with sucoess.
Yet will it pardon the forgetfulness
Of laughing loves that linger but a night
In the soft perfumed chambers of delight.
How should it pardon love? love whose intent
Is from the world to he in banishment,
Love that admits but fealty to one,
Love that is ever in rebellion.
The world is made for dutiful restraint,
Its martyrs are the lover and the saint,
All whom a fine and solitary rage
Urges on some ecstatic pilgrimage
In search of any Holy Sepulchre.
The lover is a lonely voyager
Over great seas and into lonely lands,
He speaks a tongue which no man understands,
Much given to silence, no good citizen,
His utmost joy to be apart from men,
For ids creating mind has given birth,
God-like, to anew heaven and a new earth )
Where, if he dwell apart or in the crowd,
He talks with angels in a fiery cloud
Upon the mount of vision all his days.
Therefore tire world, beholding in his face
Only the radiance of reflected light
Left by that incommunicable sight,
Which to the dim eyes of the world may seen
But the marsh-glimmer of a fevered dream,
Bids love renounce love, or be cast aside.
Has not the world’s hate ever crucified,
From age to age, rejoicing in its loss,
Love on the same inevitable cross,
In every incarnation from above
Of the redeeming mystery of love?
NIGHTFALL.
Why must you leave hie at the close of day,
When all the heat of twelve long hours is pent
Here in my thudding heart ? What day hath spent
Of light and life and love is stored away
Within the coffer of my soul; and may
Your hand not turn the hey, since God hath sent
You as His angel ? Know you what he meant?
My place is with you, darling. Let me stay.
Alone—how long?—while day through other lands
Makes progress till our east again is grey,
In dreams I hear the secret words you say,
With throbbing temples and with trembling hands.
I know your will is not with your commands.
Your dreams with mine are mated. Let me stay.
That is nicely put; and one sincerely hopes she
let him stay.

Hards of the Backb
France knows how to administer castor oil
to children. Instead of gripping the nose and
pouring the horrid stuff down, the new way is to
pour the oil into a pan over a moderate fire, break
an egg into it, and keep it well stirred till this
castor omelette is well set': then it is appetisingly
served, with a little salt and sugar, and some
currant jelly.
In the West (N.S.W.), they have found
another defect in the ’95 Land Act. If resumed
areas (the portions of runs open to selection, and
held only from day to day on license) are not
likely to be wanted for settlement, they may be
added to the leasehold, and the holders then get
fairly secure tenure —good anyhow, for
them to improve the land on. Now some big
holdings contain a little country which may be
required for settlement, but a great deal more
which will certainly not be so required, and
which, without improvement, is next to valueless.
r he Act, however, doesn’t apparently contemplate
ueh an obvious state of things, and only pr<>> ;rles
for the attachment or non-atr,iehtnent_ of the-lot.
So another “ scheme ” of amendment is promised.
THE NEARSIDE LEADER.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
When the gear is on the horses and the knotted
trace-chains hooked ;
When the last bale’s on the waggon and the
ropes are twitched and tied ;
When the brakes 'are off the big wheels and the
waybills safely booked,
You can see the old gray leader with his wise
head turned aside ;
Does a memory come o’er him
Of the long, stiff road before him,
With the lead-chains never slackened as he holds
his team to work,
Through the box-fiats and the gidyeas,
Ninety miles of plain and ridges,
To the white-railed Darling bridges and the silver
roofs of Bourke ?
Just a whisper from his master and he leans upon
the weight,
And the twenty browns behind him touch the
collar when he moves,
Then the whip rings out a warning, and the under-
carriage grates.
And they bend their backs and lift her from the
well-worn loading grooves.
So they open up the tourney,
And she starts her long, rough journey
Over ninety miles of noonday and the evenings in
between,
And the station gates have freed her,
With the station men to speed her,
And it’s “ Buckle down, my leader, on the road
you’ve often been ! ”
Now the red dust curls behind her, and the red
dust rolls before,
And from shafter up to leader they are sweat
from head to hip,
And the good ones take the collar, and the bad
ones baulk and bore,
But the grey horse strains the harder every time
he bears the whip ;
So, by lash and lurid order,
They will swing her through the Border,
With the dust upon her loading making extra
weight to pull,
And the drunken township loafer
Staggers blindly from his sofa
Just to see the first team over with the Tliurul-
goona wool.
Oh ! the camping by the river when the sun is
riding low ;
Oh ! the shifting of the collars and the dropping
of the chains;
And the music of the big bells, as they let the
horses go
To their drinking in the river and their feeding
on the plains !
So, from camp to camp-fire, daily,
They will battle through Belalie,
Till they leave the plains behind them and the
river at their back,
Where the stony hills are showing
There is panting now, and blowing,
But the grey horse keeps them going with the
chains that never slack.
So the toe-clips cut the roadway where a thousand
hoofs have trod,
While above the gold sun glistens and to West
the red sun flames
To the creaking of the waggon and the lurching of
the load
And the grinding of the tug-chains in the hooks
upon the hames ;
And the leader’s heart thumps loudly,
But he bends his old neck proudly
As he swings them through the bridges, sticking
staunchly to his work ;
And I wish the gray could hear him
When a stranger says, “ I’d spare him,
For there’s not a horse comes near him in the
teams that draw to Bourke ! ”
• ••*•#
Oh! it’s grand to bring the largest loads from
Thurulgoona side,
And it’s grand to have a leader that the smallest
child knows well;
But, if you love an honest horse, when next the
ropes are tied
You’ll leave him in the bluegrass, for the gray
has earned a spell.
He has borne the brunt of battle,
He has led your lagging cattle
With the red galls on his shoulder, yet he never
shirked a start;
And ’twere better you should brain him
Ere you burst him up and strain him,
For, just think, each trip you chain him helps to
break a willing heart!
WILL. H. OGILVIR
THE KING’S MERCIE.
[The Armenians are petitioning the Sultan, etc., etc.
The tribesmen are supplicating for merer, etc., etc.—See
daily press übique.]
Hast seen a crop of golden grain
With bended head ask Heaven for rain ?
Hast seen, responsive to the call,
The gathering showers prepare to fall ?
Hast noted, then, some cloud accurst
In million icy missiles burst,
Until the cornfields droop and fail
’Neath fusillade of direful hail ?
So wilt thou see a suppliant crowd
Implore the grace of despot proud ;
So wilt thou hear his promise fair,
His kingly cant of loving care ;
And while the people humbly bow,
With bated breath and fearful brow,
Thou’lt see the dews of mercy fall
In showers of shot and shell and ball.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 6, 1897.
Judge Windeyer Apologises.
Out of his grave comes Judge Windeyer’s
apology—his stumbling, hesitating apology. It
seems that he believed it a justification, and
that his friends believe it—or try to believe it—
a justification. So sincerely can a man mis-
understand himself—so easily can his friends
persuade themselves into misunderstanding
him ! The Windeyer apology is a deliberate
attempt to falsify history, and as such must be
resented.
* * • *
In the Dean case, Judge Windeyer was not
Judge ; but advocate, partisan. He took the
side of the prosecution against the prisoner
Dean. Throughout the trial, he emphasised
for the jury every point which could tell
against Dean, and slurred every point which
could tell in Dean’s favor. At the close, he
ridiculed the defence and demanded a convic-
tion.
* « * *
The jury hesitated. Judge Windeyer was
astonished ; he knew how strongly he had put
the case against the prisoner. “ The jury re-
tired, and remained long in consultation. The
Judge grew impatient. He could not conceive,
he said, how, in so plain a case, they should
even have left the box. He sent a messenger
to tell them that, if they did not instantly
return, he would adjourn the court and lock
them up all night. Thus put to the torture,
they came, but came to say they doubted
whether the charge had been made out.
Jeffreys expostulated with them vehemently,
and, after another consultation, they gave a
reluctant verdict of Guilty.”
• * * *
These are Macaulay’s words, describing the
conduct of Bloody Jeffreys at the trial of
Alice Lisle ; and it is singular (as Melb.
Argus pointed out) how exactly they fit
the Dean case. The jury in the Dean case
remained in deliberation for eight hours
from 12.25 on Saturday afternoon till 8.25 on
Saturday night. Then Judge Windeyer sent a
messenger bidding them return into court. The
case was so plain, he said, and they had been
so long deliberating, that he thought he mus t
have failed to put the matter rightly before
them. It was almost a scandal upon the admin-
istration of justice that they could not come to a
conclusion. He could, understand their sympa-
thy, and a recommendation to mercy, and a
strong one too ; but unless they agreed he would
have to lock them up till Monday morning.
* * • *
This is the modified language of the news-
paper reports, but it does not give in its full
brutality the impression which the Judges
remarks made upon the jury. He in effect
told them : “ You must convict the prisoner. I
am astonished that you should not convict him
at once. Your delay is scandalous to justice.
If some of you have any qualms, I’ll tell you
what I’ll do : I’ll recommend the man to mercy.
But if you don’t bring in your verdict promptly
I’ll look you up till Monday morning.”
* * * *
That was the impression which Judge Win-
deyer’s language left upon people in court, and
upon most of the jury. He bribed w'hile he
threatened. It was the jury’s duty to convict
Dean. If they did, Dean would not be hanged.
But, if they did not make up their minds
sharply, they would be locked up for 34 hours
—a formidable threat to men sickened with the
confinement of a three days’ trial. The cajoled
and bullied jury retired again, and in seven
minutes returned with a verdict of Guilty—-and
a strong recommendation to mercy ! “ lhen
you voted ‘Guilty’ in the belief that Judge
Windeyer would endeavor to get Dean a. re-
prieve ? ” asked a reporter of one of the jury-
mien. “Certainly,” was the reply. “If the
Judge hadn’t said w’hat he did say I would
have stayed there, and at least have given the
man another show on the Monday. There was
a sort of pledge to me, I consider.”
» * * *
What can justify a Judge in thus usurping
the functions of a jury, and forcing them with
all the weight of his authority to give their
verdict, not in accordance with the evidence as
they are sw’orn to do, but in accordance with
his opinion or his prejudices ? What can jus-
tify a Judge in such a calculating threat of
physical torture unless the jury bring in the
verdict he bids them bring in ? What can jus-
tify a Judge in suggesting such an immoral
compromise—in bargaining that if the jury
will force their consciences to give a verdict of
“ Guilty ” he will lessen the severity of the
sentence ? Such language, such conduct, would
be disgraceful and intolerable if George Dean
had attempted to poison fifty wives.
* * * *
Judge Windeyer is said to have considered
that “ the ill-feeling ” shown against him was
invoked by “ those who were jealous of his
success, or who had cause to hate his stern im-
partiality (!) in administering justice.” Noth-
ing of the kind : it was his own acts which in-
voked it. Judge Windeyer’s only enemy was
himself. But because he was himself, he
became, in his position on the Supreme Court
bench of N.S. Wales, the enemy of every citi-
zen who valued public morals and respected
public justice. No one who did not personally
know him cared a straw for Windeyer the
man. But it was everybody’s business to
denounce Windeyer the unjust Judge.
• * * *
In his apology for the Dean case Judge Win-
deyer is represented as putting forward two
pleas—one that it had been represented as tech-
nically uncertain whether a jury could be
locked up over Sunday, and he wished to re-
move any chance of misapprehension from
their minds ; the other that a son of the fore-
man of the jury had died suddenly, and there
would be question of the funeral. Both
pleas are worthless. Judge Windeyer had no
right to interrupt the deliberations of the jury
in the matter of George Dean’s life by the
significant hint that they would suffer great
personal inconvenience if they did not bring in
their verdict promptly. Under the circum-
stances the hint was a threat. As to the second
excuse, the foreman of the jury was ignorant of
his son’s death, and it might have been just as
merciful to let him remain in ignorance for a
few hours longer. In any case, the full and
free deliberation of the jury should not have
been interfered with, even for the sake of 50
funerals ; the question at issue was the funeral
of Dean. A Judge may sympathise with
private griefs, but he has to look first to his
public duty. Until the jury’s verdict is pro-
nounced, the prisoner is his first and sacred
charge, more especially when life or death may
hang on the issue.
* * * *
Nor is it correct to say that Judge Windeyer
was “ completely vindicated ” by the ultimate
issue of the Dean embroglio. He was not vin-
dicated in the least. The verdict was a right
verdict; but the means which Judge Windeyer
employed to secure it were disgracefully wrong.
His conduct at the trial remains quite un-
affected by the outcome : no evil-doer is “ vin-
dicated ” by the good that may spring from his
deed. George Dean was indeed justly sen-
tenced, but he was unjustly tried ; and the
memory of Judge Windeyer cannot be freed
from the stigma of that unjust trial. Nor is it
desirable that it should be. It is lawful and
right that the mistakes of a public servant
should be remembered, however he or his
friends may privately regret them. The cairn
that marks his slip is the community’s warning
to his successors.
* * * *
And an additional scandal in the Dean case
was this: that Judge Windeyer’s zeal as a
partisan aided the failure of the prosecution to
properly present the case to the jury ; with the
result that Dean was found guilty, at the
Judge’s instigation, on insufficient evidence—
evidence which did not amount to proof—evi-
dence which justified a verdict of Not Guilty.
When the case was re-heard before a special
Commission, a majority of the Commission
decided that Dean’s guilt was not proven.
More, a majority of the jurymen said that, had
they had before them the fuller evidence which
was placed before the Commission, they would
certainly have acquitted Dean. Proof of Dean’s
guilt was eventually forthcoming, but not until
long after the trial. The final issue showed
indeed that Judge Windeyer had guessed
correctly ; but the ideal of Justice is not a
learned pig which grunts among the cards and
triumphantly picks out the knave. Were any
man again to receive the mockery of trial which
Dean received, and to be found guilty on the
evidence on which Dean was found guilty, it
would again be the duty of the community to
agitate for a re-opening of the case, and it
would again be the duty of a Commission,
which had before it the facts presented to the
Dean Commission, to recommend that the
prisoner be released.
Judge Windeyer’s apology says nothing of
the Suffield case, in which he bullied a jury
into a verdict of Guilty on evidence which is a
tissue of absurdities and contradictions. It
says nothing of the Mount Rennie case—that
series of judicial murders ; that brutal outrage
on justice to which, as W. B. Dalley declared, a
parallel could only be discovered in some of the
darkest pages of Irish history ; that infamous
parody of a trial in which, as the Sydney
Morning Herald said (though not till the boys
were on the scaffold) that the sittings were pro-
tracted from day to day to such an extreme as
to exhaust the physical and mental energies of
counsel and jurymen alike. In the Mount
Rennie case Judge Windeyer wrung from the
jury and the Executive four lives (one of them
that of Read, probably an altogether innocent
man) ; and five other prisoners lingered lohg in
prisons and lunatic asylums. Eight of them
were youths about or under 20 ; and they were
charged with assaulting a girl of Uncertain
character, uncertain in the identification of her
assailants. Judge Windeyer allowed no doubt,
no hesitation, to come between the hangman and
his victims.
* » * •
The Mount Rennie prisoners were, without
exception, defended by junior barristers, some
of whom actually got not one penny for their
arduous task. The accused were, in one sense,
practically undefended —they had no adequate
means of hunting up the antecedents of the
witnesses against them, and the police and the
Crown Law Department—whose business is,
and has ever been, to show one side of the case
only, and get a conviction —made no effort to
do so. The Mount Rennie trial was the most
hideous affair of its kind in all the post-convict
days of Australia. The first day (Monday) the
court sat for 11 hours; the second day for IL|
hours; the third and fourth days (Wednesday
and Thursday) it only sat for hours each
day, for on the evening of each of these two
days Judge Windeyer “had to attend” a
fashionable public dinner given in honor of a
man with whom he was to voyage to England
the following week. On the fifth day (Friday)
proceedings began at 9 a.m. and went on until
3.30 a.m . on Saturday hours). Then (the
jury and barristers and Judge having had
hours wherein to eat and sleep and wash) the
Court re-assetnbled at 9 the same morning and
went on until past 11 at night (over 14 hours),
when nine of the accused were found guilty
and sentenced to death ! The counsel for the
prosecution got his statement ih at the early
part of the week, when the 12 men in the box
were still fully awake and able to understand
what was said to them. When the defence
came on, the Court sat for over hours with
hours intermission !
» * * *
The prisoners slept fitfully in the dock.
Read (afterwards hanged, with three Other
youths of the average age of 18 years only) slept
in the dock after his counsel, T. C. O’Mara,
(who was dying on his feet and had then been
15 consecutive hours in court) had begun the
mockery of addressing the jury on his behalf
at midnight; the lawyers dozed intermittently
at their table ; the jurymen nodded at inter-
vals in the box, and “ between whiles ” passed
a salts bottle up and down their ranks for the
purpose of stimulation ; one juryman was so ill
from sheer exhaustion that his life was said to
be in danger, and counsel might almost as well
have wasted words on a corpse. When an
adjournment was applied for at midnight on
Friday (by a poorly-paid counsel suffering from
phthisis, who declared that he was utterly
exhausted), Judge Windeyer pointed out to
him (thus does history anticipate itself !) that
unless the trial proceeded the jury “ would be
kept over Sunday ” —“ thereby throwing on
me,” as the counsel afterwards said, “ the
responsibility of choosing between offending
the jury and absolutely wasting my speech on
behalf of the wretched boy I w’as defending.”
And so, scrambling on night and day in the
fetid court and the sweltering atmosphere of
midsummer, the dozing lawyers feebly address-
ing the sleeping jury, and, the prisoners occa-
sionally waking up in the dock to hear them,'
the Case jolted along to the midnight verdict
and the inevitable gallows.
* * * *
Judge Windeyer’s apology says nothing of
these cases or of the numerous similar cases
buried in the records of his long judicial career.
Who shall count all the cruelties and injustices
he has committed in the name of the law, all
the tortures inflicted by him on poorly-defended
prisoners on trial for their lives in a time of
panic, when he should have been an unim-
passioned umpire to stand between them and
public frenzy ! The Bulletin’s attention was
first drawn to Judge Windeyer by an acciden-
tal visit to one of his hideous midnight trials,
where Judge Lynch’s bell was being rung and
Judge Lynch’s rope was being greased by a
man wearing the uniform of the Supreme Court,
and where there was to be seen, not the steady
swing of tHe pendulum of Justice, but what
looked like the eager glutting of a savage per-
sonal revenge. Thereafter The Bulletin
watched Judge Windeyer’s career, and saw as
the years rolled on more and more reason for
accounting him a menace to the respect in which
all citizens should be able to hold the Supreme
Court of Justice of their country. It saw that
the strong intellect of this masterful man was
adapted only to dealing with abstract legal
questions or dryasdust commercial cases ; and
it denounced him because whenever he sat on
the bench in a criminal case involving lust or
blood he became a raging partisan, and ever
sought to rob the jury of their lawful authority
and bend them to his wishes. For years
Judge Windeyer was only maintained in
power because his brother-judges did not dare,
even when personally challenged, to take steps
to punish the innumerable printed criminal
libels evoked by his tyrannous actions on cir
cuit; because he had the tacit support of th«
toadying legal trades-union, which, though it
utterly abhorred the Judge, quaked before
“ the one strong man ” of the bench ; and be-
cause there were enough lawyers in the House
and in the Ministry to burke every attempt
made to finally arraign him, and to take in Par*
liament an open vote as to his fitness to remain
on the bench. And Judge Windeyer had almost
until the last the thick-aud-thin support of the
daily press—both in the way of advocacy and
of suppression; and it was the daily press
which, at the most critical periods of his career,
urged him to still wilder judicial excesses.
* * * *
And now Judge Windeyer is dead—and de
mortuis nil nisi bonum ? Not so ; that is a say*
ing which narrow minds too often Use to the
public detriment in order to cloak individual
ill-doing. De mortuis nil nisi verilni is the
better maxim; for,neither the dead not the
living can ever merit more than the truth,
The Bulletin believes it has spoken truth re-
garding Judge WinDeyer —it spoke it during
his life, and it speaks it equally liow that
he is dead, for (let not history be falsified !)
there is equal need of it. The crop of sor-
rows which he sowed is not yet reaped.
Judge Windeyer’s public actions have outlived
him ; and it is those alohe with which The
Bulletin is Concerned. He was a public
trustee who dishonored his trust, and it is
before public opinion, for the profit of the com-
munity and for the warning of those in posi-
tions like to his, that Judge Windeyer must be
pilloried. It is suggested that “ venal malice”
has actuated this paper. Those who wish to
believe such a charge may believe it; but it
makes no difference to the facts. Upon them
let Judge Windeyer be praised or condemned.
They speak with no uncertain voice to anyone
with a natural love of Justice, a natural hatred
of Oppression.
Labor-Conditions in Mining Leases.
The Westralian Mining Commission lias uti*
earthed some interesting witnesses ; but none
more interesting than the representatives of
European companies—until, at any rate, they
tired the listener with their monotonous stories
of the evils of insecure tenure, and correspond-
ing flap-doodle about the blessings that would
fall on everybody if only the mines were handed
over absolutely to the discoverers or their
assigns* Mr. Modest Maryanski had a good
innings of this sort. As usual, the “poor
miner ” had most of his thoughts. This “ poor
miner” would be best served by the American
legislation and systems ; if he spent a certain
considerable sum of money on a lease (and
“ poor miners ” have always large sums of
money, of course), it should become his pro-
perty for ever. But, while it was still leaser
hold, the “ poor miner,” who should not be
compelled to work it ot pay rent for it
for the first 12 months, ought not to be
harassed by stich stupid things as labor
conditions. And to ask a tvardeh for ex-
emption—Why, it reminded Mr. Modest
Maryanski of schoolboys seeking a vacation:
“ How absurd it is to have to go to Sir John
Forrest —(ah ! sb they do go to Sir John, do
they ?) —and say, * Mister John, I want to go
to see my mamma ! ’ ” (Loud laughter, of
course.) Also, among other things, Mr.
Maryanski believed, or, rather, he said, that in
order to compel men to develop their claims
the}' should have to spend so much per year;
that public batteries, perhaps State-aided,
should be provided ; and that companies should
be given bonuses for sinkirg deep shafts. But,
above and before all, secure tenure was requi-
site. Moreover, as if Mr. Modest Maryanski’s
word was not sufficient, it was observed that
Bismarck had given John, and the Govt., and
the Commission the only straight and reliable
tip in dealing with mines and companies—“l
give that I may get.” Finally, Mr. Maryan-
ski is quite sure, in fact, he guarantees, that if
the mining law's are changed in the man-
ner he suggests “ everyone will be satisfied,
and in particular the prospector, who not
only w'ill be able to live here but will bring
his family.”
* * » *
At this point The Bulletin leaves Mr.
Maryanski to see a vision of Mr. Mar-
yanski’s heaven. It is out Siberia way, and
Bill Giles is on a prospecting tour along
with his missus and his 13 assorted youngsters.
That is all—except Bill Gilb;s’ family pets.
But Bill Giles, after all, isn’t everybody.
For every prospector (who, by the way, isn t,
generally speaking, much of a family man)
there are about 300 w'ages miners (who, gene-
rally speaking, are very much family men);
and what of these miners if Mr. Maryanski s
schemes were put into operation ? In the first
place, the Big Blow Underlay Extended, having
to spend so much money during tlm year,
would crowd on hands, and do its “ little
bit” in, say, three months; whereupon, if
things weren’t over-bright in the share-market,
or if Warnford Lock’s twin brother happened
to be manager, it would probably say that it
didn’t want any men just then, thank you—-
unless, that is (this in the ear of a reliable bell-
wether) they cared to put in a month or tw'O at
a slightly reduced wage. And in most cases
and at many times they would accept the
inevitable and the reduced wages ; and next
week the Big Blow South Extended would
notify that it could not pay any more than the
Underlay Extended was getting its men for.
• * * *
The curse of almost every mining population
is the want of uniformity in the mining opera-
tions. To-day there is a boom, calls coine in,
mines and things alleged to be mines are
worked, w'orkmen flock thither —and then
comes the slump, and the suspension of opera-
tions, and the “cutting” of wages and the
long-continued perish, and shipments of hungry,
worried workmen from under-the-cloud Ben
digos to the relief of modern Lucknows. Mr.
Maryanski’s scheme would be a great help in
making these movements rather moie frequen
and more disastrous even than they are wit l
out it—in part directly by reason ot 1 s
own badness, and in part indirectly y
promoting market booms and slumps ant
generally playing into the hands of the mar e


operator. Mr. Maryanski has, however, better
foundation for liis complaints regarding insecure
tenure. It is bad (unless we seriously intend
to take over the working of the mines our-
selves, in which case it is still also probably
bad) to limit the life of a lease to 15, 18, or 21
years—bad, because the lessee, if he believes
that he will not get a renewal, will be tempted
to pick out the eyes of his mine and so render
unprofitable all the low-grade stuff that could
be worked profitably in conjunction with the
good stuff; or the development will not be
carried out on a workmanlike basis. Twenty
years may seem a fairly long time in the history
of a young country ; but what is it by com-
parison with the centuries during which the
mines of, say, Cornwall, or even Mexico,
have been worked ? Little more can be
done in twenty years than to begin
laying the foundations of a really great
mine. To say, however, that shareholders
have no confidence in anything but absolute
private ownership is nonsense, seeing that
comparatively few of the metal mines in
Great Britain are worked by freeholders. What
they haven’t got used to yet is the liability to
forfeiture if they don’t keep men at work on
the leases. But when it is impressed on them
that this condition is imposed in lieu of rent—
that, in short, instead of demanding the high
tribute and rent and wayleaves which the
private owner demands, the more benevolent
State requires little beyond a nominal cash con-
sideration, provided that its tenants undertake
to keep a certain number of men in employ-
ment—less objection will be offered to the Aus-
tralian system. But if the foreign capialist
doesn’t get over his objection—well, his indigna-
tion doesn’t cyanide the metal out of the mines,
anyway. On almost precisely the same
grounds (which are not, it may be observed,
quite Mr. Maryanski’s grounds), suspension of
labor-conditions is reprehensible.
* * * *
If it were more explicitly stated that the
undertaking to keep a certain number of men
at work was as much part of the rent as the
cash payment itself is, fewer suspensions would
probably be granted. Indeed, less harm would
be done if, instead of relieving the liard-up
lessee of the labor conditions, the Government
agreed to forego the rent proper. In that case,
the loss would be spread over the whole com-
munity ; in the other, the burden is cast upon
the shoulders of the few, who surely have a right
to expect that the State will insist as much upon
one condition of a case as upon another. Suspen-
sions, however, would not be in much demand if
the State did not, from greed or blindness, or
out of sheer cussedness, offer inviting mining
leases, coupled with economically impossible
conditions, to anybody in respect of anything
—no matter whether the intention is to mine
or whether there is the smallest possibility of
success or not. And in that respect, anyway,
a lesson might be learned from the Continent—
or, for that matter, from South America—
where it is the pretty general rule for a govt,
to want to be assured that a man isn’t either a
shark, or an infatuated crank, or an utterly
ignorant and misguided fool, before it issues a
mineral lease to him. Especially it ought to
want to be assured against the shark.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Treason.
The conspiracy in N.S.W. Assembly of the
cabbage-tree-hat mob (who instinctively hate
Federation), with the parochial element (which
cannot understand it), and with the live-on-
the-game politicians (who naturally dread it),
has proved very successful ; and, so far as the
Assembly is concerned, N.S.W. deliberately
turns her back on the other provinces, and
deliberately breaks the federal compact entered
into with them. It was originally provided
that N.S.W. was to accept Federation if
50,000 votes were cast in its favor. The
cabbage-tree-hat mob, the parochialists, and
the live-on-the-game politicians, have suc-
ceeded in raising this minimum to 80,000 votes
—and, unless the Legislative Council throws
out the Bill, the Federal cause is just
30,000 votes nearer damnation. A fortnight
ago The Bulletin pointed out how im-
probable it was that 80,000 votes would be
obtained, since there will be only an abstract
question to vote about—without Freetrade or
Protection, or Romanism or Orangeism, or per-
sonal friendship or enmity, to excite the public
mind ; and the average elector may be relied on
to take as little trouble about an abstract ques-
tion as possible. And the fact that, of those
who do take the trouble to vote, a vast majority
are in favor of Federation, will not count in the
least; since, if only 1000, or 100, or 10 electors
vote against it—even if nobody at all votes
against it, and 79,999 electors vote for
it, Federation, so far as N.S. Wales is
concerned, will be as dead as mutton. And
without N.S.W., the other provinces, of course,
can do nothing. The Bulletin invites every-
body to scan the list of enemies of Federation—
including Holy Joe Cook and Opposition-leader
Lyne —published in the daily papers of Oct. 27 ;
and, when found, to make a note of. Wriggler
Reid and Sunday Brunker voted against the
raising of the minimum and against the breach
of faith ; and carefully refrained from using the
Govt, influence against the change, so that, so
far as they are concerned, the Federal cause may
only be damned by default. The Bulletin
wonders precisely how much difference there is
between the man who sticks a knife in your
gizzard and the man who finds you lying stuck
and lets you bleed to death ?
“Hobson’s Choice.”
The hesitating and half-hearted sympathy
extended by the N.S.W. public to the Lucknow
strikers is undoubtedly due largely to the per-
sistently sinister twist conferred by the “ Res-
pectable ” Sydney press upon the motives and
actions of the Labor end of every industrial
difficulty. Direct misrepresentation— as being
capable of specific disproof—is seldom resorted
to, but full weight is given to the consideration
that “ a lie which is half a truth is the hardest
of lies to fight,” and it has become a journalistic
fine art —by a little touching-up here and a
little toning-clown there—to put upon such a
case as the Lucknow affair an entirely false
and misleading complexion, without ever
leaving it in the power of the other side
to sav, straight out, “That’s a lie!”
In this way has been created a more or less
nebulous impression that the Lucknow strikers
were lusty and masterful ruffians, who, upon
light labors and liberal pay, had waxed fat
unto kicking-point—saturnine savages who
regarded not the voice of the charmer, and
sullenly refused to foot-it to the gentle piping
of good, easy Look—their guide, philosopher
and friend ! The miner’s calling—besides being
most laborious and none too healthy—is so
dangerous that scarcely a day passes without
the record, for some part of Australia, of a
crushing-to-death or a smothering—a blowing-
up or a sinashing-to pieces amongst men who,
besides receiving little enough for their labor
itseif, receive nothing at all for the risks
attendant on it. And in these days—when it
is beginning to be seen that the demand-
and-supply formula is not, after all, the
last word about the adjustment of industrial
relations—something more than reprehension
is required for the reduction of wages, as in the
Lucknow case, to a figure which meant barely
keeping the soul in the carcase, to say nothing
of the exercise of that “ thrift ” about which
are so eloquent those to whom an adequate
provision for the future does not involve the
surrender of a single luxury of the present.
Only by the elimination from existence of every
circumstance that could differentiate it from
the most sordid and abject slavery could the
family-supporting earner of a mere subsistence
wage manage to divert any of it from
insistent hand to - mouth requirements; so
that, in the great majority of instances,
with illness or disablement the loss of the
husband’s only capital comes hopeless domes-
tic collapse. The alternatives presented to
the bare-subsistence earner, in a word, are but
two—and very simple. Either, for the sake of
a future possibility, deprive the present of all
that makes it worth having—or, to the best of
your ability, better your condition upon that
of the beast of burden, with the certainty for
yourself of the beast’s fate when it can no
longer carry the burden ! Once more—the
Devil and the Deep Sea !
♦
The Respectable Press and the Respect-
able Child-Flog’g’er-
Will John Fairfax and Sons, owners of S.M.
Herald ; Samuel Cook, manager of ditto ; and
Rev. W. Cdrnow, editor of ditto, rise to tell
the Australian public why the Herald, up till
date of writing, Oct. 30, suppressed all mention
of the Narandera (N.S.W.) child-flogging case
on Oct. 21 ? Will J. Carey, chairman of direc-
tors of Sydney Telegraph ; Watkin Wynne,
manager of ditto; and L. C. Brient, editor of
ditto, explain why the Telegraph likewise
suppressed all mention of this shameful case?
These men control the “ leading daily papers ”
of N.S. Wales ; they frequently permit eulogies
of the power and virtues and dignity and
responsibilities of a free press to appear in those
papers ; some of them have children of their
own ; yet when a motherless little girl is cruelly
flogged by her employer in N.S. Wales, they
have the appearance of using their power and
influence not to expose, but to shield, the
offender—is it because he is a bank-manager,
and “ in society,” and a pillar of broadclothed
Respectability? And this, though G. Martin,
Police Magistrate of Narandera, used this lan-
guage (see Wagga Express, Oct. 23) in sen-
tencing Mr. Roderick Thomas to pay ,£25
and costs (he was lucky to escape committal to
the District Court, where Judge Gibson the
other day sentenced a poor devil to three years’
imprisonment for stealing a ninepenny tin of
baking-powder —in the name of Botany Bay
justice !) —
The Police Magistrate said he had no difficulty in
making up his mind about the case, which had been, he
considered, proved'to a degree. It was true, no doubt,
that the little girl was not as good as she should be. She
probably gave a great deal of trouble, but there was a
simple course open to the defendant, who could have re-
turned her to the State or to her father, and thus relieved
himself of all further responsibility. The child herself
had made admissions which showed she was not good and
obedient, but she made the admissions so unreservedly
that it operated as a strong corroboration of her state-
ments in support of the prosecution. It was sufficiently
proved that the child had been excessively beaten with a
whip or strap, and he was satisfied, too, that the incident
of the punishment by means of the cold bath had been
proved by the evidence of the last witness called. To keep
a child, or any person, in a bath for half-an-hour was un-
questionably cruel. A strong, robust constitution would
very much feel a constant shower for even five
minutes. It must not for a moment be thought
that this was not a very serious offence. Both
here and in England the offence of cruelty to
children is regarded as grave and serious, and corrective
legislation is the result. It was only a few years ago
that a well-known case occurred in England, in which a
clergyman and his wife were arraigned on a similar charge
to the present, and, the offence being proved, the clergyman
was sentenced to nine months’ imprisonment and his wife
to three months’: this case, too, called for stern treatment.
There were people in such positions that imprisonment
would mean absolute ruin, and this case he thought would
be met sufficiently by a fine. He intended to inflict a severe
fine, though not the full penalty. lie considered the case
called for a fine of £25, which he ordered defendant to
pay together with all costs. In view of the fact that it
was necessary to obtain special, legal assistance through all
the local solicitors being retained, lie would fix the profes-
sional fee at £8 Bs.
The local feeling excited by the case (says the
Wagga Express) was “ without precedent.”
And what do “ the great metropolitan news-
papers ” do when they meet a case like this ?
They burke it —burke it /—in the name of Out-
raged Respectability and of “ the People’s
Palladium,” “ the Liberty of the Press ” !
A Suggestive Will-Case. *
In annulling the last will and testament of one
George Woods, a Northcote (Vic.) hotelkeeper,
Justice Hood seems to have committed a sad
professional error. With the best of intentions
to deter laymen from trespassing upon a law-
yer’s preserve, he described the ordinary printed
will-form as “ a certain trap to the unwary,”
a remark calculated to strengthen the ancient
belief that legal assistance is absolutely essen-
tial to the making of a valid will. But Justice
Hood didn’t pause at this point and then
branch off into judicial incomprehensibility.
He proceeded to discuss the trap for the un-
wary, and, in the course of proving how the
late Georoe Woods, when he acted as his own
lawyer, had a fool for his client, the learned
Judge made it clear to the meanest understand-
ing that the average will-maker is a fool to
spend a few guineas in employing a lawyer on
the job.
The form (said his Honor) is so printed as almost inevi-
tably to mislead. In the fold of that form are two blank
pages, and the penny-wise-and-pound-foolish persons
who indulge in the dangerous occupation of using
those forms would naturally conclude that those
pages are to be written upon. There is nothing
to warn anyone that if those pages are filled up then
the testimonium clause and signature on the first
page will be out of place. In the present case, the testa-
tor could not dispose of his property as he wished on the
first, page, accordingly he commenced on that page by
merely stating that this was his last will and testament,
&c. Having thus utilised the first page, he had then
turned over and continued on the inside pages to state
his wishes. He had then gone hack to the first page and
signed his name in the place there appointed for that
purpose.
Consequently, Justice Hood upset the will in
accordance witli the wording of the Act which
requires every will to be signed “ at the foot or
end thereof.” So much for the late George
Woods’ rather stupid mistake. His will went
for nothing in the Probate Court, for the simple
reason that he couldn’t cut it short and confine
it to the one page. His case should be a warn-
ing to all will-makers who cannot dispose of
their property in a few words ; also, it should
serve to advertise the usefulness of these
printed will-forms, and lessen the demand
for legal services. Furthermore, the George
Woods case should set the Victorian public
wondering why the Vic. Government, bristling
with lawyers as it is, should permit a “trap for
the unwary ” to be sold at law-stationers’ shops
when there is no earthly reason why the issue
of printed will-forms cannot be controlled by
the Crown Daw department. Considering that
Governments permit the sale of printed forms
for the purpose of legal will-making, the possi-
bility of anyone falling into the “ trap” which
George Woods fell into ought not to exist.
Justice Hood drew a wrong moral from the
Woods will-case. He spoke as a lawyer
desirous of enlarging the sphere of legal prac-
tice, and increasing the annual crop of fees ;
whereas it was his duty, as an officer of the
State, to suggest a simple improvement in the
Wills Act which he was then administering.
Testators should at least be safeguarded, by
Premier lawyer Turner’s Government, from
forms constructed to trap the unwary.
A Hard How to Hoe.
It is a tough job for a workman to get compen-
sation at law under any Australian Employers
Liability Act. There is a pitfall at pretty well
every step ; but about the commonest of all is
the one in which a Lithgow (N.S. W.) workman
named Hannan was sent floundering the other
day. Hannan had sustained injuries ; but, as
the co.—a N.S.W. mining concern—well knew
of the accident, he did not give formal notice
within the statutory six weeks. Before proceed-
ing further, it was therefore necessary to make
an application in Chambers to dispense with
the notice. Argument concluded, brothers
Owen and Cohen snorted that such ignorance
was no excuse—and refused the application with
costs. Now this very likely is good law; but it is
still more likely bad sense. No doubt notice is
necessary in many cases, perhaps most; but, if
it can be shown that the employer has not suf-
fered in the preparation of his defence by the
absence of formal notice, it is outrageous that
the right of action should be entirely taken
from the injured workman, no matter what his
excuse may be. And, even if the employer has
been prejudiced in the preparation of his
defence by the absence of notice, why should
not the injured workman be permitted to go on
making the best of his case, with the responsi-
bility cast upon him of proving everything
right up to the hilt, and provided that
the action is brought within, say, 12
months ? Certainly, the difficulty would
not arise if the injured workman performed the
very simple act of giving notice. But if a man
knew the law just as well as Mr. Justice Owen
does he wouldn’t be shifting mullock or cutting
scrub at ss. a day ; wherefore some considera-
tion might appropriately be shown to him.
Anyhow, he needn’t be made the victim of
technicalities ; and surely nowhere else is ignor-
ance more severely punished than it is by this
absolute bar to action. It isn’t always ignor-
ance, either, that stops issue of the notice.
A good many large employers make it
clearly understood that every attempt to com-
pel the payment of compensation will be fought
step by step, while, on the other hand, for
the first few weeks, at any rate, the hope is en-
couraged that an equitable arrangement will be
mutually made. But the period in which
statutory notice must be given having expired,
too often the injured workman finds himself
“ left.” What is badly wanted, then, is that
the Court should dispense with notice when it
can be shown that the employer was so well
aware of the accident as to negotiate for a
settlement, and, further, that in any case an
action may be brought within, say, 12 months,
with all the presumptions against the workman
where no notice of any kind has been given.

Mr. Arthur Griffith, M.L.A. :
Dear Bulletin, —In your article on the MVS harry arbi-
tration scandal you remark that “the fees MVSharry
pays h ! s counsel are his own business.” On the contrary,
it's the country’s business, for, as the Crown admits some
liability and is only arbitrating about the amount, the
award must be against the Railway Commissioners, and
will carry costs. Therefore, the miserable country will
have to bear the whole of the costs of this long-drawn
farce—some £50,000 ! [lf this miserable country does,
this miserable country is more kinds of an ass even
than it looks.-Ed. B.] Is it likely that, under any other
circumstances, the plaintiff bank (there doesn’t appear to
be over-much M'Sharry in it now) would pay away tens
of thousands of pounds to counsel and arbitrator ?' This
is a worse scandal than even the Dean Commission busi-
ness, and the Govt, is doing all it can to keep the matter
quiet, both Ministers Brunker and Gould having refused
to answer questions in the Assembly as to the fees being
paid to counsel, &c. This sort of thing is one of the re-
sults of our beautiful contract system. In N.S.W. the
lawyers frequently get more out of a railway contract
than is paid to the engineers and navvies who do the
work.
The flogging of the ten-yea.r-old girl by her
employer at Narandera (N.S.W.) represents an
abuse which will always be possible in com-
munities where the use of corporal violence on
the young is sanctioned by law. So long as
gaolers flog children, so long as teachers flog
them, with the sanction of authority, so long
(probably longer, but certainly as long) will the
fiend break out from time to time in the private
employer or other temporary custodian of
juvenile delinquents, and child-torture be
scored up to the nation’s shame. The chastise-
ments which parents inflict are tempered by the
sense of continuous parental responsibility and
the action of natural affection ; but employer,
school-teacher, and gaoler are unrestrained in
these respects. And, if school-teacher or gaoler
may flog a child, why may not a reputedly up-
right employer? The perception of the im-
morality of anyone except a parent inflicting
coi poral punishment on a child has influenced
most civilised countries to ban the punishment
wholly. The flogging of juveniles has been
obliterated from the laws of France, Italy, Ger-
many (all through the Confederation), Austria,
Russia, Switzerland and Sweden. The only
civilised countries in which it is recognised by
law at present are England, Scotland, Ireland,
a few parts of the United States, Norway, Den-
mark, and the Australias. Denmark is the only
country where girls up to 12 years are whipped
bylaw. Despised Japan interdicts under heavy
penalties the use of corporal punishment in
gaols, schools, or factories. “ Copy Japan ”
should be a good cry in this matter.
“ Shark,” to The Bulletin
In re the complaint made by “ D.,” in your issue of 10th
Oct., the conditions imposed on “D.’s” wife when she
opened an account in her married name arose out of the
bank’s foolishness. The law had nothing' to do with
them. N.S.W. legislation removed this particular wrong
years ago. Under the present law, a woman has just as
much right to open an account and draw cheques in her
own name as her husband has. The bank possibly takes
the precaution of getting the husband’s consent, to avoid
the risk of being mixed up in any quarrel between him
and his wife as to the ownership of the money she is
banking. So far as the law is concerned, a woman now
runs much less risk in marrying than a man does. A man
may have to pay debts contracted by his wife without his
knowledge. If she runs into some expensive personage
on her bicycle, the husband has to stand the racket. If
she publishes a libel, he must pay or go to gaol. A man
who keeps a bull is answerable to anvone the bull may
gore, only if it can be shown that he knew the bull had a
propensity that way. Not so, if he keeps a lion or a bear.
He is supposed to know that such animals are vicious,
and is bound to see that they don’t do any harm. The
law puts the man who takes a wife on the same footing as
one who keeps a wild-beast show. It is no defence to
plead that he didn’t know she was loaded.
“Cruel Wretch” (a dominie) to The Bulle-
tin :
Arguments have been used for and against, chiefly
against, corporal punishment in Public Schools. Most
people think it is quite easy to manage a few hundred of
children, of assorted sizes, temperaments, and home-
training, by kindness, &c., and that punishment is un-
necessary. Yet where is the family of only two or three
in which the parents do not at times resort to the slipper
or the strap? Most people are agreed that young children
at least do not need the cane, yet I have seen youngsters
of from six to eight years, generally members of our over-
indulged families, or “ mother s own and only darling
boy,” defy a whole school, howl, kick, bite and absolutely
refuse to obey any order, even threaten the teachei-A
“ I’ll tell me father on you, and ’£‘ll put you in gaol.
’E’ll give it yer.” A few sharp cuts with the cane are
absolutely necessary in such cases if the teacher is to
remain master of his school. Of course, he should keep
his temper, and confine his blows to the hands, or that
part of the anatomy which was the favorite in the old
days of “ the birch.” All teachers who box ears, or in
any way injure a child mentally or physically, should be
severely punished themselves, but a few minutes’ pain
caused by a tap on the hand is really more salutary and
harmless than many other punishments which invariably
take the cane’s place when it is perforce shelved. Cases
of childish insubordination are rare—about one in a hun-
dred. The child seldom attempts it twice, though
his brothers are likely “to show off” when they
in their turn begin school life. It is a result of home-
training, or, rather, the want of it, though the teacher is
blamed for the whole business. Let those who wish to
abolish the cane beware lest they introduce worse evils—
the detention of children in school when they should be
at play, the imposition of long, wearisome tasks which
exhaust the brain, standing for hours, kneeling in corners
to the injury of kneecaps, holding slates above the head
till the circulation in the arms is impeded, and, not least,
the merciless ridicule and sarcasm, of the teacher. I have
seen girls in mental agony under a teacher s sarcasm
and it was a lasting pain, for days after they would burst
into tears at the least reference to the subject. Moral
suasion is possible with a teacher for each scholar and
the same teacher all the time. It is impossible, without
penalties for wrong-doing, when a teacher has many
pupils and has them for a few' months only.

Mr. C. J. Heydon’s 13 consolidating measures,
passed through N.S.W. Council last week,
without criticism or even formal Parliamentary
reading, may turn out to be an unsatisfactory
second stage in a badly-begun business. It
may be remembered that a previous Administra-
tion organised a gigantic commission to consoli-
date the scattered statutes of N.S. W. A sum
of £6BOO was voted for a start, but no one on
or off the commission could approximately indi-
cate where the expense would end. The com-
mission split itself up into seven commit-
tees, with a Judge at the head of each
committee, and the Chief - Justice at the
head of all. The scheme looked well on
paper, and was influential and select enough
to have held its head up for several years,
whether it worked or not, but £4295 slipped
away and the prospect of having to ask
another credit stared the purseholders in the
face. Then it was seen that very little con-
solidation had been achieved, '[’he present
Premier, glad of an opportunity to squelch an
idea of his predecessor’s, flouted the scheme,
and in poured the resignations of the
Chief Justice and other Judges, and
the pretentious Commission felo de se’d.
Had the Commission started with a credit
of £50,000 it would have got so used to
doing nothing before a fresh vote was
required that it would have felt itself justi-
fied in demanding another £50,000. That was
the first stage in the history of N.S.W. Statute
Law consolidation—-and the country escaped
with marvellous luck. Mr. Heydon.’s lone hand;
unfee’d arrangement is the second stage. But if
the Assembly acts as the Council has acted
(and it is assumed it will) where is the effective
guarantee that the consolidation will not break
down in important respects? The Attorney-
General, Dr. Cullen and others, take the bills
solely on Mr. Heydon’s word, and the balance
of the Council does the same. If this one-man
idea, minus criticism or reading, suffice for the
requirements, what a grotesque farce the Com-
mission idea was !

Further consideration of “ Two Little
Vagabonds,” at Her Majesty’s, confirms The
Bulletin’s impression that, despite abundance of
'boiled flap-doodle, the play is a dangerously top-
heavy production liable at any moment to come
■down with a crash. Only good staging and better
have prevented the crash, so far. The
wicked captain, who isn’t so very wicked after all,
continues to be a highly domesticated and uncon-
vincing warrior ; the husky crib-cracker, for whom
the drawing-room window is so obligingly left
open, doesn’t get any less suggestive of an emi-
nently respectable butler undeservedly on his
uppers ; and the cough-with-the-boy-to-it is still a
deplorable combination that cries out as violently
as ever for an indication of its origin. Also,
the alcoholophilic lady with the brogue is still
at intervals entirely oblivious of that circumstance;
and the policeman who more successfully remem-
bers that he is Oirish requires all the more
explanation on that account as a “ village-con-
stable ”in Kent. But Titheradge props up one
side of the drama as the violently-jealous husband,
with as little rant to the square inch as the gen-
tlemen who ornament the gallery could reasonably
be expected to put up with ; and the other side
rests principally upon the plump and capable
shoulders of Miss Einilie Hughes, who skilfully
avoids overdoing a part that was obviously bom
to be overdone. Mrs. Maesmore Morris, also, as
the fair cause of all the trouble, continues satis-
factorily to confute the unholy assertion that
though she may be a dream of loveliness, she can’t
act for sour apples ; and, as the remainder of the
mumming is fair to middling, the boy-burdened
cough will probably continue for some time to
expire beautifully to slow music and the promul-
gation of the most unexceptionable sentiments.
Lily Titheradge, as conscientious as the man who
blacked himself all over when he played Othello,
works really hard in sliding down the pole, and
barks her wrists and bruises her ankles realistically
every evening during the week and twice on
Saturdays.
** * *
“ Fallen Among Thieves ” continues to
draw moderate houses at Sydney Lyceum. The
series of cine.-pictures thrown in without extra
charge by a generous management are described
as jubileee reproductions ; but the audience isn’t
bored as much as might be expected, as only two
views of the event are given. “ The Prairie King”
is announced for Saturday ; and the imagination
riots among pictures of Mayne Reidish heroes in
Mexican saddles rescuing Maud Williamsons
from the clutch of infuriated Indians. P.S. —The
management particularly desires it to be known
that “ The Prairie King ” will be accompanied by
a Tank of the finest water.
*# * *
Two of Rickards’s companies have had “ a
pretty wedding,” and settled down to married life
at the Tivoli, where the Biograph is now mixed
with the Minstrels, and the man who goes to see
Fanny Wentworth takes Arthur Sherwin en
passant, as it were. The combination is a strong
one, and the seduction of it has put a new edge on
the blunted enthusiasm of blase variety-goers, who
receive with approval the suggestion that Rickards
should be first president of the Commonwealth,
with authority to engage 100,000 new variety
artists straight away, and charge them to roads-
and-bridges account.
** * *
“The Gay Parisienne,” which ran for
several weeks in Sydney, started a week of useful-
ness at Melbourne Princess’s last Saturday. Any-
thing that fills a want is useful, and the public
appears always to want an entertainment of “ The
Gay Parisienne ” variety when it is produced in
the Firm’s best manner. This is a two-act enter-
tainment, the characters mentioned on the pro-
gramme being joined together by an elastic thread
of libretto from the pen of an aptly-named
author George Dance. The greater part
of the incidental music was composed by Ivan
Caryll, but the joint efforts of Dance and
Caryll don’t strike one as having given
rise to “ The Cay Parisienne’s ” popularity.
Whereas a Gilbert-Sullivan opera success owes
everything to the librettist and composer, the
success of the musical farce aforesaid springs from
numerous causes, mostly unconnected with the
hook or the score. A respectable amateur per-
formance of a clever opera makes a passable enter-
tainment. Any amateur dealings with “The Gay
Parisienne ” would be insufferable, consequently
one is brought to the conclusion that success in
the present case, like many a bouquet thrown
across the footlights, is really provided by the
management. The show must be smart at all
points, else is there no health in it whatsoever.
Here endeth the first lesson.
» • * *
Two principal performers dominate the
wild proceedings, and share three-fourths of the
applause. Miss Willoughby and Ernest Shand
were the principals at Sydney. Their parts are
now taken by Miss Ada Reeve and Bert Gilbert,
who lately came over from London, and will he
considered fri vollers of the A 1 brand. Miss Reeve
is a bright, brainy little comedienne, with a
French method and a fresh, sweet voice, whilst
her companion in crime (for they are understood
to be a pair of eccentric blackmailers pursuing an
insane Ebenezer) is a nimble “ droll,” also a brisk
burlesque actor and singer. The performance of
both these newcomers ranges from good to excel-
lent. John Coleman, in the Ebenezer part, Miss
Alice Leamar, as a singing servant and vastly-im-
proved step - daficer, together with John Burke
and a gorgeous array of artists whose assistance is
hot required, supply about one-fourth of the
musical comedy. Scenery, dresses, and a ballet
quartette party —also the orchestra under Leon
Caron, and the limelight man, and a final display
of paper-rockets—deserve honorable mention.

Harry Rickards has engaged himself to do
four turns at Melb. Opera House during Cup
week, and the reappearance of that renowned
veteran is once more a matter for joy. Wootwell
and the Avolos are back again after a brief in-
terval, whilst a dozen other people, who haven’t
gone away lately, remain there the same as usual,
supported by cinematograph pictures of the Derby
and Cup.
** * *
“Mrs. Quinn’s Twins,” at Melb. Royal,
is a more tangible affair than either of the two
previous outbreaks, which were only billed for a
week. Moreover, the new bill runs parallel with
the opening of a St. Patrick’s cathedral, and seems
well calculated to catch the people as they come
away from divine service, annyway, bedad ! The
groundwork of “Mrs. Quinn’s Twins ” is a harm-
less Irish drama, dated back to the time when
heroes invariably fell into the clutches of a
press-gang, and always came back just in
time to save their best girls from marrying
somebody else. There doesn't seem to be
any traysonable conspiracy in this drama.
The hump - backed informer merely informs
against a young man who meets the colleen under
an assumed name, and after a considerable amount
of misunderstanding the young man receives his
colleen from the hands of a noble squire, where-
upon the other squire collapses and the base in-
former is devoured by a virtuous peasantry. The
Twins are in no way responsible for what happens
elsewhere. They make an appearance whenever
the drama needs a rest, and they dabble around in
muddy places until Maggie Moore chases them
back to her tender care and spanks them with her
maternal boot. Sometimes she pauses to sing an
Irish song or two, and on these occasions Harry
Roberts is apt to bask in the genial sunshine of
Mrs. Quinn’s smile. Roberts scores heavily as
the hero, Miss Wood hill is a satisfactory colleen,
and the company at large calls for no particularly
adverse remark.
** * *
At the Alexandra an undaunted manage-
ment has set up “ Cinders ” for about as much
as that lurid melodrama is worth. “ Cinders ”
gives the gallery twenty seven reasonable excuses
for howling at Frank Norton, and the same
number of incentives to drop a tear for Miss
Rose Conroy, who gets ill-treated with complete
success. “Cinders” ought to keep the Alexandra
warm for a second week.

A certain bright little Australian actress traces her
physical collapse to a very simple occurrence. When ex-
hausted one day, after some very hard work, some well-
meaning person gave her a glass of a French medicated
wine, Vin . It braced her up so that she continued to
use it, increasing the dose. The medication was
cocaine, and now—may the Lord have mercy on that poor
little actress 1

At an East London hall, reoently, in a tug between a
4-ton elephant and 120 men, the beast simply walked
away with the lot, and did so every time the number was
increased bv ten, till, when 170 were on the rope, some-
body made the end fast round one of the posts that sup-
ported the roof. Then the elephant took a pull in
earnest, snapped the post like a carrot, and brought down
half the roof.

Apropos of “ A Royal Divorce,” what has become of the
Napoleonic tragedy which “Demos” Palmer wrote and
