“ What becomes of local plays constantly contracted
for and apparently supplied to promising minor actors?”
inquires a recent Bulletin correspondent. The Use of
the adverb is questionable, since apparently few plays are
locally manufactured, and still fewer “sold” (some are
given away on the chance of a u slant ” production
“somewhere, someday.”) HoWeVeh possibly the in-
quirer Is one who has not “Constantly contracted,” and
as one who has (sans adverb), perhaps I can tell him what
becomes Of those “ local plays.” Most of them go into
the purchaser’s trunk, there to lie till said purchaser,
having made all the money he can in Australia, accepts
engagements elsewhere, and goes to England or
America to produce them. The Australian actor who
buys plays here to form a possible repertoire (as
some, fortunately for the despised resident playwrights,
are doing) does not buy them for local production, but
for use after he has left Australia. Why? Because
as Boucicault, Holt and Williamson have said many times,
“ There is no money In the production of a locally-authored
piece.” Pantomimes, Wherein the “ Book ” (sUch as it is)
tails in after the scene-painter, mechanist, ballet-mistress,
and electrician, need hot be pointed to as triumphant
exceptions, tn no liiie more than play-writing is the
abhorred phrase that the prophet hath honour so true
in Australia. To catch the Australian public a piece
(good or bad) must have been “The reigning success in
London,” or Timbukhtu, or anywhere, so long as distance
lends enchantment. Managers “know their book,” and
that is why one of them, having approved a local play-
maker’s work, offered to produce it on condition that the
author’s name was omitted and the piece hilled Us “ The
Latest London Success.” He knew his audiehces 1 This
slightly explains why the plays that are sold go to
“ promising minor actors,” Whose pluck and confidence
(patriotism ?) make them more speculative than the average
promising (Very nliich promising) major manager. And,
yes, the birth of the local play usually does “ begin and
elid with the newspaper announcement ’’—and its con-
signment to the aforesaid trunk. Why should the buyer
and author hanker after, say, royalties for about 15 weeks
(a good “ run” for four Australian towns), when a success-
ful English or American production may mean as many
months? I, for one, consider selling for the “foreign
market” preferable to writing in shadowy expectation of
a local one. These remarks are probably “uninteresting,”
“dull,” and “ not worth print.’’ The truth usually isn’t
worth print. And the pessimism which teaches us to
scoff at anything which cometh out of Nazareth, except
prizefighters and scullers (not skull-workers, hut the other
kind), is far more popular—and readable.—B.E.

News has just reached Samarai (writes a

Simpson, leader of the party which found payable
gold early in 1896 at M‘Laehlan’s Creek, Mam-

Dear Bulletin,— Squat, ignorant, un-
speakable Tom Bent was not knocked out by
Baronet O’Loghlen, but O’Loghlen was knocked
in by Bent. The opinion of the knowing ones is
that Bent went up for Port Fairy to split the
Prodistan vote, in order that O’Loghlen might
get the full benefit of the Holy Roman vote. So,
you see, there are still some political uses to which
Bent can be put.

The death of N.S.W.’s ek-Goveriior, the
Well-beloved Hercules Robinson, is described per
Cable as “ sudden ”; it was nb doubt due to a
seizure, the outcome of one of the chronic diseases
from which he had so long suffered. For many
months past he had been laid aside by dropsy,
caused by heart-trouble. Though many years
have passed since Sydney last saw him, Sir Her-
cules’ memory is still fragrant ih Australia. As a
blend of head, heart, and sport, he was a ttlah
among a million.

You will deservedly achieve wide reputation. It is very

One incident in the Age plan to “squash”
Randolph Bedford’s candidature for the Meib.
Eastern Suburbs seat was contemptibly humorous.
An Age reporter buttonholed the members of
Bedford’s committee, and told them—in the man-
ner of one who has damaging information to im-
part —that their man, although an ex-member of
the Age staff, had never been admitted to the
lie ‘porters’ Association ! The Reporters’ Associa-
tion, long since dead and forgotten as far as Melb.
journalism is concerned, was a mere conspiracy of
dullards (who, by the way, never dared to beard
their “proprietors”) to deny membership‘to the
brighter class of press-workers. And as a eoh-
spir '.oy it proved a dismal failure.
Sydney Telegraph Emphasises its suppres-
sion of the Respectable child-flogging case at
Narandera. by wailing in a long leading article
over the cruelties inflicted on neglected children—
in London ! Also, on Nov. 2, it reported that at
Berry police-court, on the previous day, a farmer
named W. Kennedy was fined £2 and costs for
thrashing a boy 15 years of age—a case on all-
fours with the Narandera one, with the difference
that the Narandera victim was a motherless girl
10 years of age, and was apparently much more
brutally treated. But the Telegraph reports
the Berry case, in which the assailant was “only
a farmer,” and suppresses the worse Narandera
one, in which the flogger was a Respectable Bank-
Manager !
Sunday Brunker, the man with the under-
taker’s soul, fell in badly the other night. Crick
arose, in N.S.W. Assembly, with a wooden ex-
pression in his countenance, and, in a tone like a
sermon, called Brunker the “Minister for War,”
and asked him what steps were being taken to aid
Hingland in its hour of trouble and to save the
hempire. Any other man would simply have said
“ Rats ! ” or advised Crick to take something for
it, or asked if he knew who struck Buckley. But
Sunday Brunker took this “ borak ” quite seri-
ously, and laboriously explained the situation,
until the broad grin of the House began to disturb
him. Then something seemed to strike the poor
old man, and he dragged his mouth round towards
his ears and professed to see the joke.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Commission of Enquiry into the working
of the W.A. mining laws has just concluded its sitting at
Kalgoorlie. A deal of heterogeneous and contradictory
evidence was given, but, on the whole, the witnesses
were practical and intelligent. Probably the best evi-
dence was given by manager Callahan, of the Lake View,
a highly-experienced American mine-manager, well-
known and respected in New South Wales, who sug-
gested some radical innovations. He rather startled
some members of the Commission by asserting that
the Westralian plan of neglecting everything else
for the purpose of catering for British capital was
not the “highest good;” that capital would come in
fast enough if the Govt, would simply make good laws,
encourage prospectors, and reduce vexatious charges ; in
fact, he rather pooh-poohed “ British capital ” as more a
nuisance than otherwise, till the Commission fairly
gasped. Again, Mr. Jodrell, a local solicitor, opened the
Commission’s eyes. He quite knocked over the placid
theory that because there are not many officials the public
must be satisfied with the administration of the mining
laws. The appeal courts are held in Perth, nearly 400
miles away ; and he explained that the public failed to be
enthusiastic over spending its money to “ educate the
administrators of the laws through appeals to the Supreme
Court.” He called attention to many clauses of the Act
being inoperative since they were to be administered only
“in accordance with the regulations,” but the
special regulation required had been forgotten. He
cited instances where two applications to mine
under a town site, though unopposed by the sur-
face-owners, and specially allowable by the Act,
had been refused because the Minister “ did not
see his way to grant them,” whilst a like application,
strongly opposed by the surface-owners, but in which a
Mr Wittenoom, brother of the famous Minister of Mines,
“ was present at and assisting in the pegging,” was incon-
tinently granted 111 As a sequel to the above a Gazette
notice lately appeared notifying that the two first-named
leases would be cancelled if rents not paid before end of
this month—leases that had never been granted would be
cancelled ! Mr. Jodrell further called attention to the
clause allowing alluvial mining on leased ground “ within
60ft. of the line of reef,” which, if it means anything at
all, means that a miner may work alluvial from 49ft. odd
to lin. of the reef; but this is taken to imply that the
alluvial man mustn’t go within 50ft. of the reef—just the
opposite to what it says. He pointed to heaps of other
such blunders ; and, further, showed how many clauses of
the Act are not allowed to be utilised—referring to resi-
dence areas and other matters—simply because a chuckle-
headed Minister refuses to let them be acted on.
Altogether Mr. Jodrell made it clear that of all laws the
W.A. mining law is the biggest “ass” in existence, and
the administration, to use an Irishism, a still bigger one.
* * * *
Pritchard Morgan and his partner, Mr. W. G.
Hall, seem from all accounts to have made a couple of
excellent deals in Westralia. They took up not a few
mines, and are working two of them —the others having
proved good enough to quit. The North Star, at Mt.
Malcolm, N. Coolgardie, is opened out at No. 1 level for
600 ft. The reef at this level (says a well-informed Bulle-
tin corres) O ident) was fair grade, and from end to end
averaged about 3ft., and milled 22dwts. At No. 2 level,
250 ft. has been opened on the line of reef and stoping has
begun, the ore being higher grade than above ; present
crushings go 27dwts. In No. 2 drive the reef has widened
to 9ft., goes down between well-defined walls and promises
Eermanence. The mill is 10 head, and the ore is being
andled economically, as the plant is automatic, and the
presence of plenty fresh water in the mine does away
with the usual hand ing of tailings (only worth a few
dwts.). The mill engine is strong enough to drive another
10-head—now being added. Returns began in April, since
when the mine has produced 25270 z. gold from 2314 tons
ore. There are some honest fair grade shows in the Mt.
Malcolm district, and plenty of fresh water at from 60 to
100 feet in both hard and soft country. Feed is also fairly
good, and large quantities of vegetables are being grown
there. The North Star has a good big corn paddock
below' the tailings-dam, which paddock is irrigated by the
waste water from the tailings, and promises to produce a
heavy' crop.
The Sons of Gwalia at Mt. Leonora, 10 miles
from Mt. Malcolm, is owned by Messrs. Morgan and Hall,
and is (continues The Bulletin’s informant) “practically
a gold-field in itself.” The property takes in over a mile
and a-quarter of reefs and formations, seven of which run
nearly parallel and are proved payable by milling returns.
They vary in value from Bdwt. to soz. and 6oz. The low-
grade reefs are from Bft. to 12ft. wide, and those of high-
grade vary from a foot to 4ft. and sft. There is an ample
suppl.y of water, and a 10-head mill is putting through
between 600 and 800 tons per month—a record for W.A.,
as the mine was only pegged a little over a year ago, and
is 200 miles from a railway' station. It has already pro-
duced 4271J0z. from 2856 tons ore. Another 40-head of
stamps are being added to the battery, and these will go
through as soon as the railway is opened to Menzies. A
large cyanide plant will also be put down, as the tailings
average loz. The mine is being vigorously developed in
readiness for the new battery, and when this starts the
Leonora will be amongst W.A.’s leading gold-producers.
It is one of the few regular gold-producing mines which
has not been floated, and the cost of the mining and mill-
ing (with whips and green-hide buckets and only 10-head)
is being covered at 325. lOd. per ton. Cages have just
been put into the main shaft, and steam-winding is in
progress now. The mine is expected to clear expenses on
6dwt. when the big battery starts.
• * * •
General Gordon Extended —meeting lately held
in Adelaide. Letter read from the original holders of the
lease, protesting against the proposed liquidation. “ The
lode had been struck at the 140 ft. level in the parent
mine, carrying gold assaying up to 70oz. to the ton.”
When, which, where, who ? “ Considering the boundary
was within 20 chains, and that the Gen. Gordon North
was developing well, it seems a pity not to give it a
further trial.” The same old wheeze of the paid-up
holder. The contributors have had their trial, and a
very severe one it has been. Where is all the gold talked
about in the prospectus, by the lies of which so many
people were fooled into taking up these worthless shares ?
The chairman did not know what was the best thing to
do. H. Bickford said his opinion of the property was
that it was not worth two straws. That, too, appears to
be the opinion of every man who has seen it and is capable
of telling the truth. The £4OOO said to remain should at
once be refunded.
• * • *
The almighty Kaufman has left Westralia—it is
believed for good. Now it is more than whispered in
London that the greater-than-Kaufman—Whitaker Wright
—is about to turn his attention to British Columbia “as
a field for investment and development.” It looks like
it. When Macintosh, the Lieut.-Gov. of the N.W. Terri-
tories, was in London lately he was banqueted by
Wright and co., and Lord Dufferin, the new chairman of
the London and Globe Finance, presided. Macintosh
ridiculed the idea of climatic difficulties, said the trade of
Yukon was going to be immense, the fields were of enor-
mous extent and richness. He remarked that he always
travelled with a specimen, and, at this moment, a man,
who had lived 10 years at Yukon, over six feet high,
vigorous and well-bronzed, rose to his feet. Macintosh
remarked—“ There’s an emaciated specimen for you ! ”
If Wright makes a move in that direction, thousands will
follow him. He is a champion mining operator, and
behind him there is another masterfully-silent person
whose name is seldom heard : Herbert Trower, formerly
of Sydney, once a clerk in Dixson’s tobacco-factory, then a
big tobacco and other merchant. After—well, not suc-
ceeding in Sydney, he floated the Wentworth mines for
half-a-million in London in the midst, and in spite of, the
Baring Bank crises. Kaufman, it is said, once asked :
“ Who is this man Trower?” “This is the kind of man
he is,” said Wright, producing Trower’s cheque, marked
good by the bank on which it was drawn for £500,000.
Yes, the L. and G.F. is a mighty corporation, but where —
oh ! where —would it be but for the Lake View —one of
the first half-dozen of the world's gold-producers? With-
out that it would have had to carry the Wealth of Expec-
tations, W.E. Extended, Paddington Consols, Golden
Crown, and others, which, up to date, have produced
little or no gold. Verily, the sweetness of one solid great
mine smothers the stench of cats unto seventy-times
seven.
• * * *
Clarke and Co., in their special W.A. issue: —
“ There are a dozen mines at Kalgoorlie that alone may
reasonably be expected to turn out, on an average, over
4(K>ooz. per month, when fully equipped, or, say, half-a-
million oz. per annum ; and more than a dozen more
that, on present prospects, may very reasonably be ex-
pected to become regular gold - producers; and, as
developments with depth in the past year have added so
greatly to the value of the field, is it not more than
probable that further developments will reveal more
wealth in this mineral belt? That Kalgoorlie will yet
supply the greatest boom ever seen in Australia we have
not the slightest doubt, but the time has not yet arrived.
Not until the mines generally are turning out the great
quantities of gold the developments of the past six months
foreshadow, will we see a boom.” Output figures are also
given, to show that W.A. is going ahead generally, and
that, without Kalgoorlie, it is destined to be a great gold-
producer. It is predicted that the total W.A. output this
year will be 650,0000 z., or 10,000 oz. above Queensland for
last year, and that next year Vic.’s '96 yield—Boo,ooo odd
oz.—will be easily passed.

The alluvial rush at Kanowna is obviously one
of the best of a great many occurring in that country
during the last four years. “It is causing a marked re-
vival in alluvial mining all over the country. There is
likely to be a great scarcity of water this, summer. A
fortnight ago it was 10s. per ioOgals., now it is 255. Some
3000 men are on the field, and as many leaving as are
going.”

» * * *
General Gordon mine, W.A. — Cable appearing
in London papers dated June 15 apparently official :
“ Battery of ten stamps now in working order, success-
fully started 13th June; 31,000 tons ore available for
treatment; will be able to crush 600 tons monthly ; mine
looks well.” Yet that battery only crushed a few tons
for a hideously disappointing return before closing down ;
another manager resigned, all hands were turned adrift,
and suspension Secured. Even all this and a lot more
things do not disturb the discreet silence of the tipster
who described it as “A second Great. Boulder,” and for
long never wearied in advising people to buy.

* * * *
SomeWestralian P.O. officials have been making
out-of-the-way “ perks.” At least one has. Afghans and
other Mahommedans will not accept interest; and when
interest has accrued in respect of savings-bank deposits
on the goldfields, the Asiatic has declined the unclean
stuff - and the official has pocketed it. A Minister) look-
ing eagerly for odd pence, got wind of the practice, and
that form of “perk” is now at an end.
B. H. Proprietary shares continue to pass to the
London register; at time of closing for the Oot. div.,
367,116 were held in Australia as against 592,884 in Eng-
land. Australia’s holders are thus distributed : —2713,
representing 195,616 shares, in Adelaide; 722, with
127,587 shares, in Melb. ; 222, with 34,613 shares, in Syd-
ney ; the rest scattered. On this basis of a shilling div.,
Australia gets £18,355 ; the other side £29,645.
* * * *
Pinnacles Group, stumped for money, still pot-
ters along, hand-picking a few tons of ore. And even
that makeshift has been interrupted by a strike of the
youngsters employed.
European smelters are offering less objection
than they did to the zino in the Barrier sulphide concen-
trates, and, consequently, a less thorough extraction, is
now aimed at in some mills, with the result of an in-
creased output. British concentrates, for example, now
contain 8* per cent, zinc for “ firsts,” and 11 per cent, for
“seconds.” The competition of the Illawarra and the
announcement that the B. H. Proprietary would start
concentrate-buying has, no doubt, had most to do with
the change.

ditions art favorable for a fair run of it.

S.A. shareholders in the Eveleen, the disastrous
Northern Territory silver-lead mine held by a Sydney-
registered co. in liquidation, are beginning to kick. Some
years ago they paid a liquidator’s call of ss. a share ;
now a demand for 7s. more has come along. As it was
half-promised that no further call would be made on
them, the shareholders have agreed to throw in 3d. a share
“ in order to employ a solicitor in Sydney to inquire into
the matter.”
• * * •
N.S.W. Attorney-General Want thinks Crown-
Solicitor Colquhoun wrong in advising that a mine is not
“ unworkable ” because the co. can’t get men at the price
offered; ergo, apparently, J. Want thinks suspension
should be granted. Now (writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent), I’m in a bit of trouble. It’s a case of bigamy, and
I'm committed. I want a lawyer. I’ve offered one half-
a-sov. to see the thing right through on condition he lets
me search him every time I interviewed him to see he
hasn’t “lifted ” anything belonging to me. He objects,
and declines the job. So I want suspension, and am by
this mail writing to the Attorney-General to hold over
the case against me till the next court, or such other time
as this fool-lawyer accepts iny terms. Of course he will.
* * * *
The Lucknow strike came to an end while
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne’s no-confidence motion
was still on the carpet, and the little wind there was in
his sails thereupon left them. The strike was declared off
on manager Lock agreeing to pay the old rate of wages
after Ist May, ’9S, and, meanwhile, to guarantee 7s. 6d.
per eight-hours’ shift to men employed on contract work ;
the new miners'-house-searching rules to be abrogated.
Manager Lock’s statement that the trouble could all have
been arranged three months ago had “ his own men ”
come to him in the matter is publicly regarded as rather
thin ; and an idea prevails that he has “ heard from
London ” on the subject.
• • • •
A Sydney agent who does an extensive business
in arranging partnerships and the sale and transfer of
businesses, told a N.S.W. Income-tax official some days
ago chat, if they put their heads together, they could
drive half the traders of Sydney to gaol for perjury.
“ Just,” whispered the confidential agent, “ show me the
profits they paid Income-tax on, and I’ll show you the
profits they gave me to bait partners or purchasers with.”
Another Income-tax story : A Sydney merchant took his
nephew into the business, and, to make room for the
young man, dispensed with the services of his book-keeper.
The ex-book-keeper was accepted as temporary clerk in
the Taxation Department, and in a fortnight after the
commissioners made demand on the merchant for £l5
extra as tax on income. “ Host non propter,” observed a
suave commissioner when bailed up on the subject ;
“ Propter etpostl” yells the vietjm.

strong interest in Barrass and Conley’s Peak mine.

* * * *
Nowra, N.S.W. —“ Large cyanide works will be
erected at Yalwal as soon as the litigation in connection
with the Homeward Bound mine is settled.” When is it
to be settled? It has been going on now for nearly four
years, and it all arose through a small lease being for-
feited for non-payment of rent-despite the fact that an
agent of the co. was given the money to pay over ! Yet
a co. which paid some £20,000 for the mine, and has spent
over that sum in plant and development, has had to fight
for four years to make its title clear, because an agent
forgot, or otherwise neglected to pay, a few paltry pounds’
rent. The forfeiture clauses are a blot on the mining
laws of Australia. Let the State fine, and fine heavily if
it likes, for non-payment of rent or neglect of labor-
conditions, but, for the sake of the industry, abolish the
monstrous principle of forfeiting and handing over valu-
able estate to the man who plays the part of informer.
And, let it be asked again, why on earth (granting that the
Yalwal mine was justly forfeited by the State) should an
informer be rewarded with a property worth, say, £20,000
at auction ? Why shouldn’t the State put £19,500 into the
Treasury and pay the informer £5OO for his trouble ?

* * * *
A chicken of one boom-curse has come home to
roost with an unlucky Australian Bohemian who was very
“thick” with the local branch-boss of a certain defunct
financial institution, which too many people have good
cause to remember. Said the boss one day to the Bohe-
mian—“ Want any stuff, old man? Yes! Well, if
twenty ’ll do, just sign that.” (P.N. for £5O, at six
months). Cheque written and sent to Cashdown Bank,
and, when clerk returns with the sovs., boss puts £5 aside
for his concern’s interest, “That leaves £22 10s. a-piece
for you and me. Let’s have a little bottle.” A few days
later manager is cursing his luck. “ D —d fool I was not
to make it £5OO ! Might as well had that as a miserable
£5O ! I ought to have guessed what the game was, from
the way they used to worry me, as soon as I had anything
like a decent balance here, to wire it along to head*
quarters.” “ Why, what the devil's up ? My blessed
directors have cleared with the sugar—that’s all!”
Later on, within a few weeks of expiry of statutory limi-
tation-period—a stranger, who has bought the book-debts
of the frizzled institution for something under Is. in the
£, gets judgment against Bohemian for £BO, amount of
p.n. and interest.
* * • •
The propriety of banks dummying shares carry-
ing a liability was again vindicated in Melb. Insolvency
Court last week, when an insolvent clerk in the deceased
City of Melbourne Bank got his release on liabilities,
£98,000 ; assets, £5. The bulk of the liabilities were on
shares in Wallachs Limited and City of Melbourne Bank.
The former concern had been body and soul the bank’s
property for some years prior to the bank’s collapse, and
the farce—if not worse—of a bank advancing money on
either Wallachs’ or its own shares, when the bank must
have known what hot goods to handle those shares were,
is simply a misappropriation of the funds entrusted
to the bank by its solvent shareholders.

One of the Bendigo “ fifty ” (men who replaced
the Lucknow strikers) to a reporter as the train was
about to leave : —“ I’ve been out of work two years. The
miners have no union here, only an accident society. It’s
near time there was a strike here.” And, all things
considered, the Bendigo A.M.A. is a bit out of date- with
its veteran secretary “barracking” for the Freetrade can-
didate capitalist Lazarus; snarling at Labor-candidate
Hamilton; figuring, photographically, as a hero in Hirsch’s
Beacon ; and writing congratulatory notes to Ballarat’s
Mayor, Shoppee, over the Tillett business. Ye gods !
You would think that wages gold-miners were a set of
shopkeeper-deacons.

• • • •
In May last, the estate of C. H. James (described
as a “ sheep-fanner ”) was sequestrated Any ordinary
Victorian insolvent would, on pain of imprisonment for
contempt, have had to file his statement of affairs within
two months of sequestration, but James (proprietor of the
smellful Dominion Bank and arch land-boomer) has failed
to comply with the law up to date of writing. On an
application being made in Melbourne the other dav for
James’s committal for contempt, it leaked out that all the
creditors were satisfied, excepting the man who wanted to
put James away, and his claim was stated to be £l7 only.
The Bulletin yearns to know the true reason why' the
£l7 man should spend about ‘lOO on counsel’s fees for
such a small amount, especially' as the general body of
creditors’ claims, stated to be satisfied, are considerably
over £lOO,OOO.

Dear Bulletin,—Tn a Bulletin par. it is stated
that banking hours are seven a day. Perhaps you would
be surprised to know that the Melb. Savings Bank clerks
not only' work ten hours a day regularly, but have for
weeks past been working all Sunday.—R.A.
• • • •
The Svengali Mining Co. (Tas.) —promoted
“ on the off chance ” —consists largely of men who have
little to lose. Most of the shares having been recently
acquired bv a crowd who w-ere early acquainted with the
developments, a resolution has been passed to make all
the fully-paid £2 shares liable for another £l—this, ap-
parently, w’ith the object of freezing-out, the little men.
To the disgust of the crowd, another wealthy man has
undertaken to pay up all the new £1 liability of the poor
original shareholders. Now, what will the big men do?
• • * #
Labouchere, owner and editor of London Truth,
has done his best to prove that the Jameson raid was
part of a sordid stock-jobbing transaction by Rhodes and
co. to enrich themselves and impoverish the publio.
Henry Hess, editor of the African Critic (a paper no
doubt shored up by the S.A. Chartered Co. gang) is now
giving a very bad turn to “ Labbv,” who, at last dates, has
not defended himself. He has got hold of a lot of private
letters written b.y Labouchere long ago to a now-deceased
fellow-operator in the stock-market (millionaire Beit is
said to have paid £7OOO to deceased’s executors for those
epistles), and has published a number of fac sim'les, and
old extracts from the World tending to show that while
“ Labby ” was financial editor of that paper (in 1875 or
thereabouts) he boomed certain ventures and damned
others for his own requirements; that he thereby
swindled the public ; that he was first a “ bear” and then
a “ bull,” and was so satisfied with the result of his opera-
tions that he remarked in one of these letters : “ It seems
to me that this can be renewed several times.” A num-
ber of English papers are calling upon Lab. to disprove
the charges or resign his seat in the Commons and do
many other disagreeable things. If “Labby” has no
other way out of it he might say : “ When I recall the
things I have done since ’75 why trouble myself about a
little thing like this?”

The mild boom in Mt. Deddick, an alleged
silver-mine in the Vic. Snowy River district, has enabled
the Mt. Deddick United to come out in a feeble way. The
stories told of the wonders of this camp recently induced
a well-known Broken Hill man to send along his expert—
a very old war-horse, indeed. The writer cannot give his
report, but the effect of it was: “No likee.”
% » * m
“ Henry Bratnober, who represents the Ex-
ploration Company in British Columbia, made an
attempt to purchase claim 31 on Eldorado Creek for
£25,000, but the offer was refused.” There is another re-
pot t to the effect that Bratnober had got off the track
somewhere in that country, and sought shelter in a cave,
where he was found curled up alive and well after passing
several days without food. Anyhow, it is quite evident
that the big Bismarck-like Bratnober has got toKlondyke.
He has been in all the most remote mining regions on
earth, and has bought millions of pounds’ worth of mines.
Three years ago the writer heard him swear that he was
full-up of looking for gold-mines, and soliloquise on what
a fool he was to travel round such a country. But that
was while sitting on a barren white quartz-blow the
“ great richness ” of which he had camelled over 100
miles from Coolgardie to see. Anyone who heard Brat-
nober curse things on that “blow” can picture him in the
Klondyke cave.

SOCIETY
At a meeting held to sympathise with the
Newcastle (N.S.W.) church official lately fined for
cuffing a misdemeanant urchin during service, an
unspeakable reverend person (local paper report)
thus orated :
“ The main question was—was young Farrar wrong?
To his (the cleric s) mind it was a wrong thing to make
rabbits in church. He could not conceive of Samuel
making rabbits in the Tabernacle. Who, either, could
conceive of the Saviour when five years old making
rabbits? Or, who could imagine St. Timothy doing it ?
It was a most irreverent thing to do. There was a time
for everything. The church, during divine service, was
certainly not the place for conduct of the kind, and the
churchwarden was right in stopping it. A gentleman in
Islington had told him that he had once made a rabbit in
church, and, when the matter was reported to his father,
he received the greatest hiding that he could remember.”
And yet nobody seems to have howled ! The "un-
godly jester is simply not in it with the uncon-
scious humorist of the monkey-beard and the long
upper-lip.
W.A. Police-Commissioner Phillips’ post-
prandial boast that his men were the most intelli-
gent in Australia, gives extra flavor to the follow-
ing choice effusion lately addressed by a W.A.
“cop.” to his landlady :
Madam, I beg to instigate my abandonment as a
hoarder of your keeping, as the position I now occupy
calls me to achieve restricted policy of familiarity.
Thanking you for your kind patronage. I will lie honor-
ably pleased to comply with your small debt.
Even the much-derided Babu would find it hard
to beat this.

Last Saturday The Bulletin sniffed with
interest the burning grease which Henry Lucy
poured over himself in S. M. Herald, apropos of
the Lueys’ receipt of the Dunmow flitch of baom
for conjugal virtue. (Dr. Brewer, in his “ Phrase
and Fable,” says of the flitch that “ the attempt
to revive this ‘ premium for humbug’ is a mere
‘ get-up ’ for the benefit of the town !”) And
after this paper had considered the egotistical
Lucy by and large, and had read the doggerel
composed in his honor, its eyes drifted to the
column adjoining the sacred shrine of Lucy family
gossip, and it read (), horror ! —this:
PREPARATIONS FOR WHELPING.
Some modification in the dietic regimen is generally
necessary with a bitch in whelp. If she lias been habitu-
ated to on'e meal per day, and that, at night, she should
also have a meal in the morning. However, cave must be
taken not to overfeed. . . . Every expectant dam should
have her kennel made ready after the seventh week, so
that she becomes accustomed to the surroundings. . . .
A few days before the eventful period the kennel
should be thoroughly cleaned and washed with hot water,
thereby destroying all eggs of worms that may he pre-
sent ; and another good plan is to rub into the dam’s coat
precipitated sulphur from head to tail. A final prepara-
tory measure used by most breeders is a dose of castor od or
other cathartic; but perhaps this is only necessary in some
cases The breeder can put away the old frozen
idea t hat castor oil is necessary at this period. It also has
the effect of vitiating the milk more or less.
The conjugal felicity of Henry Lucy along-
side the conjugal felicity of “a bitch in whelp!”
O, Mister James ! Mister James ! what a comical
dog you are !

Lobelia.” Really, the tiles develop into grand
opera. “The Honorable Mrs. Montague has
eight, by the famous Woolooniooloo.’’ Wooloo-
miaw ! “She is sacrificing them at 355. each.”

* * *• *
The late Duchess of Teck was the “ Bonnie
Mary ” of the Guelph family in her younger days,
and people used to cry out against Queen Victoria
for not giving her handsome cousin permission to
marry one of the British dukes, or marquises, who
would have jumped at the chance. Mary had to
waste her sweetness on the desert air until she was
33, and then his Serene Highness Teck took the
bouncing spinster to church. She grew rather
Mr. BERT GILBERT.
Miss ADA REEVE.
(Of the “ Gay Parisienne ” Co.)
Photo., Brown, Barnes & Bell, Liverpool. Photo., Beaufort, Liverpool.
appallingly plump after marriage (Princess May,
her daughter, is proceeding on the same lines), and
it is recorded of the Persian Shah that when he
saw Duchess Teck dancing at a Buckingham
Palace ball, he laughed as no Eastern potentate
had ever been known to laugh since the night
when Belshazzar got drunk.
* » * •
Cockaigne : ‘ ‘ The Tecks were as poor as
rats until Clarence turned his languid eyes that
way. It is a current joke that Lord Cheylesmore
got his title for buying the Teck picture-gallery.
I have seen it in print that the Duke of Teck ran
away to Vienna with Mrs. Rousby, the actress,
twenty years ago. The Duchess of Teck, as Prin-
cess Mary of Cambridge, was particularly attached
to Lord Hood, but the Queen interdicted it. The
saying in London used to be about getting things
on Teck.”

Dear Bulletin, —Passing
through a Melb. suburb the other
day, a leaflet was handed me ad-
vertising a certain medical cure-
all, and containing laudatoiy letters
from a few local residents, among
others. I thought I would inves-
tigate. The first address was found
easily enough, but in place of being
occupied by the alleged testimonial-
writer, it was in the possession of
a well-known undertaker. I stopped
investigating.—F. Croson.

♦ * * *
While the navigator of every colliding
mud-punt in Australia is solemnly hauled before
the local Marine Board to account for his action,
no news lias hitherto been heard of Baron Brassey
being summonsed to explain how he ran the Sun-
beam ashore in Queensland (to say nothing of
other occasion). Gov. Brassey is the holder of
a Master’s Certificate, and the cancellation of
this, if such a blasphemous proceeding could be
even suggested, would probably break the old
man’s heart —despite the fact that he is selling the
Sunbeam and will probably have no more truck
with big fry in the boat line.
* * * *
W. T. Stead, apropos of Gov. Brassey’s
Nineteenth article on the Jubilee in Victoria,
suggests what he calls “an admirable use ” to
which Colonial Governors might be put. “ They
might be made a sort of Imperial newspaper corre-
spondents to send home to the British people vivid
narratives of Colonial functions.” Yes, but what
newspaper would publish such rubbish ? Who
can imagine a “ vivid ” description of anything
coming from the ponderous dullard Brassey, or
the wheezy Nonconformist-Conscience Buxton?
The idea of making Governors “useful” is, of
course, good, in so far as it is a recognition of
their present uselessness.
* * * *
Gov. Buxton has again distinguished him-
self. He recently visited the Jamestown (S. A.)
show, and Mayor Cameron drove him from the
railway-station to the place where he was to stop.
Before alighting, H.E., thinking the charioteer
was a stipendiary Jehu, slipped half-a-erown into
his astonished hand. However, this was better
than the historic penny which a Victorian states-
man is said to have bestowed upon a disgusted
Echuca bootblack.

heartfelt sorrow, and to which they had responded with
their heartfelt sorrow and their sympathy.
Brother Bulletin’s glad that Brother and Sister
Yarrington value the handshakes and the tears so
much higher than the cash and the pretty cot-
tages. Now, this Brother has still so much sinful
Adam in its clothes that it would possibly forget
the handshakes and tears while rattling the testi-
monial dollars and thinking how nice a third
pretty cottage would look alongside the other two.
Brother and Sister Y. are evidently not built in
that sordid way. But', in face of this Danae
shower among the vines, what becomes of the
bitter cry of the submerged parson ?

* * * *
The best display of millinery ever seen in
Hobart happened a few days ago, when some 600
Christian Endeavorers met in convention in that
respectably-conducted village. The chief item in
the luggage of every one of the noble 600 appeared
to be a bulky bonnet-box. The good young men
of Hobart had a surfeit of prayer and petticoats,
and now wear a “ don’t you feel well ? ” look which
excites profoundest pity.

A few years ago,
(M.L.) was washed
almost as disastrous
lately subsided. It
debris behind it —the
hopes of a young
among the rest. The
Dutchman was engaged
to a comely young
Hawkes Bay
out by a flood
as the one that
left a lot of
matrimonial
Dutch ni an
widow. She was
aged 25 ind
weighed a great
many pounds,
and possessed a
farm. One night
the deluge came
down unexpect-
edly, and drown-
ed out her place
along with many
others. Hans Van-
derschmidt was out
at daybreak among the _
rescuers, and by heroic
efforts he steered a crazy
boat through theflood to
the place where the lady’s
house-roof just appeared
above the waters with
the lady herself clinging
to it in her night-dress ;
she had been taken so
completely by surprise
in the night that she had
swarmed out of a win-
dow without wasting time
over her toilet. There was
a long, long pull back
again to the nearest dry
land, and forty yards from
shore an oar broke, JHans
promptly seized his intended
vrow in his arms and bun-
dled overboard, and started
to wade ashore amid wild
cheers from the adjacent
crowd. But he was tired and
stiff, and the mud was trea-
cherous, and the lady was
heavy and would slip down
into the water. Every time
she slipped Hans gave a tre-
mendous heave and lifted her
to his shoulder again, and
every time he heaved the
lady’s scanty attire went up
six inches or a foot, or thereabouts.
She screamed and struggled and even
bit ; the crowd on the bank yelled hila-
riously ; but Hans kept his eye steadily on the
water in search of snags, and gave the lady
a fresh heave at intervals of a few seconds.
Finally he struggled ashore, and stolidly de-
posited on the bank a fiancee whose one gar-
ment was in a string round her neck. Then
the hero’s eye fell upon her ; he realised the
enormity of his offence, and, uttering a wild
shriek in Dutch, he fled over the nearest hill
and never came back. When the crowd had
done laughing, the rescued lady was taken in and
hospitably dried, and, later on, when she had got
over her anger, she advertised extensively, asking
her lost Dutchman to return and all would be for-
given. But apparently he had fled back to
Amsterdam, for the advertisements remain unan-
swered unto this day.

Over 1000 N.S.W. Seventh Day Adventists
have gone under canvas for the society’s current
tent-dwelling function, near Sydney, and its col-
lege at Coonabong (N.S.W.) possesses 87 pupils.
This curious faith appears to be spreading by
mere vis inertice —by which chunk of Latin
The Bulletin understands that when the dull
and heavy Faith falls on a man it knocks him
flat, and as he falls he knocks another flat, and so
on—-like heavenly card-houses.

* * * *
A haughty militaire, arriving in a hurry at
the wharf to welcome returning Jubilee N.S.W.
braves, inquired of a low-grade warrior whether
the men had landed yet, and was astounded to be
told that “they were not all drowned yet.”
Thinking the man was actually daring to take a
rise out of him , the great man thundered exceed-
ingly, and, upon the soldier repeating his state-
ment, reported him for insubordination. But it
was only a matter of a missing “h.” What the
man had really said was, “They’re not ’auled
round yet.”
* * * 9
Premier Turner, a few weeks back, sent
out a circular to Mayors and Presidents of shires
ARTIST AND CRITIC.—From an Early
Australian Point of View.
(It’s all right as long as the gentleman with the
club is NOT a Public Gallery trustee.)
throughout Victoria, calling upon them to supply
him with photos, of the most interesting persons,
places, or things in their respective districts, it
being his intention to paralyse the British investor
and the Royal Family with a picture-gallery at
the Agent-General's new office, representing the
wealth, beauty, enterprise, and intelligence of the
country. Turner loved his idea, and expected
great things of it, but it has occasioned the great
sorrow of his life. The Mayors and the shire
presidents have ’gleefully responded with about
two van-loads of photographs of fat presidents,
and purple Mayors, and woolly shire councillors,
and gorged aldermen, also with likenesses of the
large, substantial, over-dressed wives of these
people, and their elderly, ugly daughters, and
their fearful twins. Besides these there are hun-
dreds of views of the grotesque mansions of presi-
dents, and Mayors, and councillors, and their
mean butcher’s-shops, and pubs., and groceries,
and pictures of the Bullybinga pound, and the
Dingo Creek saw-mill, and of prize hogs, and fat
cows, and hunks of butter, and piles of cheese,
and entires, and hens, and other stock. The land-
scape department is filled with unnumbered repre-
sentations of the same old hanging rock, and the
same old cave, and bits of the inevitable “ crick,”
and the eternal hill and mount, likewise views of
the stagnant lagoon, and the everlasting dam ;
and Turner, sitting under the avalanche his folly
has evoked, feels his intellect totter, and expects
to be smothered beneath the dreadful accumula-
tion of pictorial hogs, dogs, horses, bulls, rams,
jams, butter, and aldermen.

wife : “ Whose profession ; yours or hers?” —

The Bulletin’s London correspondent :
“For years advts. of a similar tenor to the follow-
ing have been appearing in the London dailies,
and they must annually cost the warped-minded
female who inserts them many hundreds of pounds.
She never replies to letters, except in print,
hough many * coin-sniffers ’ have been on her
ack ”
PERSONAL, &c.
"lUTY DEAR MEN. —Am afraid you quite misunder-
stand me. I meant you not to mind about me,
as I seem to be quite unable to fight against the im-
mense opposition, which I suppose comes from my
relatives, the women, and the detecs., and I also seem
quite unable to get personally acquainted with you,
so it seems useless to ask you to wait for me I have
not got a man for myself and for the last seven years
have never had a written love-letter from one of you,
except those printed in agony column of which I can’t
find out the names and addresses of writers. I was
thinking of your happiness and not of my own when
I wrote to ask you if it also proved too much for you
to go back and marry other girls I mean amongst
your own people for with one exception I have not
ever recommended you to marry any girl at all or
woman either. But as to forcing you to do such a
thing 1 never have, for it would neither benefit your-
selves or myself. I mean I consider it a pity for me
to be “in it ” at all as I seem to be such a poor hand
at finding out things, by myself, and unaided, and
my small income does not add to the possibility of
getting personally acquainted with you. For instance
there are many people whom I would be glad to en-
quire about but I am afraid to do it in the public
press in case some other women should hang on to
you, so lamin a fix. L.F.A.B.G. and thus I should
hurt you without benefiting either you or myself.

Dear Bulletin, —In noticing the Naran-
dera child-flogging case, you represent defendant
Thomas as saying he “never stripped” his ten-
year-old nursegirl when he flogged her. May I
point out that in the Wagga Express of Oct. 23
(which gives a much fuller report of the case than
the Narandera Ensign) the amiable bank-flogger
is represented as saying : “ I did not order her to
strip herself until I punished her, neither then or
at any other time. . . I never ordered her to strip
herself until I punished her on any of these
occasions.” . . The double emphasis on the
“ until” is suggestive: to say “I did not order
her to strip herself when I punished her” would
be a very different thing. Bv the way, when you
are saying your prayers to-night, you might try and
put yourself for five minutes in that motherless
child’s place. The flogger has called you down
to the bathroom ; he has made you strip ; he has
seen your shivering body covered with sears, old
and new—scars on the arm, scars on the thighs,
scars on the abdomen, scars on the back so
numerous that, when a doctor attempts to count
them, he “ cannot arrive at a fair estimate ; ” you
have turned on the shower (remember, it is a nice,
cold morning); and now the flogger has gone to
the stable for his whip {vide laundress’s evidence,
which the P.M. believed), and you, with the cold
shower-bath deluging your shivering sores, you
are waiting . . . waiting , . . pricking your ears
for the sound of his returning steps . . . with the
whip . . . the greenhide whip ! Doesn't it make
you feel cheerful and early Christian, hey v Don't
you thank the goodness and the grace that < n
your birth have smiled, and have made you, not a
dumpling little heathen girl, reared in sin and
darkness, but a happy young Australian, in a
land of churches, and Sunday-schools, and polo-
playing RESPECTABILITY. Doesn't it . . .
—Ex-Respectable.

* » * *
The alleged trick of the Bathurst palmist
and doctress who, after sundry passes, pinched the
abdomen of a patient and produced some “ tap -
worms,” which a doctor pronounces to be merely
the “ skin ” of an egg, has its prototype in the
practice of the Northern Territory blacks. One
of the tribe falls sick. The medicine-man works
up the excitement of the crowd, then calls for
silence, and in the midst of solemn incantations
makes a plunge, pinches some part of the sufferer’s
body and at once holds up a fragment of stick or
stone which he professes to have drawn out of the
body and which he claims to have been the dis-
turbing agent.

The projected Metropolitan (Sydney)
Charity Organisation runs imminent risk of being
stung to death by sects and private parties inter-
ested in little schemes of their own. It is possible
to live by charity as well as on charity.

operates. Melb. Sportsman would be only too

PALMISTRY EXTRAORDINARY.—A
HARMY SKETCH.
H. : “Herbert Booth says he has con-
gratulated ‘ the General ’ on Australia having
topped England in the self-denial collection. It
recalls to him the fact that while the Army makes
headway in Australasia, and seemingly does not
decline in Europe, America, or India, it is
on the wane at its very heart in England.
Same as with Buddhism in India, its birth-
place, where there are now only about two
million Buddhists, whereas this faith has
more adherents than any other on earth. A
grievous crux with General Booth is the develop-
ment of Socialism among his officers. He rigor-
ously snips it off, and weeds away the Socialists.
Any approach to the benevolence of Ben Tillett
is nauseous to the General’s backbone of financial
supporters, Cory, and other millionaires, who dump
him down a thousand pounds at a pop.”

The merits of ammonia are becoming gradually
understood. Its cleansing powers areas great as
they are healthy. In the matutinal bath, which
was recently threatened by certain water com-
panies , with a small tax, a tablespoonful of
Scrubb s Cloudy Household Ammonia forms a re-
freshing and useful addition. It has a softening
effect upon the water, and a bracing effect upon
the skin. A large shilling bottle also fulfils other
requirements, by removing stains from clothes
and brightening cutlery and silver. The praises
of Scrubb s Antiseptic Toilet Soap are also deserv-
lng of celebration, and amid the rivalry which at
the present day exists among various soaps, this
will certainly hold its own.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 2, 1897,
Mr Dear Myee—
u y ear ’ s C U P will be known locally as the
China Cup.” So many race-goers went to Melb.
by the huge P. and O. steamer that half Sydney
was at the Quay either going off in the ship or
waving good-byes to the pleasurers. The women
wore their smartest frocks, and the departure had
the look of a society function. Gay dresses dotted
the green, and gave the point a garden-party
aspect. The departing show-folk, Ada Wil-
loughby and E. Shand, had an interesting send-
off. Ada has won more “ front ” admiration than
almost any visiting fbotlight fairy, but her depar-
ture wasn’t considered altogether a calamity
behind the scenes.. Jealousy, perhaps ? E. Shand
had also an admiring contingent. I never see a
theatrical leave-taking without remembering
Vincent Crummies bidding farewell to his “ lion-
hearted boy.”
In all my life I have never heard so many
divorce-rumors as are current now. All sorts of
people are named as impending customers of
Judge G. B. Simpson in his matrimonial jurisdic-
tion. The names of at least two prominent poli-
ticians are freely handled as “ co.’s,” and scandal-
mongers don’t hesitate to talk about an Australian
Governor. But I have authentic information, in
regard to at least three cases which have been
most circumstantially gabbled about, that there is
absolutely nothing (except malevolence) in the
rumors circulated j while as regards one or two
other cases, their significance is much discounted
by the fact that those “moving” are chronically-
necessitous persons, whose names, according to
public opinion, simplyspell blackmail.
At a high-toned concert, last week, two per-
formers had a disagreement on the platform. A
ruddy-haired ’eelloist was scraping a strain by
Popper—nobody seems to write ’cello music except
Popper and, occasionally, Dunokler—and his ac-
companist’s fingers tripped dutifully up and down
the piano-keys, when suddenly the astonished
audience heard the big fiddle break off in a loud
twang, the player hurling an emphatic remark at
the pianist, and beheld him abruptly disappear
down the platform-steps, while the accompanist,
looking like an indignant bantam, slowly followed
him.
Marriages are made in—thb registry-offices.
Said a Sydney employment-agency employer the
other day to a single man who had been worrying
him for work : —“ Look here, if you were married
I could fix you up with a first-rate place ; yourself
—coach, garden, &c. ; wife —cook and laundress.”
The man went away, returned inside the hour
with a wife, and took the place. A little thing
like marriage couldn’t be allowed to stand in the
way of £75 a year in these times.
Ilorence Schmidt’s numerous friends and ad-
mirers rallied round her on Saturday night at her
concert in the Town Hall, and breathed a sigh of
satisfaction at the brilliant way in which she
threw off the mad scene from “ Lucia,” the best
piece of the programme. In this she was assisted
by a smiling flautist, whose name I wouldn’t
attempt to spell. He raised the spirits Of the
entire house by his joyful look, and when his turn
was done he came in and beamed from a front
seat. The soprano, clad in a well-fitting blue
bodice, was handed up much assorted garden-
stuff, and made an effect a la Trilby by clasping
tenderly a large sheaf of white lilies, passed
respectfully up from the front row. As a counter-
blast t° the joyful fluter the baritone Staedtgen
vvailed forth a lay of the most soul-harrowing
sadness, and later took a top note that rivalled
sister Mary Jane’s. Mrs. Riley and Mr. Wood-
house sang well. The house was not crowded,
but the Derby-night is an off-night here, and the
Town Hall is too large for solo concerts, anyhow.
A hall sized midway between the vast Centennial
and the well-like Centenary is a long-felt want.
An unexciting little evening party was given
last Wednesday evening in the Equitable build-
ings by the committee of the new Teachers’
Registry, which has fixed its local habitation in a
convenient room in that sky-scraping palace. The
company received light refreshments and an ex-
planatory circular with the rules of the new ven-
ture which ought to prove as successful as it is use-
ful and badly wanted. The committee names are
freely adorned with M. A. and other learned caps,
and represent the highest dondom in this province.
Miss Garran arranged the meeting which went off
successfully. The circular shows some fine ex-
amples of scholastic jaw-breakers. Any vacancy
is to be filled up by “ co-optation,” which must be
a useful kind of stop-gap if it fills every vacuum.
University English is always Latinised into
corpse-like frigidity.
Superstition must be fading out among Aus-
tralians. It was formerly thought unlucky to
meet a squinting person on the threshold of any
new undertaking, but at a recent wedding in an
Australian capital the bride courageously allowed
two cross-eyed bridesmaids to follow her to the
altar. Such little outbreaks of defiance of fate
are called by goody folk a tempting of Provi-
dence.
The professional bridesmaid is the London
novelty. Young ladies, beautiful in face and
form, especially Americans, earn large fees as
bridesmaids to perfect strangers, and make it a
regular business. A New York millionaire hired
all the finest he could obtain in the markpt for his
daughter’s wedding. This is something to refresh
farcical comedy.
The parties in the arbitration suit now being
tried in No. 2 Jury Court, which has had a rather
longer run than “ Charley’s Aunt,” are arranging
a theatre-party in honor of the arbitrator, and
have invited him to choose between the piece
played at the Standard Theatre and the drama at
the Lyceum— i.e., between “ Rip Van Winkle ”
and “Fallen Among Thieves.”
The Hambourgs and J. Lemmone, assisted by
some well-known local artists, announce a concert
in the Town Hall on Friday night in aid of the
Consumptives’ Hospital. The artists will gener-
ously hand over the gross taking to the charity,
as was done by the two brothers at their recent
Melb. concert, at which they raised over .£2OO for
two deserving objects.
Brisbane folk, I hear, are becoming so economi-
cal that they are hiring servants on the co-
operative system. Two Nice families, closely
related, but settled far apart, share thus the ser-
vices of a Kanaka who does general utility for
gardens and kitchens of both houses, spending a
fortnight at each. Another house-holder, also
one of the Nicest, loans out the family slavey to
his married daughter, and poor overworked Biddy
has to serve two mistresses and trudge backwards
and forwards between the two—day-about.
An audience assern bled by invitation in the
ir’ii d kist week to hear little Frieda
JVliJJer, a child-artist of 12—all infant phenomena
stop short of ’teens—expound her musical dex-
terity on a concert grand piano. She is a gifted
child, and as a feat of memory alone her show-off
was notable. It was displayed not only in going
through one classical op. after another without
book, but in taking part in duetts for two pianos,
in these events the youthful pianist had some-
times to take a spell while the second instrument,
worked by Frieda’s teacher, did a lap or two
solus. It is a difficult feat to chip in again exactly
on the beat, but the little Hambourg in short
skirts never missed her time, showing that she
possesses a cool head as well as a cool hand. All
j'j flapping, flowers, and grown-up criticism
didn t give her a nerve-quiver. The music of the
near future will be a complicated kind of sound-
puzzle. Lvery fashionable noise-factory now
tu ins i. out dexterous piano-thumpers of the
brilliant executant” school, and no girl can
expect to score as a “promising amateur” with
anything less than a brilliant effort in five flats by
a foreign somebody ending in “ owski.”
Of all the pastels showing at the Society of
Artists by Alice Muskett, the design which the
committee lingered longer over in debate than any
other was the first to bear the coveted little red
ticket in the corner. The artist has learned
in Bans the newest notions in frames, as well as
methods; the snake twining among the lilies in
the bordering woodwork of the picture gives an
up-to-date impression of suggestion much more
than is actually expressed ; also, Miss Muskett
displays, since her return from foreign parts, the
Merode manner of ear-concealing hairdressing—a
fashion that hasn’t caught on here yet to any ap-
preciable extent. Many of the Naicest would be
ever so much nicer still, if they didn’t show their
ears.
A wedding which caused an excited social flutter
last week was that of Miss Dalton, of North
bydney, to Mr. Mark Sheldon. The R.C. church
in Ridge-street was crammed with guests and
populace almost as at some of the sensa-
tional hitchings in the city churches. In the sar-
torial show, the Cardinal, who performed the rite,
aided by many priests, was easily first. His
imminence, who had got himself up very dressily
in a towering hat and yards of silk, with
an elaborate crook of office, inserted an unusual
amount of preaching into the ceremony. For the
bride and bridegroom, gaily-decked kneeling-
stools were provided ; white for her, purple for
rum. The bride s passage down the isle had to be
cleared ahead for her through the throng-. She
looked orthodoxly nice in the usual bridal way.
At the subsequent garden-party at the bride’s
fathers.house there were hundreds of guests and
a room full of toast-racks, photo-frames and other
souvenirs, Pa helping to soothe the path of life for
his daughter and son-in-law with many thousands
AT el , Ul S, a y! a house “ for keeps.” Mr. and Mrs.
Mark oheldon are honeymooning at Melb. Cup.
Bke Australian Health Society, at its recent
Melb. meeting, discussed cookery. Our national
failing and its terrible results were discoursed on
by Dr. Neild, who has always elevated good
cookery to a religion. Like most intellectual
men > th e doctor is a small and dainty feeder him-
self, and his outbursts of righteous wrath against
criminal culinary mistakes, especially in baby
food, are refreshing to witness. A practical sug-
gestion which cropped up deserves immediate
adoption in Sydney quite as much as in Melb.—
inspection of hotels and restaurant kitchens. The
public would benefit, but the inspector who
pounced down unexpectedly on some of the dark
corners during the preparations for a meal
wouldn t have much appetite for his own dinner,
it doesn tdo to be too particular, though ; pro-
bably if a guest were to see a grand banquet in all
the stages of preparation he would eat but little
or it. The best cooks are not the cleanest.
Chinese are Francatellis in the art; but I once
heard a lady, who had stayed in a swagger Hong-
kong hotel and enjoyed its dainty dishes, describe
the horrors of the Chinese kitchen, into which she
penetrated accidentally. She lost her appetite for
a week.

W. Beattie, had a brilliant run of one consecutive
u jthe Opera House last Friday, before an
audience which lavishly spread itself over the
high-priced portion of the house, while back
benches were empty. The drama depicted the
misfortunes of a rising artist, Mr. Burns-Walker
who having abruptly married his model, Mrs.’
Kleming, was haunted by the fear that his irascible
uncle would resent the rash act, but the wiles of
Nancy won over the relative (Mr. G. W. Lambert
disguised m an overcoat and a Roman nose), and
the play ended happily amid a crash of plaster
from a falling ceiling which brought the climax.

Tim lady who rules the destinies of Sunday
limes women’s page has unearthed a married
couple vvho live “ comfortably ” on .£1 a week
and advise others to go and do likewise. The
happy pair—“ they are still very young ’’—inhabit
a room costing 4s. per week, and have the remain-
ing 16 bob to face the world with. Thus the
female economist who advises such a step goes
l ess than John Davidson, in his “ Thirty Bob
a Week,” and he declared that marrying on that
sum was a naked child against a hungry wolf.”
However, this 20s. couple “ not only live, but
extst beautifully, in a room which, I am told, is
like fairyland.” Alas! Primitive man, who
lived in a cave and wore a dress-suit of wood, had
a much better time.
, One of the past glories of Wagga is that it gave
1000 golden sovs. and a cup of the same metal for
a horse-race in the days when Australia was
younger. Every new-comer to the town has it
dinned into his ears. Just now the local turf club
has a fit of rejuvenation. Efforts are being made
todig up ghosts of former palmv times—the times
when the .Wagga w_omen used to glitter in-earnest.
Mrs. - , wife of one of the T.C. committee men,
did a bold thing one day when the thousand-
pound cup was run for. Between each race the
lady took her carriage and drove home. On each
occasion the women on the stand and lawn were
transfixed with envy at seeing Mrs. arrayed
in a different costume of the latest fashion and
costliest fabric.
A picnic party of lads and lasses, after a glorious
day s ramble among the fairy bowers of a pic
turesque fern-gully under the shadow of Mount
Wellington lately, committed the indiscretion of
having its photo, taken. Everybody was very
tired, but supremely happy and careless, and the
girls, in their negligent innocence, thought not of
the morrow. When the finished picture appeared
in the photographer’s window, exposed to the
calm, critical gaze of an unfeeling public and
sundry Hobart mothers, there was much con-
sternation and many tears. For boys and girls to
caressingly clasp necks and waists didn’t seem
indiscreet —at a picnic, but 1 The photo.-man
soon sold out.
The poor and helpless widow seems to “ get
left ” wherever she is. Even in Charters Towers,
where money is abundant (a correspondent tells
me), there are folks mean enough to sweat her. A
seamstress for a wealthy drapery firm has to
keep six children on what she can make by
machining blouses at 3s. per dozen.
News arriving by post for the “ social column ”
is now regarded with suspicion in some newspaper
offices. One journal was cruelly “ got at ”in that
way by a baekblocks correspondent, who sent in a
flowery description of a high-toned revel in the
district-town, given by one of the local Nicest,
and attended by all the usual country-town
sassiety—P.M. and wife, bank bosses and wives,
medico and wife, &c. The par. ended with the
usual—“ Among those present were.” A few
days after the item appeared, there arrived at the
office a horrified epistle from the indignant giver
of the feast, declaring that he had been insulted
by the publication of the names of the alleged
guests, many of whom had never been inside his
house. The irreverent par.-writer had, to brighten
the news-item, rung into the party-list the names
of all the notorious petty-larcenists and drunks in
the township.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“On Derby-Day, ’97, the wind was certainly
tempered to the shorn lamb— i.e. , the weather
suited the book of the woman with one fete frock.
She, poor thing, unable to provide for all possible
aberrations of temperature, hopes for the best, and
at this time of year puts on festal raiment suited
to an alleged ‘summer gala.’ Last Saturday, ‘as
the clouds rolled by,’ the day grew glaringly hot
and sunny, to the disgust of wise virgins, who,
with purple and fine linen in readiness for rain
and shine respectively, had donned the former in
the shape of coats and skirts, handsome cloth
Russian blouses, and similar beacons of a well-
stocked wardrobe. But nothing looked so well
that day as flimsy muslins, thin silks, cool can-
vasses with lacy flounces, frills and tucks mean-
dering up and down the skirts or starting higher
at the back and sloping towards the front hem.
Gov. and Lady Brassey, who stayed at home
mourning for a deceased Royalty, probably en-
joyed a restful time. After all, wouldn’t it be
agm human nature to take sackcloth and ashes
seriously all on account of the demised mother-in-
law of an Heir Apparent once-removed ? In the
absence of official heads, tvvo coachfuls of vice-
regal relatives, visitors and suite arrived unob-
trusively on the scene, shepherded by Mrs. Free-
man-Thomas. This pretty young matron, like
Lady Hopetoun in her time, has laid on layers of
since her arrival in these benighted colonies.
With the fierce glare of sassiety’s eye ever beating
upon her, a woman either shrivels or expands. If
handsome and aware of it, she probably chooses
the better part.
“To return to Gov. Brassey’s daughter: she
looked much taller and more dignified on Saturday
than the small, kittenish person of her first Melb.
Cup. Her Derby frock was pale green silk, with
fal-lals of black chiffon and ficelle lace applique,
and a big black hat with plumes and ospreys blow-
ing in the breeze, and green roses nestling on her
hair bright brown hair, by the way, though the
owner is a regular brunette. 1 Sweet Alice with
hair so brown ’ is almost an unknown quantity
among Australian-born girls. Our sun undoubt-
edly darkens tress-tints. If you start life lint-
topped, you are ‘ mousey ’ by the time you turn-up
your corkscrews. Chestnuts and similar shades
deepen into the darkest possible brown, to all in-
tents and purposes black, that commonest of Aus-
tralian hair-colors.
“ The two Miss Brasseys, fairish young ladies,
more like ‘Uncle Tom’ in appearance than his
own dark-eyed daughter, duplicated one another’s
rather creased white satins fluted with pale green,
and .Tuscan hats with feathers and pink roses.
A dark-haired Sydney girl wore the simplest of
yellow pin-spot muslins, while Miss Sylvia Darley
looked pale and willowy in white silk and chiffon
and a floppy leghorn hat.
Talking of hats, the ‘ garden of flowers ’ has
had its day, so has the jumble, suggestive of
Josephs coat of many colors. Head-coverings
are still bright enough, but they have some con-
nection with the rest of the costume, and the
gayest specimens ring the changes on one brilliant
hue, with, maybe, black, white, or both, thrown
m. All-pink structures, for instance, were effective
items in the Derby millinery show, and seem to
have driven their scarlet predecessors off the field,
buch a peach-bloom confection, topping the white
silk frock worn by one of a family of pretty Kew
sisters, made up a color-harmony equal to a blend of
cochineal and condensed milk in a bi-colored
batch of cocoanut-ice.
As usual, the ladies in the vice-regal pen had a
neat rather than a gaudy time, and one visitor in
particular watched the racing world wearily
enough through her long-handled pince-nez. The
aides, as in former years, treated their fair com-
panions to short grass constitutionals, all but the
jj,. sheep of the flock. He only quitted the
saddhng-paddock for the lawn right in front of
his rightful fold, where he frisked conversationally
in the cheerful company of a golden-haired society
matron whose white-scrolled terra-cotta silk was
combined with cream pleated chiffon.
Sartorially speaking, the vice-regal attaches
were more-or-less uninteresting in dark grey, un-
brightened by a flower. The Governor’s son-in-
law wore a green tie to match his wife’s frock,
while the longest and leanest of the staff ‘took
on dreadfully, when he first arrived, about some
slight damage to his hat. As he hung over its
impaired glossiness, with all his soul in his eyes,
spectators thought pitifully of Evo finding mur-
dered Abel, and similar weepful chestnuts.
“ From the stand, Miss Elliott-Page showed up
on the lawn as well as most. Her white silk skirt,
striped sparsely with black, and festooned with
diagonal frills of black chiffon, had a bodice of
turquoise velvet, a chiffon Empire scarf, and her
hat was tipped rakishly to on6* side.
A poor little Indian ‘Prince,’ rather like a
fraal monkey, in drab tweed suit, brown boots,
and fez cap, seemed the loneliest waif at Fleming-
ton. He lingered wistfully near the band all day,
as “ R’ cheered him up to hear the music play,
and finally fell back on the society of his atten-
dant, a more imposing figure than his master, in
red tunic and white trousers. Meanwhile, the
tiny Princess,’ swathed in white muslin, crept
about mouselike, followed by a miniature maid.
Ihe latter s skirt, slit up the side, showed short,
skin-tight pants and bare brown legs, weighted
with heavy anklets, in odd contrast to the pigmy’s
meek, plastered, parted hair.
“Lady Holled-Smith’s Derby gown was black
silk, scrolled with purple. Miss Daisy Madden
came in cool, crushy, white muslin, and leghorn
hat, worn saucily askew. A huge amber-straw
coal-scuttle bonnet, heaped with ostrich feathers,
overshadowed a puffy, bunchy, yellow-flowered
muslin gown worn by a fresh-faced damsel wit!
parted hair, who possibly mistook the date of tht
fancy ball, and came to the races as ‘ my grand
mother when a girl,’ or ‘Dame Durden.’ Anothej
monstrosity in headgear was a ‘ flop,’ dipping
nearly to the wearer’s waist behind, and curling
weirdly in front ’midst a plantation of plumes.
A well-cut black brocade, with toque and sun-
shade of bright turquoise-blue, was one of the
most effective costumes of the day, and second tc
none in quiet costliness was Mrs. Everard
Browne’s primrose silk, all fleecy flounces of ivory
silk-muslin, tucks and lace.
_ “ Pastor Dr. Strong’s elder daughter was mar-
ried last week, not at papa’s church, but on the
Earental hearthrug. Dr. Strong sensibly loathes
ig wedding functions, with a gaping crowd in
the church, and much parade of flowers, teas
and hymns. His newly-married damsel was one
of the prettiest girls in Melbourne, and has
character as well as beauty. She married a son of
Brooks’ paper mills.
“ Visitors to the Princess’ one night last week
witnessed a spectacle in the circle that subtracted
all the glow from Gordon’s Turneresque sunset off
St. Helena. The tableau was provided by Prince
Najal Ismad, etc., etc., etc., from Hyderabad,
Princess Najal, ete., etc., andsuite, and the Orien-
tal grandeur of the costumes of the Prince and
the Princess, and the suite, quite paled the sar-
torial magnificence of Napoleon’s gaudy officers.
The Prince is a gorgeous, coffee-colored creature,
but his Princess remains a mystery, since nothing
of her was visible amongst her fabulous garments
but a pair of dark, glittering orbs. Comparing
this Asiatic reserve with the liberal display of bare
European woman scattered about the theatre, one
appreciated the wisdom of the Oriental system
that makes matrimony a monopoly for the man.
The shock of this discovery about coroner Youl’s
marriage fell upon Miss Youl. Since her mother’s
death, many years ago, she devoted herself to her
father and the family, with the greatest self-sacri-
fice. She is familiar in Collins-street, rather
petite, with something of her sire’s fine **nsible
nose. Imagine the fine, cheery old doctor, with
his secret! However did he manage to keep it?
‘Murder doesn’t always out.' One can imagine
him chuckling to himself, as he solemnly enunci-
ated the contrary. And when on earth was it he
went to Sydney, to get married ? It must have
been that invalid excursion. The widow inserted
her bombshell ‘ in memoriam ’ three months after
the veteran’s demise.
“ A leading Melbourne shop which gets Govern-
ment House patronage has the usual large-
framed certificates to that effect from Governors
Brassey, Hopetoun and Loch. But the Brassey
and Loch sheets are kept in the shop, while that
of the Scottish laird is shown in the window,
which incident is peculiarly illustrative of local
feeling.
“ Melb. Women’s Hospital Committee, an
aggregation of society dames, has decided to waste
a heap of expensive certificates owing to the word
gynaecological ’ being misspelt, inasmuch as it was
not engraved with a diphthong <e. Considering
that diphthong letters are semi-obsolete, and, at
the present time, are merely pedantic affectations,
and that, anyhow, the difference is infinitesimal,
this throwing avyay money by an alleged charit-
able institution is a sinful waste.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Vic. Penal Department the other day
found itself powerless to open the big safe which
held its books, accounts, and coin. For once Red-
tape did not carry the day. A quiet sub rosd
message was sent out to Pentridge prison asking
tlie Governor of that hostelry to send down the
best pick-lock he had in his charge, and the neces-
sary article arrived and did his work with neatness
and despatch.
Grantham, Q.C., the Tory hack who has
been elevated to the English bench (vide
cable), gets his appointment as a reward for much
impudent mendacity in the cause of law’norder.
He was one of the authors of the silly slander
that Bradlaugh once took out his watch and
challenged the Deity to strike him dead in five
minutes. It was a lie, but it served. On a sub-
sequent occasion, Grantham appeared for the de-
fence in a libel-action brought by Bradlaugh
against one Laker, who had said that Bradlaugh
had been had up for neglecting his family and
leaving them chargeable to the union.” Gran-
tham told the jury that Bradlaugh could not
possibly be injured by any slander ! However,
Bradlaugh got a verdict. Now, Grantham, after
a long career of dirty work for the Tory party,
goes to the bench. Moral: Blessed are those who
lie and slander for the cause of wealth and the
classes.

his province holding an Attorney-Generalship in
Australia. Byrnes, Want, Isaacs, Kingston were,
most of them, born in or near their own capitals.

thitherward. Bennett’s explanation of the col-
lapse of New Australia is short—“ Wine and
Woman.”

The sowl of the late Martin Loughlin
ought to be getting along comfortably. £30,000
of his Ballarat gold is building a church in Ireland.
John Kentwell, the first to grow fruit on
a large scale in N.S.W., died at Castle Hill last
week at the age of 93. Like most patriarchs, he
has left descendants like to the sands of the sea
in number.
Recorded that Jack Want, when member
of a Ministry some few years ago, received a wire
while in Tasmania to this effect: —“ Come back at
once ; bursting up.” Jack’s reply: “Burst and
be .”
A lately-deceased Melbourne millionaire is
not likely to turn in his grave on account of his
children’s extravagance. One of the gilded heirs
has been advertising for a small furnished house
at a low rental.
Australia will be curious to know the nature
of the cabled “ accident ” which eliminated poor
Mr. “ Lily ” Langtry so promptly upon his incar-
ceration in a mad-nouse. Asylum accidents have
sometimes an esoteric history.
N.S.W. Commandant French and Queens-
Dr. Kevin Izod O’Doherty, occupying opposite
poles of political thought, are now a family blend
of genial outlooks. The doctor’s son has married
the Commandant’s daughter.
On Thursday, Oct. 21, Dean Cowper, of
Sydney, now in his 8-th year, celebrated the 63rd
anniversary of his ordination. His first appoint-
ment was as chaplain to the Australian Agricul-
tural Co., at Stroud, N.S.W.
Premier Kingston, in his new role of the
sober, serious, dignified statesman, has electrified
S.A. Parliament. The old summer habiliments
have also been displaced by a black frock-coat and
semi-clerical hat. O Hingland, what crimes are
committed in thy name !
Obit : J. F. Small, aged 76 years, a native
of Parramatta and a 60 years’ resident on the
Clarence River, where, in the pioneer days, he
had many sharp encounters with the aborigines,
numbers of whom, as the yarn goes, did bite the
dust by reason of his unerring aim.

whom all the good stories are told, is alleged to
have finished up 3ome remarks to the children of
a certain school with “ Now boys and girls, Ihope
you will all grow r up good men and women and
become great heroes and heroesses.”

Joseph Rodgers, the gallant Portuguese
sailor who swam ashore with a life-line from the
wreck of the Royal Charter in 1859, died recently
in Liverpool (Eng.) Hospital. On his return to
Melbourne a big purse was subscribed for his
benefit. Latterly he had been in command of a
rb er tug-boat.
