the other day, bequeaths to his wife everything
the testator might die possessed of. The estate
was sworn at close on .£lO,OOO. Mrs. Eddy lately
inherited from a relative of her own a somewhat
similar amount.
British Freethinker attributes to Wilkes
an anecdote which Parkes used to tell of his first
election —when he opposed fat little Kemp, of S.M.
Herald. “Hi canvassed very hard. One man
whom hi hasked for his vote replied : * Hi’d sooner
vote for the devil! ’ ” “ Yes,” said Hi, “ but sup-
posing your friend doesn’t stand ? ”
A new Yic. M.P. has an electioneering-
story of a dear friend, who promised him vote and
support, and, after election, confessed that he had
voted for the other fellow. “ Why,” asked the
M.P., “ surely you do not believe in his opinions,
do you ? ” “ No,” replied the enlightened elector,
“ but, poor fellow ! he’s got such a bad leg.”
Mr. Harmer, the little clean-shaven, spec-
tacled man with the comic legs, who is nominally
Anglican Bishop of Adelaide, writes his flock that
he has postponed his return from England until
February. Mr. Harmer will have been away
nearly a year, and could apparently stay away
altogether only that Adelaide is the “salary ” end
of the billet.
Apropos the consecration of St. Patrick’s
Cathedral, Melb. A few years ago, at a meeting
to raise funds for the big church, Dean O’Hea—
a very wealthy retired clergyman—offered to give
£lOOO if nine others could be got to give a similar
sum. Said Archbishop Carr, who presided, “Now,
no conditions, Dean; down with the dust,” and
the dust came down.
A. S. Bailes, returned to Parliament by
Bendigo (Vic.) electors in place of “Dives”
Lazarus, is an old Parliamentarian, and was sup-
posed to have lost his seat, six years ago, in conse-
quence of some uncomplimentary reflections upon
the life and character of Albert Edward, Prince
of Wales. Bendigo is now understood to have
gone back on Albert Edward.
Vic. : “A preacher tells me this, as a fact:
A little boy was dying, in the Warrnambool dis-
trict, many years ago. His mother called in a
local preacher to pray. He began, ‘ O Lord, if
Thou wilt—’ The mother said, ‘No, I cannot
have it that way, I must have my child.’ The
good man changed his petition accordingly. The
boy grew up to be hanged, as a murderer.”
Paddy Glynn, M.P. (S.A.), who, while the
Sydney Convention was sitting, was married
rather suddenly, expresses his opinion that it will
take him quite two years to get used to it, because
of the confirmed state of bachelorhood he had
lapsed into. Meanwhile, he admires the “ infinite
patience ” of his wife, as manifested in respect to
his gradually-disappearing Bohemianism.
Journalist: “ Duffy, in Melb.,
always wrote out his important politi-
cal speeches, and, however lengthy,
he would speak them verbatim. Thus
the Argus and the Age got the
proofs beforehand with implicit re-
liance, and would only send a re-
porter to put in the “cheer” and
otherwise, or interjections. Casey
thought he could do the same thing,
and furnished the Age with a full
copy beforehand of an important
Ministerial speech which he had to
give as a candidate at Mandurang.
However, the Age sent two reporters,
with the proofs. At about half-past
eight they forwarded a wire: “He
is not speaking one word of it. Dis.
the lot. Our copy will come through
directly.”
Henniker Heaton, before he
blossomed, was just a common, garden
variety of person, like any of our-
selves. He was connected with the
press, and somehow he turned up in
Mudgee, N.S.W., as editor of the
Western Post. Laurence was then
proprietor—known familiarly as
“Rum and Onions.” Now Henniker,
in those days, was a soft, gentle,
caressing sort of chap, with a draper’s-
assistant’s appearance and a Young-
Men’s - Christian - Society manner.
Probably he did not appeal to the
sympathetic approval of “Rum and
Onions,” whose manners were of the
three-pronged-fork order. Anyway,
Henniker, poor fellow, didn’t catch
on to the necessities of the position
of a country editor. He brought out
a paper full of nice ideas and sweet
platitudes, and be was so proud of it
that when, next day, he saw “ Rum
and Onions ” reading a number, and
thought that his face bloomed with
the blushes of approval, he sidled up
and whispered, “A good paper, Mr.
Laurence ; a very creditable- paper,
sir.” “Rum and Onions ” cast one
baleful look at him, felt the paper
between thumb and fingers, and
growled: “Good paper! Why not?
Of course it’s good paper ! Too blanky
good to be spoilt by your dam muck,
anyhow ! ” Henniker was a passenger
per coach next day, and coming to
Sydney found sweeter appreciation
from Sam Bennett. Dear ! dear!
and now he hangs out at the Carlton
Club with belted earls and coronetted
dukes—while poor old Laurence is
frying- -somewhere else.
“ One hears that brutal Guelph story
everywhere,” writes a Bulletin correspondent
from London. “ Sometimes it is this admiral’s
daughter, sometimes that admiral’s, but always
an admiral’s (the American papers give it as
Admiral S ). George apparently did not fancy
the idea at first, and his present miserable and
dejected air dates from that illness he had when
poor dear Clarence dropped out of the running,
and he found it would be politic to revel in the
charms of May. An admiral’s wife, who thought
she was scurvily treated by the Govt, at her
husband’s tragic death, gave the thing away. She
said that ‘the mother-in-law of a prince should
receive a better pension, anyhow.’ The Guelphs
stood in awful dread of the Duffs inheriting the
crown. Hence the prostitution.”
Dr. Charles M‘Carthy, the Sydney medico
of varied talents, who doesn’t “ fear to speak oi
’98,” was taken prisoner in the Franco-Prussiai
war. He left Dublin attached to an ambulance
party, and as soon after as possible got into “ the
thick of it.” Irish sympathies in that day went
wholly with France, but, as England stood
neutral, Irishmen who wished to fight on the side
of France had to go disguised a* ambulance men.

Andrew.
N.S.W. Agent-General Samuel when over
60 married handsome Miss Isaacs, the belle of
Auckland (M.L.), and a member of a cultured, un-
obtrusive, wealthy Jewish family. Major Alfred
Isaacs, Lady Samuel’s elder brother, a tine athlete
in his day and a very presentable amateur come-
dian, died in Sydney a few years ago. Two of
Lady Samuel’s sisters were married out of their
faith to two Auckland medical men—the brothers
Haines.
John Anderson, colleague of Zox for E.
Melbourne, is peculiarly gifted with lucidity of
expression. In a debate on sewerage rating in the
last Parliament the shorthanders “ took him ”
thus:—
I hope that this House will soon retrace their steps, and
seriously consider the position. It is not fair, or just, or
honest that when a portion of a people join in a scheme,
if only- for a time, I hope that the sense of this House
will take into its serious consideration the great injustice
they are going to give to a people who never deserved it,
a people who have always paid their engagements, and
intends to do so ; and the people I refer to who come
into this contract, and want to get out of it ; and I’m
going to remark, Mr. Speaker, they’re getting water now
at a moderate rate, and that was one of the conditions of
their being taken in, which cost an enormous sum of
money, because they have got the water now and don’t
want the sewage and if this House passes this it will do
one of the most dishonorable things ever done in this
House or any other.

Peter Russell, aged 95 years, died last
week at Singleton, N.S. W. He had resided there
80 years—how he must have enjoyed himself !
and well remembered many of the incidents con-
nected with the mutiny at Major Mudie’s, of
“ Castle Forbes.” Six men were tried at Sydney
in 1833 over this affair, five were found guilty;
of these three were handed in lower George-street,
Sydney ; the leaders, Hitchcock and Poole, were
taken to Singleton and hanged from a tree in the
centre of the town.

Melbourne Paunch
Mr. Langton, in being rejected by all constituencies, has
one consolation in being the only Victorian mentioned by
name in Froude’s “ Oceana.”
It wouldn’t be much of an honor if true, but it
isn’t true. Gillies, the late George Verdon,
Astronomer Ellery, Mr. Service, Prof. Irving,
S’william Clarke (referred to as “Sir , a
baronet with £160,000 a year,” and as “ a distin-
guished, high-bred-looking man ”), vigneron De
Castella, and Andrew Rowan, all figure in the
four chapters which Froude devotes to Victoria.
The suicide of Land Commissioner
Warren, at Rockhampton, was a remarkable case
in several ways. Warren’s books were in perfect
condition ; he was well-to-do, correct in life and
perfectly sober. But he is alleged to have used—
by error or otherwise—defaced stamps on letters
(he collected stamps for his grand children ; had a
drawer full of defaced stamps ; and was 65 years
of age with weak eyesight); and certain o! his
upright subordinates plotted to expose him. The
charge so preyed on his mind that he took his own
life in desperation. And the total amount of his
default was at most a few— pence! !
It is claimed for the lately-deceased Mr.
Pentecost, lecturer at Newcastle (N.S.W.) School
of Mines, that he was largely instrumental in
setting on foot the silver-mining boom which led
up to the opening of Broken Hill. Thirteen or 14
years ago the old Board of Technical Education
sent out itinerant lecturers, and Pentecost went
to the Emmaville (N.S.W.) tinfield. At his first
lecture some specimens were handed up, and he
pronounced them silver-lead. Next evening he
lectured' on that combination ; and the day after
that there was a rush for silver claims. From
that time the excitement continued until in suc-
cession Sunny Corner a.nd Broken Hill were
unearthed. There had, however, been previous
silver finds at Boorook, but little came of them.
A Vic. journalist
Re Gowen Evans’ demise. Our world, in general, never
could quite “ place ” Mr. Evans at the Argus, or under-
stand how this apparent faineant came to be a boss apple
in the dumpling. Though fairly well-informed about
newspaper affairs, I did not know till now exactly that he
represented the Edward Wilson interest as against the
Lachlan Mackinnon. Editor Haddon has held the scales.
I must be permitted to think that the tri-partite general-
ship, like most such things, has not been so good as the
one-man-show of redoubtable David Syme, who, despite
the undeniable literary excellence of the Argus, has
fetched up the rough-cut, angular Age from 2000 in 1867,
when Gowen Evans arrived, to what he probably with
justice calls 100,000 in ’97. For data, to correct my
general impressions, I have referred to the flies of 40 years
ago to see what kind of a writer Edward Wilson was. I
find him distinctly inferior, sludgy, thin, hysterical, at
the time when he was to the Argus as Greeley to the
New York Tribune. He secured the proper editor in Mr.
Higinbotham. The cardinal mistake in the life of the
Argus was allowing Higinbotham to leave. Morris's life
of him shows that the reason was that the proprietary
could not go with him in his Liberal ideas. Therefore,
the way was opened for the Age to cut in. Comparing
Melb. with London, the Argus preferred to be the
Standard rather than the Times. All this added gall to
Attorney-General Higinbotham’s Parliamentary privilege
inipri6onment of the Argus p iblisher, for the alleged
libel on M‘Culloch, in saying his statement “ bristled
with falsehoods.” The Argus editors have been Wilson,
Higinbotham, Watts, Jarrett, Windsor, and Haddon.

Julius Knight's particular trouble, as Marcus Superbus
was to conceal his slight tendency to bandyness—not but
what his legs are straighter than a lot of ’em. Hence the
eternal posing and rather excessive padding of those all-
important limbs. Nobody ever saw the Roman Prefect
stand upright with his supports (Heavenly twins, the
girls called them, bless their simplicity !) in juxtaposition
after the manner of an Addie Conyers.
Dear Bollbstin,— Had “Paradis ’’ever seen the authen-
tic portraits of the rival empresses in Paris he would
surely never suggest that Miss Page bears any physical
resemblance to Josephine, who was two inches shorter
than Napoleon, and, as depicted by dramatist Wills, a
soft, dinging, lovable, lachrymose oreature, who is con-
tinually using the pathetio stop.” Now, Miss Page is bold
and vigorous, not to say masculine, in her method; is
destitute of pathos, and, beside Julius Knight, looks a
giantess. Miss Perrar wears the little bob-curls which
were the vogue at that period; Miss Page a modern coiffure,
which may be becoming, but is certainly not historically
correct. In what, then, does the latter lady resemble the
Creole from Martinique ?— Parisian.
The Australian theatrical world looks forward to a very
interesting event which will necessitate the temporary
retirement of a certain pretty actress.
A Sydney paper says that Mr. J. C. Williamsoft, so far
as London is concerned, has been formed into a no-
hability company. The idea of a no-liability concern is
distinctly humorous. 'Tis true there are many no-
liability theatrical managements, as actors learn to their
cost; but modesty prevents these managements from pro-
claiming the fact to the world at large by registration.
Williamson and co. have never been built that way. The
no-hability laws apply to mining co’s, and those only.
Let us hope W. and Al.’s London management may be a
gold-nnne.

N.: “ ‘ Confessions of a Ballet Lady. How Women are
Victimised in England,’ now going round the British press
is wormwood, and doubtless partly true—the emanation
Of a ballet-woman on the shelf. The report, or interview
says she has been ‘ conspicuous as a character-actress in
burlesque and comic opera.’ She calls the stage-manager
the tyrant of the stage.’ He is too apt to be an ogre
seeking to ruin pretty girls. They get on, according as
they favor him or not. She gives samples of the language
of one of the best-known bullies, who is one of the
greatest stage-managers in the profession.’ He called
out to the proprietor, before the whole company, ‘My
God, where did you get that girl with the horse’s face?’
His customary way of ordering is: ‘That thing in blue.’
That cow in red,’ ‘That girlwith the sailor hat,’ ‘That
fat woman.’ He has been known to send a poor girl into

a 'u An Psenist lady friend went out to Islington to see
Achurch do ‘ A Doll’s House,’ ” writes an Australian to
•ta, tV- LLETIN . rom London. “The theatre was packed
with Islingtonians, who never got such a surprise in their
lives. These Britishers lingered on in the vain hope that
something would happen, but nothing happened. Why
Nora left her man they could not fathom. He didn’t get
drunk and bash her; she never even had a black eye;
her maintenance was ample ; there was no other woman
m the background. The Islingtonians laughed at the
proposition that the separation was on account of “ in-
compatibility of. soul.” In short, they treated the whole
Ibing as a huge joke, and tolerated it good-humoredly
None of the people in it acted as they had ever seen
married couples act down Islington way. Achurch went
through her part heroically, and acted finely, though
running into flesh since her Australian tour.”
A Melbourne man with an alleged comedy in his posses-
sion interviewed a manager recently. He found the
great man sitting grumpily in a c-hair with a large, fat,
black cigar in his mouth and his corporation accumulated
in front of him, and explained his business. The manager
grunted heavily. Then the visitor went into details
about the plot and characters, and the manager grunted,
lie read extracts for half-an-hour or more, while the
manager fixed him with a hostile eye and grunted at
intervals. Then the author asked for a definite opinion.
Look ere, said the tired potentate, “ye can ’ave yer
Ti?°M in operas, an’ ye can ’ave yer bloomin’ comedies,
the bloomin legitimate drammer’s good enough for me.
lin goin’to shut up the bloomin’ theatre. Ye can take
the bloomin theatre if ye like, an’ ye can hire the bloomin’
company if ye like, an’ ye can put on ver own bloomin’
comedy if ye like. I’m bloomin’ well full of it.” And so
v.r i!i er , slfc5 lfc of Australian literature went where the
blighted dramas go.


One of the shows of Sydney is the shop of
A. G. Ring, 33 Imperial Arcade. The proprietor
has spent his life, including three trips round the
world, in collecting stamps, and he has now a
wonderful hoard. He has many stamps cata-
logued by the “perfesh” at J6lO, .£2O, and .£3O.
He has one stamp which is as precious to him as
the crown to the king—he would not sell it for
.£lO,OOO. He would fill any man up with lead
who would try to steal it. As a stamp-collector,
A. G. Ring is a boss.*
Queensland is not the happiest hunting-
ground for a missionary, unless the Gospel-man
can punch his forty whiskeys a day, and send the
scorner home on a shutter. It is not the Paradise
for a business-man unless his commercial instinct
will start sampling nightcaps at 9 o’clock in the
morning. It is no place for the politician who
cannot drink the Opposition under the table, and
captivate an audience of total abstainers with his
breath. Queensland has a nose that can scent
good whiskey a mile off, and a palate which can
never be deceived. And Q. has pronounced Sir
John Power’s whiskey to be the sweetest thing
it’s had.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*

ple under such circumstances as these surrounding
the effects of Warner’s Safe Cure. There is no
place in the world where a good thing is better
appreciated than right here at home. Go where
you will, inquiries prove that Warner’s Safe Cure
has saved people from lingering disease and death,
and Australians give credit to whom due. Mr.
C. I. Bohler, Cowan Road, St. Ives Sydney,
says : “It is with great pleasure that I write you
in order to testify to the excellence of Warner’s
Safe Cure. Some twelve months ago I was suf-
fering from kidney troubles and affection of the
liver. After trying different remedies, I gave
Warner’s Safe Cure a trial, and after a few doses
experienced an improvement. The pain in my
right side disappeared, and I felt better in health
than I had for a long time previously. I only
took two bottles of this valuable medicine, and
felt so gratefnl that I have recommended it to all
my friends.”

When the pelican builds her nest she
builds it pretty extensively. On Sandringham
run (Centralia) about 40 miles from the station, on
the edge of a dry lake-bed, may be seen a patch
of country about a quarter of a mile long and 300
yards broad, oovered with pelican nests about
three feet apart and apparently scooped out by the
wing. Thousands of eggs are to be seen dotted
over the place.
It is not an uncommon thing bo discover a
bullock with a horn growing out of its head and
into it again, but a beast has been discovered in
Nathaba (Vic.), the point of whose horn had
entered the skull below the eye and grown right
through, coming out again on the other side !
The bullock was apparently none the worse for it,
and when the horn was sawn and the point re-
moved it left a hole that could be seen through.
The writer has seen a bullock with the two horns
meeting at the points, and one growing into the
other. The point of one had been sawn off to
prevent contact, and then the animal had been
left to itself, with the result mentioned.
Dr. R. Semon, in his “ In Australian Bush
and Coasts of Coral Seas,” makes some interesting
zoological remarks. He came to Australia
specially to get young stages of Geratodus, the
Q. lung-breathing fish, Echidna, the marsupial
ant-eating hedgehog, and other distinctive beasts
of this continent. According to Semon the platy-
pus chiefly lives on one of the cockle tribe (Corbi-
cula nepeanensis). The platypus and the ant-eater
reproduce but once a year, and ripe and fertilised
eggs are found only in the left oviduct. Rats !
He also declares that the dingo is responsible for
the disappearance from the mainland of the Tas-
manian devil ( Sarcophilus) and the Tasmanian
striped wolf ( Thylacinus ), which, by the way, is
not a wolf at all, but simply a carnivorous mar-
supial.
R. : “ Vic. blacks of Buninyong district
continually repeat a tradition that once upon a
time Mts. Buninyong and Elephant quarrelled,
and heaved fire and rocks at each other. This
has been the slender basis for the contention that
the blacks lived in Victoria before the great vol-
canio period in Ballarat district which produced
the basaltic rocks overlying the gold-bearing
country. Now, however, geologist Hart, of
Ballarat School of Mines, has found beneath the
lower basalt in the Buninyong Estate G.M. Co.’s
mine, in the black clay at a depth of 238 ft., a
whole pile of fossil bones of kangaroos and walla-
bies. Biologist T. S. Hall, of Melb. University,
not only has identified the bones as marsupial,
but declares that one big rib-bone of the extinct
giant kangaroo has been altered in shape for some
purpose by human agency. Which goes to prove
that the blacks’ tradition is correct in substance,
and discloses a big eruption of the two now in-
active volcanoes.”

During the Orange-and-Green row at Cool-
gardie one poor Paddy was getting chips knocked
off his face by a burly Williamite when his wife
urged him to greater efforts with “Kill him,
w addy—kill the ! ” Poor Pat eased off for a
moment and said : “ Howly Mother, ain’t I batin’
the blanky sowl-case out of ’im ! ”

West’s Hydraulic Tyre Compressor.
(See letterpress on this page.)
The “ very wealthy ” Tagh Mahomet,
Afghan camel-driver and merchant, who came to
a sudden end lately at Coolgardie, panned out for
only after all.
West’s Hydraulic Tyre
Compressor.
(See illustration on this page.)
The accompanying block illustrates a re-
markable invention which has lately been
introduced, to Australia. It is a hydraulic
machine for setting vehicle tyres cold. It
consists of a heavy, cast bed-piece, to which
is attached a weldless steel ring of great
strength, on the inner side of which a num-
ber of hydraulic rams are assembled. To
the left of the illustration is a three-throw
pump of special design, which forces oil into
the rams, and makes them all converge
upon the tyre, which, with the wheel, is
placed inside of them. Hew tyres are made
to drop loosely over the wheel, and they are
then compressed on the wheel. Old tyres
may be tightened without removing the bolts.
Mr. 0. Napier Bell, M.1.C.E., who has
carefully examined the machine in use in
Sydney, says : “The essential feature of the
invention is that cold wrought iron can be
compressed within certain limits without any
change in its < ther dimensions. I do not
know that that property was ever known
before, as one would naturally suppose that
if iron were squeezed up so as to alter its
length, its width and thickness would be
altered also. I examined a ring of iron
about sft. 4in. in diameter and 4in. wide and
lin. thick ; the ring was 16ft. 9in. on inside
circumference ; after being compressed in
the machine, it measured 15ft. Bin., but the
width and thickness were unaltered.
I find that the specific gravity of the iron
was altered from its presumably original S.G.of 7.65
(as given in Kircaldie’s experiments)to 8.04, which is
tlie S.G. of steel.” Colonel Sir George S. Clarke
R.E., K.C.M.G., chief of the gun and carriage de-
partment at the Royal Arsenal, Woolwich (Eng.)
where the machine is regularly used, has made experi-
ments similar to Mr, Bell’s, with similar results
Ihe Scientific American, in which a description
f Q “ e Machine was recently published (February 6
1597), also mentions some remarkable results nro-
duced by the machine.
Briefly described, the merits of the machine are :
it sets tyres cold ; it hardens and improves the material
ot the tyre ; it makes wheels perfectly round • it ob-
viates the charring of the rims inseparable from the
old process, and it enables tyres to be tightened wit.h-
bolts or injuring the paint.
The inventor of the machine is Mr. J. B. West of
Rochester, N.Y. , for whom it has brought a reward
which few original geniuses live to enjoy The
owners of Australasian patents are West’s Patent
yre Setter Company, Limited, Haymarket, Sydney.*

POLITICAL RPOINTS
In only tentatively filling the Agent-
Generalship Premier Reid is keeping a big card
op his sleeve. You never can tell what may
happen.
Crick : “ From secrets confided to ladies,
the (N.S.W.) Govt, was by no means a happy
family.” Crick, then, is in the confidence of the
ladies ? Aha !
G. H. Reid is the twelfth Prime Minister
N.S.W. has had in 41 years of Responsible Govern-
ment. If Lyne consummates his ambition he will
■trike the unlucky number.
THE BUDGET BUBBLE.
R IP VAN WINHLE DIBBS : “Just you see me let
the wind out of it !”
Ministerial, “crises” seem to visit Aus-
tralia in epidemics. Simultaneous trouble has
just been encountered by the N.S.W., Maoriland,
S.A., and Sleep-a-lot Ministries.
Ed. Bulletin, — Mr. Christie didn’t make
the most stupendous failure during the recent
Vic. elections. Mr. Titus Wallis Farmer is most
certainly entitled to the distinction : he polled but
49 against two other candidates out of a roll of
2384 for Villiers and Heytesbury.— Fair Play.
The departmental answers to questions in
N.S.W. Parliament have grown to be generally so
ihcomplete and evasive that a small group of
members have determined on some near occasion
to check dust-throwing officialdom by moving that
the answer gi veil be referred back for amendment.
The motion will go with wings.
A big new daily for Brisbane is said to be
on the stocks. The necessary linotypes, it is
alleged, are already on the water. The affair ap-
pears to have been engineered by a prominent
Bananaland politician now abroad. Politics—
mainly of aforesaid politician’s peculiar brand
with a strong anti-TuLEGRAPH bias.
The Age and the Argus are now fighting
for the denarii of the bourgeoisie. It doesn’t ap-
pear much in their pages, but is there all the
same. As soon as Syme lets out the gathers and
gussets a bit, and patronises Labor, some of his
coppers get too hot to hold. In other words, the
Shopkeepery won’t have it. A good word for
Tillett, out loud, would have meant the smash of
twenty thousand circulation.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne is shock-
ingly artless in his grabs after office. For two
nights the N.S.W. Labor-party dodged the neces-
sity for tackling Mines Minister Sydney Smith on
the question of his Lucknow-strike bungle. On
the third night Lyne impatiently tabled his
motion of censure, whereas the obvious move was
to wait for the Laborists to bite first. Then he
would have had them under the whip.
Newsagents declare that the revolt against
the Age in North Melbourne since the elections is
most emphatic, and Argus sales boom. D. Syme
meets the agitation with a declaration in fat,
black letters that his circulation is four times
bigger than the other fellow’s. Ex-M.P. Prender-
gast recommends the Argus on the plea that an
open enemy is better (han a false friend, but this
is playing with fire. The Argus that is first en-
dured may eventually be embraced.
The election - programme of N.S. Wales
National Ass :
o Land-taxation only through local government, and
reconsideration of the income-tax.
b A reasonable customs-tariff, for revenue purposes.
c Economy in public expenditure.
e Amendment of the mining laws “ in such a manner as
to remove the existing discouragements to the invest-
ment of capital in mining enterprise.”
Which, saying the first clause of the first item, is
all indefinite enough to cover any political villainy
you like to conceive under any one of the head-
ings.
N.S.W. Govt, will, one of these days,
announce the dropping of the title “ Colonial
Secretary ’’ and the substitution therefor of Chief
Secretarj 7 , as in Victoria and Queensland. There
were but three Colonial Secretaries, properly so
called, in Australian history—Frederick Goulburn,
Alexander Macleay, and E. Deas Thomson.
These were subject to the Imperial Colonial Office,
and were in direct communication with the Princi-
pal Secretary of State in London. In those days
the title had fitness, but to-day it is an absolute
misnomer, defensible on no ground, and deserving
extinction if only because of its associations.
The Q. Govt, evidently doesn't want its
Land Board to be an impartial body. Its new
Land Bill provides that the Minister may order
the Board to re-hear any or every case without
giving reasons. When the Minister orders the
Board to re-hear a case he thereby distinctly tells
the Board that he isn’t satisfied with its decision,
and wants the verdict given for the other fellow—
probably his political supporter. And as the
Minister has power to sack the Board if it doesn’t
take this broad hint, it probably will take the
hint. If there is any appeal it should be openly
to the Supreme Court, not in secret to a politician
who has political supporters to placate.
H. : “ Let us found a jeremiad upon poor
Graham Berry. The only career worth having is
that which embraces a good business and a good
home, which are the things a politician makes
ducks and drakes of in his craving for the ‘ insane
root ’ (notoriety) that takes the reason prisoner.
Suppose Berry had stuck to the grocery, he pro-
bably would have enjoyed a happy life and
swarmed up to a place among Melbourne’s Dal-
getys, Grices, and others who have really sucked
the orange for all it is worth. But, no ! —mad man
must go neglecting his legitimate business and
family, missing all that makes life worth living,
* the applause of listening senates to command.’ ”
“Billy” Maloney, the big-hearted little
medico who represents the Labor Party of West
Melbourne in Vic. Parliament, is a real Eyetalian.
At a recent meeting he denounced Turner as a
“cowardly our,” Isaacs as a “thing,” and the
Vic. Ministry generally as persons abhorrent,
while he several times called on “ the holy name
of God ” and ‘ ‘ Almighty God ” for assistance, and
concluded by dramatically setting alight to a copy
of the Age with the remark. “ Boys, we will light
such a spark of freedom to-night as will not be
put out until the whole colony is ablaze.” The
cool-headed Trenwith, who was present, here
chipped in with the observation that he did not
believe in such “theatrical rubbish,” and the rest
of the proceedings consisted of the said Trenwith
being denounced as “a traitor to the workers’
cause.”
A QTIET DAY NEAR WALLUMBILLA, QUEENSLAND.—A SCRATCH PULLING MATCH.
A man walked from somewhere in Vic-
toria into an Albury public-house, a few Sundays
ago, and was served with liquor on the ground of
being a traveller. In the police-court, the man’s
statement that he had walked ten miles was not
impugned, but the publican and he were fined on
the ground that he hadn’t walked ten miles in N.S.
Wales, the Bench Solon being sure N.S.W. law-
makers meant that! Let us yell for Federation.
He walked the ten miles in Australia. That was
enough.
N.S. W. Agents-General under Responsible
Govt, have been W. C. Mayne, 0. Cowper, Wm.
Forster, and Saul Samuel, with J. P. Abbott in
the succession. Of those named, two were ex-
Premiers and three ex-Ministers of inferior rank,
which makes it appear that the post is regarded as
an ex-Ministerial perquisite.
ON THE PERFUMED
YARRA.
MRS OTOOLE: “Share, captain, dear, fwhat are thim 'dead
slows’ that shmell so bad?”
Fogarty, M.P., who lately moved the
abolition of Q. Parliamentary refreshment-room,
and, on his defeat, went to that self-same temple
and poured out libations to the priesthood of Self,
has raised his voice against Australian Federation :
The Australias, while a part and parcel of the British
Empire, have the British flag to defend them, and it is
not necessary to raise the question of federal defence.
Foxton’s new Q. Land Bill has got fine old
Tory patches in it. Lessees in settled districts are
to have priority of selection, on the principle that
“ to him that hath shall be given more even than
that he hath.” Also, in any part of the country
which the Minister, or Land Board, or one moved
by the other, or t’other moved by the one, or both
moved by each, shall choose to imagine is not
likely to be required for closer settlement for a
while, leases for ten years in “settled districts”
may be granted.
The Bible-League agents caused the har-
mony existing between minister and congregation
to be displaced by a most unfriendly feeling. They
were doing an immense amount of harm in the
land. It is not the business of the State to teach
religion—it is the business of the churches.” — Rev.
A. Rivett, to Melbourne Congregational Union.
In still another case, that
of the Kempsey bridge, the N.S.
Wales Govt, has refused to ac-
cept the lowest tender because
it came from Melbourne, and
gave the contract to a local man
at =£325 higher price. This by
way of encouraging local indus-
try, and artificially coddling the
cornstalk contracting business at
the cost of the taxpayer, and
doing all the other things which
Premier Reid denounces with
such a heart-rending shriek every
time the fiscal ghost gets on the
scene.
The Maoriland Govt, seeks
to make 8 per cent, the maxi-
mum rate of interest on mort-
gages, and 10 per cent, the maxi-
mum on I.O.U’s, bills of sale,
promissory-notes, and all other
loans. Consequently, the air is
full of the wild, blue shriek of
Fat Man about the impossibility
of regulating matters of private
contract, and the like. Fat Man
has proved at different times the
impossibility of regulating the
hours of child-labor, of fixing the
age below which children shall
not work in factories, &c., of
compulsory education, of sani-
tary legislation, of State bank-
ing, of State note-issues, of State
land-banks —of everything, in
fact, which interfered with the
blessed monopolies of Fat. And he has proved
an unreliable Jeremiah every time.
Barton, when N.S.W. Acting-Premier :
“ I hope I’ll never have a hand in appointing an-
other J.P. on the present system. It’s a disgrace
to the country,” Premier Reid, two years ago:
“ The present practice of appointing J’sP is a
political invention as little creditable to the party
that uses it as the parties on whom the title is
conferred.” Col. Sec. Brunker, twelve months
ago : “ The most disagreeable part of my work ;
I hope never to have to touch another list.” The
same Brunker, a week ago, in the presence of Reid
and with a claque of “ hear, hears,” replied to
Affleok, M.P. : “The Government have no inten-
tion of changing the method of appointing J’sP.”
The “disgrace ” seems to pay.
The unregenerate Copeland is the only
man in N.S.W. Assembly with courage enough to
bring in a bill to legalise Sunday trading. It
would be interesting to know what kind of a
chance Copeland thinks he has got. The average
M.L.A. doesn’t want to vote for the abolition of
the Sunday-closing law, and thus lose the snuffle-
busting vote, and he doesn’t want to demand the
enforcement of the same law and lose the soft-
tack-and-banana vote. It suits him very well to
be solid with the pious crowd by keeping the
existing law on the statute-book, and with the
impious crowd by letting it remain a dead-letter.
Besides, nobody will thank him much for legalis-
ing the Sunday banana, which couldn’t get on
very much better than it does now. Copeland has
logic and honesty on his side by demanding a
straight-out vote on the subject—but, all the same,
the average member will be unavoidably absent
that day getting his back tooth stopped.
He never learnt no fighting,
And not becos ’twas wrong,
For this was all his skiting,
And this was all his song:
“ I’ve got no need for slugging,
Yer see, I’ve got the pull;
If any cove starts plugging,
There’s my big brother Bull!"
His little song was chanted ;
But when, I do declare,
Big brother Bull was wanted,
Big brother Bull warn’t there !
So take this intimation
As how the matter stands—
Let ev’ry man and nation
Learn how to “ use their hands.”

Public Notices.
PUBLIC SERVICE BOARD.
50 Young-street,
Sydney, 29th October, 1897.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of Candidates for
JUNIOR CLERKSHIPS in the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney and at various country
centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to suit the
convenience of candidates, on the 20th DECEMBER next,
and four following days. The estimated number of
appointments to be made is 25. Candidates must be not
less than 16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day
of examination; of good moral character, and in sound
health. Persons already employed in the General Division
of the Public Service will be admitted to the examination,
provided their age does not exceed thirty years.
The subjects in which the candidates will be examined
are Handwriting, English (dictation precis writing,
grammar, and composition), Arithmetic (full course),
Geometry (Euclid, First Book), outlines of Physical and
Political Geography, and the Geography of the Austral-
asian colonies in moderate detail; English History from
the middle of the eighteenth century to the present time,
especially with reference to the colonies and depen-
dencies. Candidates will also be allowed to take any
two, but not more than two, of the following subjects :
Mathematics, English—a prescribed author—(the same ag
that prescribed for the Junior Examination of the current
year—Tennyson’s “ Gareth and Lynette ” (MacMillan),
Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and
Elementary Geology. Application to attend the examin-
ation must be made on a form for the purpose, which can
be obtained at the office of the Board, and must reach
the undersigned not later than the Ist December, 1897.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee
of 10s., which must be lodged with a clerk of petty'sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to
the Secretary of the Board with the form of application;
A list of registered candidates, with centres of examina-
tion, will be published in the Gov ernment Gazette after _
the time for the receipt of applications has expired, and
candidates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined. The regulations and copies of the
papers set at the last examination can be obtained at the
Government Printing Office and the principal booksellers.
By order of the Board.
9a31 J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

“ Don Carlos means to be president and
then King of Spain as soon as Cuba is pacified or
lost —the latter is almost a certainty,” writes Thh
Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ According
to Lord Ashburnham, the leading English Carlist,
the Spanish pretender fears to damage his prestige
by taking the reins only to lose a bit of Spanish
territory, i. e. , Cuba. For 20 years the Carlista
have been steadily organising, and the aged Don
feels certain that they are now so strong that
there will be little or no fighting over the ejection
and replacement. The loss of Cuba will, it is ex-
pected, cause a revolution in Madrid, and this will
be his opportunity to come forward and promise
the retention of the Phillippines—where the insur-
rection is but in its birth state —and Spain’s re-

The Red Bird Wilson chain, when in conjunction
with our cycloidal sprocket, makes a perfect-running
gear. The chain wheel has raised centre, which ob-
tains a half-inch pull on an inch pitch chain.
They are made with the only practical hardened
rivet which is proof against wear ; unlike the roller
pattern, they do not stretch. When you buy a cycle,
select one with a chain which has the greatest speed
and does not require to be replaced every few months.
Tub Red Bird Wilson chain is the chain with the
half-inch pull on an inch pitch sprocket.
The world’s mile record in competition record is
held by Martin on this chain ; time, 2min., unpaced.
This chain also won the great Austral Wheel Race in
record time.
We have been asked what are the little oil-bath
bearings? They are the only perfect bearings for
bicycles, and contain continuous oil-bath in which the
balls run. The advantage of this style of bearing
over the old can be well understood in a country
where grit and dirt can be picked up in any quantity
twelve months in the year. The little oil bearings do
not require to be cleaned ; they are always right.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Saturday’s racing gave Clarion the best of Melb.
Cup chances “on paper.” The son of Carbine and Clare
showed himself to be all right by running away with the
Maiden Plate. Presently Metford, who finished behind
Clarion in the A.J.C. Derby, beat a pretty good field for
the Hotham Handicap, after which Amberite won the
blue ribbon easily, and was reckoned to have a great
chance for the Cup. Clarion had run second to Amberite
in the A.J.C. Derby, and was to meet his conqueror on
241 b. better terms in the Cup. Backers who considered
Clarion’s “ form ” and breeding were bound to like him
for the Cup, although'a 6st. 81b. boy is not usually a
prudent pilot in two-mile handicaps.
* * • *
The Derby-Day crowd at Flemington looked
larger, in the Grand Stand enclosure, than that of any
previous year since ’9l, whereas the Flat was seemingly
less densely populated than usual. Visitors from afar
provide a considerable percentage of the Stand takings.
Many of these are ex-Victorians who come from the West
on a holiday gambling trip, but the majority represent
N.S.W., Barianaland, S.A. and Tassy, and have only a
Cup connection with Melbourne. Therefore the infer-
ence that local enthusiasm for racing diminishes, whilst
the “ Carnival ” meeting becomes a stronger all-round
attraction than ever. Federation is in the air.
All of the six events oil Derby-Day were cap-
tured by foreign trained horses, though one of the winners
—Metford—has a Victorian owner. Out of 18 horses
placed first, second, or third in those six events, only jive
were trained in Vic. It is not surprising that the local
faith in local “ tips” should be shaken to its foundation.
Much suburban racing threatens to rob the Vic. thorough-
bred of his speed, by getting him so accustomed to a
“ stiff” run that he can’t gallop when the money’s right.
Amberite’s Derby —only half a second slower
than record time —was a true-run race, barring the foolish
mishap which caused Aurum to drop back, apparently
beaten, at the nine-furlong point or thereabouts. There
were but six horses going, and yet the favorite, seemingly
ridden on the reckless system that usually obtains in
Australian circular-course racing, came to blows with
Eiridsdale, and undoubtedly suffered from the scrim-
mage. Later on there was no apparent reason why
Aurum shouldn’t have won had he been a great
horse. The writer reckons that Amberite scored on
his solid merits, for he pulled up fresher than
his rival, who was somewhat, distressed. Neverthe-
less, the beaten favorite, with the fetlock of his near hind
leg bleeding freely, might have owed his distress to the
accident aforesaid. Ere this paragraph appears the Cup
has settled Aurum-Amberite discussion for the present,
and if Mr. Wilson's colt, handicapped by a split fore-hoof
and not improved by a wound in the foot behind it, could
beat the son of Carbine over two miles—then the writer
is very .much surprised.
The finish for the Melbourne Stakes on Satur-
day was full of incident. Firstly, the sudden collapse of
Delaware (broken down, it was said) when tackled by
Wait-a-bit. Then the taking-down of Wait-a-bit by
Coil, who had the race won until Battalion came, with the
latest of rushes, and beat him on the post by a nose.
Meantime, little Key was also making an unexpected rush
that lafnded her into third place.
Wait-a-bit, on Saturday, seemed once again to
show a lack of heart, or want of sprinting power. But
this opinion (printed before the Melb. Cup race) may be
brought into ridicule by the 2-mile event. The writer
confessed himself “off” Wait-a-bit a fortnight ago,
owing to the horse’s “ rotten ” performance in the Caul-
field Stakes, at which time he was palpably out of sorts.
What was wrong with Wait-a-bit on October 9 that he
should then run about 3st. below his “ form ” of October
30? Such startling improvements within the short space
of three weeks make horse-reporting the hollowest of
mockeries. The present writer was not the only one de-
luded in this matter. The Argus said of the Caulfield
Stakes: "There seemed to be no excuse for Wait-a-bit,
who finished almost as slow as a tortoise; but Amberite
never tried a yard.” The C.S. is a nine-furlong flutter,
and the “tortoise” was travelling like a hurricane at the
end of nine furlongs in the Melb. Stakes.
Positano, much less fleshy than when he ran at
Sydney, last Sept., cut a poor figure in the Melb. Stakes,
and the appearance of the handsome, snorting British
animal before and after that race put his Cup - backers
rather out of conceit with their investment. There seems
to be something unmentionable the matter with Positano.
The Maribyrnong Plate was probably won by
Lady Most.yn’s jockey rather than by the mare. She was
driven along close to the Lawn railings, and, with the
advantage of a clear run on the hard, untrodden turf, she
finished lengths in front of the others, who were mobbed
together on the opposite (and worn) side of the course.
Jockeys are the most conservative of imps. Because
they are accustomed to stick to the inside rails in run-
ning round the track, they prefer the inside even when
they have a perfectly straight course before them and
should be glad of a ohance to spread the field. Whilst
Vigorous and Essex, in close company, were having a
desperate struggle at the head of the mob, Lady Mostyn
was comfortably making her way to the judge’s box,
alone and unperceived by nine-tenths of the people m
the Stand enclosure.
* * • *
In 39 Y.R.C. Derbys the winner’s name has
only twice commenced with A — viz., Angler, in 1865, and
Amberite. A crowd will say, “ What about Admiral and
Australian Peer?” But both had a “The” in front, so
began with T, and ’Arvester doesn’t count for the same
reason. There’s not a single A among the sires of V.R.O.
Derby winners.
• • • «
The victory of Gaulus —with Grafter, another
Gozo horse, second ! —Aurum third —in Melb. Cup, gives
just the kind of advt., long needed, to boom the sterling
Gozo blood —so much fancied by that Sydney coterie.
Now, the value of Gozo horseflesh will go up with a rush.
* » * *
“ English stewards have a peculiar way of treat-
ing horseowners even suspected of ‘ hanky-panky,’ as the
Westralian exploiter has realised,” writes Tub Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “In June last his horse Bride-
groom was a hot 6-to-l favorite for the Jubilee Stakes, and
Clwyd, his stable-companion, a 50-to-l shot. At the last
moment the former was scratched, and the latter started
at s’s and won 'midst savage hooting. Then the handi-
cappers got a hint. Henceforward Clwvd carried 9st. or
more, until Calvert, recognising the position, sold it to
Mrs. Langtry for 1600 guineas. The first entry of the
lady owner—the Cambridgeshire—and Clwyd’s weight
went down to 7st. 91b.—an unmistakeable sign of what
the authorities' past action meant.”

* * * *
Giffen has just put forth the opening instalment
of his autobiog., in Melb. Sportsman. London publishers
will issue the whole book. Gif. (perhaps assisted by a
ghost) entertains in an easy style. Yet it is a job which
might rather be done by “ Felix ” Horan, of the Austra-
lasian, incomparably the best talky cricket penman that
has ever appeared, notwithstanding his weakness for the
Billv-Tommy-Sammy style of prattle.
* * * •
Giffen, in his autobiography, shows that his
course was by no means a track of roses. To be sure, he
drew great attention as a lad of 17, like the Vic. bowler
Allan, but he was most unlucky in the first season, when
his boyish promise had caused him to be pushed to the
front. He says that, but for uncommon forbearance, he
must have been relegated to those who make a coltish
spurt and all is over. The first time he was called upon
to do something great be made a pair of spectacles, while
his bowling failed utterly too. He recalls, as the happiest
moment of his life, when he was allowed, as a lad, to bowl
to W. G. Gr ce, at practice, in Adelaide.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin, —I am indeed glad to see that
somebody has backbone enough to tackle the Australian
Cricket Council on its own dunghill, and I think that
Giffen should have the firmest support from all his fellow-
players. Of course we all know that the last Australian
Eleven had a decided breeze with the A.C.C., but that was
by cable, and the council swallowed the pill. Crack
cricketers should all remember Turner’s career; what a
sterling player he was, and, after devoting the best years
of his life to the game, what did he profit bv it? With
the prospects of immense attendances now is the time for
the players to reconstruct the financial conditions under
which they play, and if they don’t upset the A.C.C. now
they never will. The gods of crieketdom, Major
Wardill and Mr. Phil Sheridan, helped to make a very
fine hash of the last Australian Eleven, and if it is true
that the Major said Turner ought to play in place of
M'Kibbin in the coming test-matches, he ought to have
gone on to the cemetery and dug up a few more of our
old friends.—Pluggbr.
* • * •
Stoddart has brought a nuggetty team this
time, if appearances are anything, and they impress local
critics as being fit and strong enough to play all day and
travel all night (writes a Bulletin s correspondent” from
Adelaide). Hirst, Wainwright, Hayward, Hearne, and
Board, of the new men, are splendidly-developed athletes.
Druce, Mason, and Ranji., the two former overgrown,
and the latter puny, are the exceptions judging by
appearances. Stoddart himself looks stout and well, but
fears a weak knee, which, by the way, kept him out of
the latter end of the English season.
At the practice-nets (this was written before
the match) Ranji. greatly impresses in two ways. First,
he is an artist at wristy strokes, leg-glancing and cutting
being his specialities. But, secondly, he looks an easy
victim for a leg-before-wicket decision. Time and time
again he puts his left leg right in front of the wicket to a
ball straight on the leg and middle stumps. If the
Prince misses the leg glance he is out every time. But
those who know Ranji. smile and say that the batsman,
when in form, doesn’t miss ’em and the hopeful bowler
suffers.
Stoddart’s team will probably turn out to be
strong In all-round batting, but weak in bowling.
Richardson excepted ; the bowlers, bar Hirst, do not im-
press. Hearne will likely only succeed on poor wickets.
Wainwright and Hayward are better batsmen than
bowlers,, and Mason is only up-and-down. Hirst is a
really good bowler, with an ugly leg-break ; and Stoddart
will probably rely on the Yorkshireman as Richardson’s
main support.
Ranjitsinhji is delighted with Australian
wickets, taking a clue from Adelaide. He says the Ade-
laide wickets are unequalled in England, and are delight-
fully true and fast. “ 1 like them ten times better than
English wickets,” he says, “ and 1 think they will suit me,
especially when the weather becomes warmer.”
Hayward impresses the writer as being the
soundest bat in Stoddart’s crowd, and is as likely to make
runs as any of them. He is far and away the man of the
new men in every department. Hirst, Board and Storer
are hitters, and ought to do well on the fast wickets.
Long Tom Richardson looks well and fit, but he does nob
seem so fast with the ball as on the last trip. Summed
up, Stoddart has a good all-round team, strong in batting,
but in bowling—well, doubtful. As with the last team,
the trump-card is Richardson. Hirst may prove to be
the Bobby Peel of this team ; if not, then there may be
money in backing Australia.
* * * •
The Adelaide match ended unsatisfactorily in a
draw, after three and a-half days’ play, when the Eng-
lishmen had to move on to fill their Melb. dates. So far
as play went, the sides seemed pretty evenly matched ;
though the Englishmen had to do without Stoddart, who
was too ill to bat. S.A. made 408 in Ist innings, and 187
for 5 wickets in second ; England, for Ist only, knocked
up 475. In batting young Clem Hill carried off the
honors with a 200 lst-innings score, playing very soundly.
Ranji gave a taste of his quality, and took
credit for 189, offering a good many chances.
The prince, however, was not in good feather;
and nearly all of his side complained that they
were not “ fit ”to play. Jones, with 7 for 190, got great
bowling kudos; but the Englishmen’s umpire, Phillips
(who umpired the last ’Stralian Eleven in England) after
cautioning Jones on the second day, no-balled him on the
third, and he had to bring his pace down. The English-
men mostly relied on Hearne and Richardson with the
ball ; and in the first S.A. innings former got 3 for 86 and
latter 5 for 120.

pad it with the required bank-notes each game.

in Adelaide. Perhaps there is something in the whisper
that it is hard to tell which are pros, and which amateurs
in this team.

* * * *
League of Vic. Wheelmen has had to back
down before the country clubs over the matter of Xmas
Day racing. For years past Xmas Day and Good Friday
have been the big cycling days of Ballarat and Bendigo,
yet a few of the League pietists calmly ignored the vested
interests of these cities and prohibited any more such
meetings. A big row followed, with the result that the
League got scared and hastily repealed its law, at any
rate for the time being.
* * * «
Cyclist Barden having claimed the World’s One-
mile Handicap competition-record, a correspondent writes
to the Age, “ in justice to Australia,” that the W.O M.H.-
record was made on 17th Feb., at Adelaide Exhibition
Oval, in 2min. 2sec. (3 l-ssec. quicker than Barden), by
W. Martin, riding on somebody's tyres. “In justice to
Australia”!'.) W. Martin !
, * * *
The irony of fate was never more strongly in
evidence than in the case of a young man who came up to
Sydney from the Islands for his health. He seemed to be
improving rapidly when he developed pneumonia and
died suddenly. Two days after his funeral a ticket which
he had bought in a lottery drew a prize of £BOOO.
* * * *
The W. A. Rowing Club has taken to the rowing-
club-race-sweep industry with a vengeance. For Melb.
Cup, upon a £250 (Is.) subscription, the club offers £125
in prizes ! Half the subscription for expenses is pretty
“ steep,” even fora business-like institution that promptly
sues for unpaid yearly subscriptions, and won’t accept the
resignation of any member whose belongings are worth
distraint for the amount.
* * * *
“ Jack ” Parsons, the ex-champion Vic. cyclist,
was operated on last Sunday week by Dr. Plevna Ryan,
who found that the one-time god of the Vic. public could
not have lived more than two years, with the prospect of
sudden death at any time, had he stood off the operation.
As it is, Parsons is promised good, reliable health, with a
probable return to his old form.
A M.L. correspondent to The Bulletin :
“ A. H. Fisher, the young Dunedin bowler who has been
invited to visit Australia to practise in view of his in-
clusion in one of the test matches, is a left-hander who
has bowled for his province with great success for the last
four years against all classes of batsmen and on all kinds
of wickets. Singularly enough, the better the batsmen
opposed to him the better he seems to bowl, as witness
his great success against the Queensland, N.S. Wales (the
last two) and Australian teams that have visited us of re-
cent years. In Australia he will bowl on faster and truer
wickets than he has been accustomed to, but great confi-
dence is expressed here of his ability to succeed. He has
plenty of pluck and bowls with great judgment; in fact,
all his best wickets secured against visiting batsmen have
been the result of beating his men. His natural style of
bowling, with plenty of pace and command of the ball,
make him a troublesome bowler. He can break a little
either way—just enough to beat the bat —but depends for
his success chiefly upon his sudden change in pace and
pitch. Is a fair bat, who can hit hard and score fast when
once set: is also a fine field.”
* * * ■*
“ Albert Trott, who goes to South Africa with
O’Halloran for the English winter, has had a marvellously
successful season,” writes Tub Bulletin's London corres-
pondent. “ He heads the English bowling averages with
50 wickets at 13'84 each (for first-class cricket only, of
course). In minor matches, however, he has taken an
additional 200 wickets at a little over 6 runs apiece. In
his last game against a suburban 15 he took 11 for 18 and
made top score, 72. Middlesex are jubilant over his
wonderful improvement, and expect to do great things
with his aid next season. There is every possibility, if he
keeps up his present form, that Trott will be the best all-
round cricketer of ’9B in England.”
• • • *
Two veteran cricketers —Harry Trott and Jack
Worrall—nearly came to blows the other Saturday on a
question of stump-drawing in the South Melb. v. Carlton
match. As a preservative of youthful enthusiasm cricket
is a great game. The professors never grow old and
common-sensical. Look at George Giffen and his “ reso-
lution ” not to play in big matches this season ! _ He
intended it to shake the little world in which he lives,
moves, and has his being. And his little world was
shaken—though not as severely as George had expected.
• • • •
Unusual cricket incident. At St. Kilda (Melb.),
on a recent Saturday, N. Melbourne were batting against
the locals. The ground was wet, and a St. Hilda man
intercepted a ball which had just been fielded, and pro-
ceeded to dry it with his handkerchief before passing it
on to the bowler. Veteran “ Dick ” Houston, can tain of
the North, appealed (probably on the spur of the moment),
and the umpire promptly decided to add five runs to the
North’s score ! This was stretching rule 41 ad absurdum,
with a vengeance. Bowler Robertson had 25 runs hit off
him for five wickets, but his analysis reads five for 30 I
• • • *
It is noticed that Platt-Betts never ventures on
the training-track when there are more than one or two
machines out. Cyclists assert that the record-breaker is
frightened of getting a spill—timidity which would, oI
course, aocount for his failure in man-to-man racing.
* * * *
The average Australian athlete, with his eye on
the £5OO-prize of the A.N.A. Wheel-race, would regard
with a snort of disgust the myrtle-branches won by Flack
at last year’s Olympian sports. These dried-up twigs
represent the 800 and 1500 metres championships, and,
framed as a picture, were presented to Flack's school
(Melb. Grammar), at the smoke-night of the old pupils in
Melb., last week.
* * * *
Despite the awful sameness of cycle races, the
fine day and good programme should have attracted
more people to N.S.W. Wheelmen’s Carnival on Saturday.
The programme turned out well ; the finishes were
actually exciting.
The Championship of Australasia brought out a
fine squad of athletes in the first heat, and the way Walne
left Megson in the finishing sprint, deserved all the plaudits
the winner received ; also Green’s long, determined sprint
in the seoond heat was altogether too hot for Payne,
Body, Meadham, and the rest. But the final was exhi-
bition racin Walne sprinted nearly the whole of the
last lap at his top, and simply spread-eagled the field.
Platt-Betts’ attempt to lower the record was
an awful frost. When the record-breaker did his pre-
liminary round the track everybody was saying, “Oh,
what limbs! What a big gear! What a pocket Her-
cules But when he finished his ride, with the result
that he did the five miles in 10.38 (Lesna’s time is 9.59),
everybody sang a different song. “What did I tell you'
I knew the little podge wouldn’t do it.”
The Sydney Wheel Race, with its first prize
of £2OO, brought out some sensational riding. Elliott
won his heat by mere judgment; Megson won his by
beating pacer Hunt home. Prowse sprinted like Bil-
martin down the straight to snatch first place.
* » » •
In June last The Bulletin passed a remark
upon a Tasmanian law-suit, touching the disqualification
of a horse known by the poetic name of Gabby. The case
was simple enough on its surface. Gabby, last year, won
a race at one of the Brighton (Tas.) Turf Club meetings,
and was objected to on the allegation that he had run
inside, instead of outside, a certain boundary-post. Pro-
test dismissed. An appeal by the owner of the second
horse was informally lodged, and subsequently abandoned.
Nevertheless, Gabby’s owner couldn t get the stakes from
the club. After repeated applications, he took out a
summons, whereupon the stewards, or some of them, held
a hasty meeting, and decided to disqualify Gabby (who
had won other races in the meantime) for 12 months;
likewise the jockey was wiped out for two years. None
of the Gabbyites were called upon to defend themselves
at this sudden enquiry, yet the result went along in the
usual way to the Tas. Racing Club, which august tribunal
confirmed the sentences passed by the stewards of the
subordinate club. Gabby being now a “goner” for
immediate racing purposes, his owner brought an action
for damages against ttie Brighton Turf Club. The Judge,
in summing up, stated that the defendant stewards
had committed a “stupid, ass-like blunder,” but inas-
much as stupidity sans palpable malice is permissible in
racing law, the jury verdieted in favor of the stupid ones,
giving them the benefit of the doubt as regards malice.
The Judge could merely recommend the Tas. Racing Club
to remove Gabby’s disqualification. Months went on, and
the ruling powers ignored the Judge’s advice by doing
nothing in this little matter. When the Gabby party
asked for the apparent wrong to be righted they were
referred to the Brighton Racing Club for redress in the
first instance. The B.R.C. (a syndicate of five) was by
this time defunct. The Gabby party protested against
the absurdity of being referred to a non-exist<nt. corpora-
tion, but the Tas. Racing Club has stuck to its resolve tc
do nothing, except at the instigation of the B.R.C.’s
silent corpse, and so the Gabby scandal stands, and is likely
to stand evermore. It seems only a small scandal, from
the financial point of view, but as a suggestion of urgent
necessity for reforms in Tassy racing government its
length, breadth and depth are considerable.

failure in its first result. But any system of cycling
championships would be ridiculous in Australia, ai
AN ANNUAL INFLICTION.
The race is run, my “ cert.” is done -
They've “ done him like a dinner,”
And gloomily I try to shun
The pal who backed the winner.
But all in vain, with joy insane,
Amidst the ringmen’s babel,
He breathes the whisper once a.-aiu s
“ I got it from the stable.”
O, woe is me ! to think that he
Did get it from the stable.
Bill’s beaming smile disturbs my bile;
At other times I like him ;
But now he seems so mean and vile,
I’ve half a mind to strike him.
I feel the pain that prompted Cain
To murder brother Abel,
When comes the whisper once again 1
“I got it from the stable.”

The flight-flour Committee, having ob-
tained the permission of Major-General French,
have decided to hold a Naval and Military Tourna-
ment irt the Agricultural Grounds on Nov. 9,
together with a sports meeting, including bicycle-
races. The programme is a varied one, and in-
cludes events of all description. The military
events have attracted entries from the country,
also a large number of the contingent from Eng-
land will compete. Stabling for horses will be
provided, and seating accommodation is now
available for thousands, who will be able to view
the sports from the new grandstand. The price
of admissioh is adults is., children 6d,*
; Iri Melb. last week an ex-Vic. school-
teacher charged a young lady (also an ex-school-
teacher) with perjury at an enquiry which resulted
in his dismissal. Defendant was accused, inter
Mia , of having stated “ that you did not put hops
down my back ” ; and complainant’s counsel ex-
plained that he had known the girl “only in a
purely scientific way ”
He was a man of culture, who studied metaphysics and
the occult sciences, and wished to see the duplicity of
female nature ill all its nakedness, and a flirtation, which
Was all on her side, commenced. In seeking information
he found this simple, very plain - looking girl to be
possessed of a very extraordinary turn of mind, and
desired to find Out whether her iikings were anything
tilore than a sink of deception.
Ah, these “ simple, very plain-looking girlß ! ” In

by complainant to defendant contained the follow-
ing—
Protestation of love, pure and inextinguishable. . . .
You are my ideal ; the mainspring of my life. Why do
you keep aloof from me ? When I place myself at your
feet, you plead for me. Is my head cracked or soft? I
will worship you always all the days. ... I worship
the ground you walk upon. You are a hard mistress;
you don’t give me a moment of liberty. Allow me a little
love for another person, you monopolist. The chains are

I'he Dunlop cycle Is manufactured by, and is
the sole property Of the Dunlop Cycle Co., Ltd., of
Coventry, which is a company specially formed for the
manufacture and marketing of these machines —which
have shown themselves in many tests to be the fastest
cycles in the world. One of its recent victories over
“ Father Time ” was the wonderful performance by Mr. J.
W. Stocks, who rode 32 miles 448yds. Upon a Dunlop
cycle within one hour.*

According to the official list there are five
different brands of Presbyterianism in N.S.W.,
most of them the outcome of secessions sprung
from the laughable and sometimes disgraceful bic-
kering of the holy men in Presbytery assembled.
Sydney City Council’s polo-like argument
over Surveyor Richards’ rubbish-mission to Eng-
land proves two things : first, that an influential
organisation can successfully nobble the bulk of
the Sydney press ; and, second, that this, when
achieved, is not quite the same thing as nobbling
the public.
S.A. Sheriff Boothby haa travelled on cir-
cuits for nearly 40 years. At one time he acted
as “whip” on the Southern circuit, a coach-trip
of over 600 miles to and fro. Boothby claims tp
have originated the Australian ballot-system.
Guy Boothby is his nephew, by the way, and not
his son.
Thus the London Tailor and Cutter :
H.R.H. the Duke of York, in Dublin, wore an exceed-
ingly stylish frock-coat, which bore evidence of skill and
taste both on the cutter’s and journeyman’s part. We
think it a pity H.R If. decided to have a breast pocket, as
it is apt to give the fore-part a rather crowded, overdone
appearance. As he has not yet attained to the propor-
tions of his father we do hot think the fore-part wide
enough to allow of this. The end of the pocket and the
top button were too near together. 11.R.H. wears his
vests rather to the long side, and leaves the bottom but-
ton unfas'i tied which gives an air of nonchalance that
modifies the stiffness too often surrounding a frock suit
of this class. The trousers were cut in the moderate
style, with perhaps a tendency to looseness.
Q. Worker prints this interesting prayer
with which Speaker Cowley opens every sitting of
Q. Leg. Assembly—for the last seven years the
most corrupt Assembly in Australia :
Most Gracious God, we humbly beseech Thee, as for
this colony in general, so especially for the Parliament of
Queensland under our most religious and gracious Queen
at this time assembled :
That thou wouldst be pleased to direct and prosper all
our consultations, to the advancement of Thy glory, the
good of Thy Church, the safety, honor, and welfare of
our sovereign and this portion of her dominions, that all
things may be so ordered and settled by our endeavors,
upon the best and surest foundations, that peace and
happiness, truth and justice, religion and piety, may be
established among us for all generations.
These and all other necessaries for us and Thy whole
Church, we humbly beg in the Name and Mediation of
Jesus Christ, our most blessed Lord and Saviour, Amen.
After that the Boodle ! Talk about Blasphemy !
They keep telling stories in Melbourne
about how David Syme, of the Age, dictates the
Government policy, and about the close friendship
between him and Premier Turner. Yet it is diffi-
cult to imagine Byrne and Turner as friends. One
of Turner’s great ambitions is to pay off the deficit
and pose as the financial saviour of Victoria.
Therefore his life is one long howl after revenue.
If the presumable millionaire Syme would peg out
this year the probate duty, added to that arising
from other recent deaths of wealthy citizens,
would probably give Turner illoo,ooo more than
he expected, and he would be the best and luckiest
Treasurer in the Seven Provinces. Therefore
Turner, being human, can hardly help hoping that
Syme may peg out, and it would be only natural
and patriotic for him to leave the door open on
the off-chance of the draught getting on Syme’s
spine. In a country where there are heavy pro-
bate duties an impecunious Treasurer, whose soul
is in his work, can’t hope that his wealthy
acquaintances are feeling well.

■will be held at the
Agricultural Grounds, Sydney,
ON
Prince of Wales’ Birthday, Nov. 9,
In Aid of the TRADES' HALL BUILDING FUND, for
PRIZES VALUE £.200.
Bicycle Eaces for Prizes value £4Blos.
INCLUDING
RESCUE RACE ON BICYCLES.
To be run under the Rules of the New South Wales
Cyclists’ Union.
Foot Races, All-Comers.
One Mile Flat Race.
Professional & Novice Handicap (150yds.)
And Obstacle Race,
for Frizes value £23 10s.
The Naval and Military Events include Jumping
Competitions on Horseback, Wrestling on Horseback,
Victoria Cross Race, Lemon Cutting, Cleaving Turk’s
Head, Tent Pegging, Tilting at the Ring, Balaclava melee,
Wantage Race, Naval Field Gun Dismounting, Cutlass
Exercise, First Aid Competition, Manual and Firing Exer-
cise, Bayonet Exercise, Tug-of-War, and Events for Mem-
bers of the Public School Cadet Forces.
The above events have attracted entries from the coun-
try districts, and also a large number of the Contingent
from England.
Accommodation for horses will be provided on the
grounds.
Vehicles will be admitted and stabling for horses will
be provided.
The Agricultural Grounds have, under the supervision
of Mr. F. Webster, been greatly improved, additional
terraces and a new grandstand having been built, and
every person present will have an uninterrupted view of
the sports.

WALES OF THE CONVICT SYSTEM, by
L Price Warung ; Is. 3d. posted.
Meteorologist Wragge waxes indignant at
the finical crew who carp at the poetical outbursts
in his official weather-bulletins. Clement pro-
fesses to see “idylls and stanzas in the rustling’ of
the wheat-fields on the Downs,” &c., &e. Further-
more, the rampant Wragge threatens to couch his
notices of phenomena in isobars, isotherms, iso-
brontons and diabletons, if the Press doesn’t stop
its eternal poking of borak.
The, young girl pavement-trotter has become
very numerous of late in M.L. cities, and the
Government has drafted a bill for the appointment
of “ discreet women ” as inspectors, with extensive
powers to stop and interrogate the girl who is out
at a late hour. She is also authorised to escort her
home, and see her safely deposited on the parental
bosom, or, if the late girl is a very hard case, to
take her to an establishment specially provided,
and leave her in charge of the matron, pending
inquiry. This is, at least, more decent than the
scheme of last year, according to which the
girl who was out after 10 was to be interrogated
by two large, full-blooded policemen, and then, if
necessary, 1 d before an amorous male J.P., to be
affectionately hugged by him, at a late hour, in
the execution of his duty. The worst that is
likely to befall the. girl who is beginning to go
wrong, and who falls into the hands of a big,
hard-faced “discreet woman,” is to have her hair
pulled a good deal on the way home. But,
unless the discreet woman is an enormous
person, and well-armed, and two of her go
togetl er acoompawed by a reliable dog, there
is apparently nothing to prevent her being
kissed by beery male revellers when she is out “on
jooty ” at midnight's so’emn hour; the reveller
can’t be expected to see how ugly she is when he
meets her suddenly on a dark, wet night, or to
know that she isn’t only a larger Tottie than the
average. And the Maoriland climate is cold on
winter nights as well as wet, and unless the lady
is allowed to take a drop of gin with her when she
goes forth to capture the sodden midnight Flossie,
bronchitis seems almost inevitable. Moreover, in
the early stages of her career, before the public
gets used to the new order of t hings, she will have
a hard time. She must needs arrest and escort
home on suspicion many girls out on perfect y
innocent, errands, and when Michael Mulligan,
pick and-shovel-gentlcrnan, looks out of his
wooden cottage and finds that a strange woman has
brought his weeping daughter home by the collar
and wants to ask if she is q iite respectable,
the next act will probably be a discreet function-
ary in petticoats with a black eye falling back-
wards off the verandah. The Buu.etin has no
desire to say.anything against the new idea,
which is probably about the best thing that could
be devised under the circumstances, but it reckons
there are rough times ahead for somebody. The
matter is full of complications. For instance, the
new lady functionary can t always avoid being
good-looking, and then Policeman O’Toole will
assuredly want to mash her when they are on the
same “bate.” And if she doesn’t let him do it—
well, the next time she is trying to arrest and
lead home a large, desperate, fighting street-Tottie
she may whistle for assistance trl all is blue, but
officer O’Toole will be too busy after an unregis-
tered dog at the other end of his beat to hear
anything.
A Boon to Sufferers. —A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of sufft ring from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*
“I owe much to you, my dear friend,"for
strength in moments of weakness, for consolation
in severe trouble, for new lights of thought a-nent ,
the barrenness of life.”—Parkes to Duffy, 1864.
