Hori Grey’s Lone Hand.
[For the Bulletin.]
Throughout his career, the old warrior acted con-
sistently up to the idea that he had been dealt a
lone hand by nature, by Providence, by his
guiding star, by fate, or by the force which
changed the primeval jelly into the cell or sphere
and commenced the sequence of evolution which,
so fax’, has culminated in civilised man. Where-
ever it came from, he was always certain he had
it, and was calmly determined to play it on his
‘‘own.” We can talk thus about him during his
slowly waning life ; for, though autocratic, when
he considered the situation required it, to an
extent which earned for him the name of the
great Pro-Consul, his aims were always pure, and
the results satisfactory when the old boy was
allowed a "free hand. That is where his absolute-
ness differed from the autocracy of crowned
heads, and that is why we never hear the truth
about the latter, till tliey are not only dead but
very much decayed.
There was nothing of what the Maoris call
oho-rere about Hori Grey ; no rushing in where
angels would fear to tread, he didn’t care
whether the angel would fear to tread or not ;
he was there calmly and deliberately, when
he thought he was the man for the situation.
He studied his lone hand at every stage of the
game, and he consulted no one, colleague or out-
sider, as to what card he should play, unless
“ witli intent to deceive,” which was strictly diplo-
matic. The disappointment of feilow-Minister
vho had thus been shown the back of the full
hand (the truly liberal Montgomery) was said to
have been voiced as follows : “ Now if this old
man—this deep old man—does things too deep for
me—why, what a most particularly deep old man
this deep old man must be ! ”
* » * *
When, after esoteric deliberations, Hori had
made up his mind to a course of action, no amount
of pumping would extract from him a hint as to
the probable line of procedure ; his actions must
first speak. There are many amusing stories told
of the evasions he contrived, to prevent his inten-
tions being'known. It was impossible to “pull
hjs leg. ” He was perfectly unpumpable, for he
would not approach the remotest bounds of a
subject he wished to avoid. He not only
exercised strategic skill in getting rid of the
obnoxious subject; he contrived in a bare-
faced manner entirely to reverse the theme.
Instance his interview with General Cameron’s
aide-de-camp. ,
It is matter of history that the relations between
Sir George Grey, as Governor, and General
Cameron, as Commander-in-Cbief, were consider-
ably strained. Sanders, in his late!v-pub'ished
history, thus sums up the situation :—“ Hut,
vvorse than all, the power he had used so effec-
tively in 1840, as the Governor, the Premier, and
the Oommander-in-Chief, combined in one capable
man, was now allotted to Sir George Grey, to Mr.
Fox, and to General’Cameron, each of whom had
to be consulted and convinced before any impor-
tant step could be decided on.”
The General had battled his way through the
lower Waikato, and had dug a lot of more or less
useless saps before the stronghold at Rangiaohia.
He was putting in the time till he could gain
Grey’s consent to the assault, and he sent a letter
to the Governor by -the hand of Col. Pitt. On his
arrival in Auckland,-Pitt went direct to Govern-
ment House, and was shown into a room, where
Hori was sitting.
“Good day. Pitt. Fine day. Letter for me,
Pitt?”
Surprised that Grey-apparently knew his errand,
Pitt produced the missive.
“ Thank you, Pitt. Do you think Mrs. Whit-
taker a pretty woman, Pitt? ”
“ Really, Sir George, I have never considered
the point.”
“ That’s strange. What’s in this letter, Pitt?”
“I'm sure I cannot say, Sir George.”
“That’s as.it should be. But, tell me, Pitt,
didn’t you write it?”
“Well, Sir George, as secretary to General
Cameron, I think I may own that I am aware of
the contents, in a perfunctory manner.”
“Quite so, Pitt, quite so! And I think you
said you had not pondered over the beauty of
Mrs. Whittaker, even in a perfunctory manner,
you know, Pitt ? ” Pitt looked mighty uncomfort-
able. Hori followed up the question with another
more to die point:—
“By the way, Pitt, General Cameron wishes me
to authorise an assault on Rangiaohia, does he
not ? ”
Pitt respectfully referred Sir George to the
letter he was turning over and over in his hand.
But Hori Grey would not give a chance to face or
temper to indicate the sentiments with which he
would view the contents. So he thus closed the
interview: “Yes, a charming day, as you remark,
Pitt. By the way, which door did you come in
at ? ”
“ This one, Sir George.”
“ Just so, Pitt. Suppose you leave by this one ;
it will be a charming variety. There are some
lovely shrubs in the grounds. Good-bye, Pitt.
A pleasant ride, Pitt! So you don’t think Mrs.
Whittaker a pretty woman ! lam sorry for that!
Good-bye, good-bye !”
• * * *
A different man was Mailing, a pioneer of settle-
ment in the pre-colonial days ; an athlete equal to
Selwyti : a stalwart Maori-fighter, and subse-
quently a Judge of the Native Land Court, and
the author of that inexpressibly charming book
“ Old New Zealand—by A Pakeha Maori.” When
the germs of war were smouldering with a heat only
observable by the expert, Mailing held a confiden-
tial position in the Waikato, and things came to
his knowledge which he-thought should be com-
municated to the Governor. He rode in such
haste to Auckland, that when he reached Govern-
inent House his pants had succumbed to saddle-
friction. Now, Grey had exceptional means of
acquiring information on native affairs, probably
from both sides, and it is only on the supposition
that he was perfectly acquainted with the state of
native feeling that we can account for his recep-
tion of Maning. The latter found Grey in the
gardens devoted to the care of rare birds and mar-
supials. “Good morning, Maning; glad to see
you. • Charming day, is it not ? ”
“ Good morning, Sir George. Haven’t had
much time to notice the weather; I have hastened
to see you in my travel-worn state, to tell you of
the serious aspect of affairs in the Waikato. The
natives are not only sullen, but I am much in-
clined to think they will break out in open re-
bellion.”
“Indeed, Maning, that’s serious. But let me
ask you, whose opinion I value, what is your
idea with respect to a great Southern Continent?”
Maning was dumbfounded. The crisis was so
important that he could not believe he was being
trifled with. At length he replied, “Really, Sir
George, if I had any definite ideas on the subject,
the very serious business I have on hand prevents
me from explaining them at present. I can
assure you, Sir George, that I fear an outbreak
every day. ”
“ Just so; I appreciate your care'and attention.
But just look for a moment at the bird behind you,
the great Solomon Island Emu ; a bird evidently
allied in species with the moa, the apteryx, and
the kiwi, and yet separated in its native habitat of
to-day by thousands of miles of ocean from that
of the birds I have mentioned.”
“ Very true, Sir George ; but might I ask you
to give me vourattention for a few minutes, whilst
I explain my reasons for being alarmed.”
The Solomon Island emu stalked solemnly
around, unobtrusively curious about the visitor.
He was particularly attracted by the spot where
in Maning's clothing the saddle had wrought a
“ solution of continuity. ” Grey stood watching
the bird, and, as was his habit, occasionally stick-
ing the point of his walking-stick in the ground
and flicking it upwards with a sudden jerk. As
Maning made his last appeal for attention, Hori
turned courteously towards him: “You are very
good. Quite natural on your part, Maning.
But now, just look at that bird and ask yourself
again if he is not a direct link in the evidence
for the former existence of a great Southern con-
tinent?” To emphasise his words, he dug his
stick in the ground and flirted a pellet of clay into
the Solomon Islander’s eye, whereupon the bird,
hastily concluding that this strange assault must
come from the stranger thing he saw, made a
sudden onslaught with his beak upon the patch of
white in the tightest part of the pakeha-Maori’s
pants. Then Hori was the calmest of the trinity,
as Maning hurried away, damning Grey and the
emu in a strictly impartial manner.
* * • *
But, in all probability, Grey had already decided
how to play the lone hand, which he did to some
purpose when he jumped the claim of the military
commanders, and assaulted and took the Whareroa
Pa with a levy of colonial boys and friendly
natives. And there was no melodrama about
him ; he didn’t play to th.e gallery or seek indivi-
dual kudos. The unborn millions about which he
was continually chaffed were to him a reality
kept steadily in view, and Bentham's “greatest
happiness of the greatest number ” was improved
in his aspirations to include the minority.
Observe the immense vitality of the democratic
mind in a moribund body, displayed when he pain-
fully dragged himself to visit Dick Seddon, that
he might welcome one the Tory papers called
“ the Lancashire working-man.” Long may he
linger—we don’t want him to R.I.P. just yet.
Maoriland.
BORIN STUDHOLME.
Having consideration of the dingy, in-
fected police-court Bible, Vic. Crovvn Law Depart-
ment signifies by circular that in future persons
desiring to take the oath in the Scotch form are to
be permitted to do so. The Scotch invariably
take it without sugar.
Richardson, W.A. ex-Minister of Lands,
last year sent out a surveyor to look for rabbits in
the S.E. The explorer found them all right, near
Twilight Cove, and recommended that Govt,
should import thousands of cats and let ’em loose
in that dismal territory round Eucla to prey on
bunny. As if Westralia hadn’t enough wild cats
already !
Writes a Melbourne correspondent :
It is to be feared that The Bulletin Scripture know-
ledge is “a bit off.” (See recent par on Barrister
Higgins’ Biblical plural voting analogy.) Judas did
become a landed proprietor. Read what a little Melbourne
school girl called “The Acts of the Opossums,” chap. I,
verse 18 : “ Now this man purchased a field with the ie-
ward of iniquity, and falling headlong he burst asunder
in the midst.”
The Bulletin respectfully replies that its Scrip-
ture knowledge isn’t in the least degree “off.” The
Bible gives two accounts —that of Matthew being
that Judas went to the Council, and threw
his 30 pieces of silver back at that reverend
body, and then hanged himself. The other
account is the one quoted above. The Bulletin
accepts Matthew’s version for various reasons.
The story of a man hanging himself is compre-
hensible, but that of him bursting in a paddock
looks highly improbable. Also, there is a certain
dramatic force in the picture of Iscariot aiming
his bag of ill-gotten money at the joint head of the
Sanhedrim, and the littlo pile bouncing on the
pavement of the temple, and this paper absolutely
refuses to part with that beautiful incident. If it
didn’t happen then it ought to have. Anyhow,
for 1860 odd years it has been an accepted fact
that Judas d'id hang himself, and that involves
the acceptance of Matthew’s story according to
which he didn’t buy any land," and The Bulletin
declines to have its religious views upset at this
late hour by anybody.

Homeward Bound.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
With tallow casks alldunnaged tight, with tiers on
- tiers of bales,
With cargo crammed from hatch to hatch, she’s
racing for the sales.
A clipper barque, a model ship, a “ flyer ” through
and through,
O skipper bluff ! O skipper brave ! I would I
went with you !
’Tis turn of tide, ’tis time to sail, the flood is out-
ward flowing ;
Another glass, another shake, and then, my lads,
for going !
Black eyes ’long shore beam bright farewell, blue
eyes with tears grow bonny—
Around the capstan-head we go—“ Yo-ho!” and
“ Whisky - Johnny !”
He swings her round — The Aberfoyle— in slow and
stately way ;
Her house-flag flutters main-truck high; she’s
heading down the bay.
Then as his hawser slacks and strains, whilst
wharf-men cheer and shout,
Midst bo’s’n’s pipe and captain’s curse, the tug-
boat hauls her out.
’Tis “Good-bye, Sis!” and “ Good-bye, Sal! ”
and “ Good-bye,* Liz and Polly ! ”
Good-bye to all the girls ashore, and all a sailor’s
folly.
Blue Peter flies ; the hatches down ; our boys have
spent their money;
“Stand by, my lads, to ease her lines ! Stand
by ! ” and “ Whisky Johnny ! ”
Her master’s face is flushed and red—you see, lie’s
“ had a few ” ;
His voice grows husky as he roars loud orders to
the crew
(But when the wind comes up afar, and all is dark
as Death,
He will not lose his self-control, nor will he waste
his breath ;
But on the poop he’ll take his place, though seas
sweep o’er the railing,
To look the devil in the face with eyes that know
not quailing ;
He’d see her down if need should be, and when
all hope was gone he
Would raise his voice above the sea, and give ’em
“ Whisky Johnny ! ”)
Her sails were bent ten days ago, her decks are
scrubbed and clean,
Her spars are white as seagull’s breast, her hull is
painted green,
Her blocks are greased to run with ease, her yards
swing easy too —
.The time is short, the way is long— she has her
work to do !
The tide has turned, the wind is fair, the joys of
land are over—
Whilst ships are made to roll the seas, poor Jack
shall be a rover.
So sweethearts dark and sweethearts fair, look
blithely sad and bonny,
And wave your handkerchiefs once more—Heigh,
ho ! and “ Whisky Johnny ! ”
He’s cast his lines ; the tug’s about —her master
shouts “ Goodbye ! ”
Now some will sulk and some will laugh, but one
mayhap will sigh,
As from the ratlines glancing round, a second as
she swings,
He sees the land to starboard lie and thinks—of
foolish things !
’Tis homeward bound! ’tis homeward bound!
we’ve done by now with grieving,
For underneath our feet we feel the Old Eternal
heaving.
So lend a hand to loosen sail, and dry your eye
there, sonny !
The girls ahead are just as fair—“ Wey, ho ! ” and
“ Whisky Johnny ! ”
O, when he crams the canvas on, and shapes his
course away,
She dips and dives and shakes herself, like sea
bird at her play ;
She riots like a wilful child from punishment set
free,
To feel beneath her buoyant keel the open, joyous
sea.
For “ Homeward Bound ! ” for “ Homeward
Bound ! ” the breeze itself is singing,
And fore-and-aft, through shrouds and lines, the
melody goes ringing ; ■
She gathers speed “ More sail! ” he cries ; and as
he .claps it on he
Sings softly to the ship he loves, the strain of
“ Whisky Johnny ! ”
Hull down, at dusk— The Aberfoyle —and ere the
dark afar,
A line of foam upon her wake, she hails the
Evening Star.
A watchful shark on guard astern, a porpoise at
her bow,
An albatross to lead the way —she’seutting through
it now.
The waves may roll, the winds may rant, the
hungry sharks may follow
On hills of water she may pitch, in holes of water
wallow,
But on her course she yet will keep, that gallant
barque and bonny,
Until the dockers hear the ring &i “ Wey-hey !
Whisky Johnny ! ”
The last to leave of eight or ten, the first to sight
the Nore,
She beats the record homeward bound, she leads
the fleet once more.
And won’t the skipper greet his friends, and won’t
the agents cheer ?
And when her lines are fast again, O. won’t it
flow—the beer !
To Cousin Sis and Cousin Sal, and pretty Kate
and Polly,
To all the Jews and “seamen’s friends,” and all
the me-smates jolly,
To foaming pints and cosy fires and waiting blue
eyes bonny
She “ paddles in ” with joyous lilt of “ Wey-ho !
Whisky Johnny ! ”
From a recently-set Glasgow memorial-
stone—
By favor of Almighty God, in the presence of the Lord
Provost and the Town Council of Glasgow.
“ Nous an'res ! ” as it were.
Indiana State Board of Health has ordered
all schools to he provided with pencil-sterilizers
and safety drinking-fount tins. The, sterilizer is a
Russian iron oven, heated by gas, gasolene, or
alcohol, in which pens and pencils are to be heated
daily to 275 deg. Eahr. :
A Bit of a Wag.
[FOB THE BULLETIN.]
Smith-Jones was a dandy from the crown of his
tall silk-hat to the soles of his spruce tan shoes.
His coat was always stylish, and no Democratic
wrinkles marred the clear-cut contour of his curv-
ing vest. Nothing less than XXXXX would ade-
quately indicate the measure of his excellence in
personal appearance.
His pompous propriety, accompanied as it was
by the super-sensitiveness of overweening vanity,
must have been painful to himself. It certainly
was to his acquaintances and fellow civil-servants,
and would have been intolerable but for the fun
we got out of his peculiarities.
Smith-Jones was a stickler for style and good
form,” and had been known to walk home of a
wet afternoon rather than ride in a cab that had
lost the freshness of its paint and was not strictly
spick-and-span in every particular.
How lie could tolerate that pup of his—an ugly,
bushy-tailed specimen of no particular use or
breed—we could never quite understand. The
pup may have’ loved Smith-Jones, which was
very doubtful ; that he admired that conceited
individual with an awe-inspiring admiration was
apparent in every action.
As the pup grew into ungainly doghood his
abject worship increased. He seemed to live for
the express purpose of gazing at Smith-Jones with
a look that loudly proclaimed his canine belief in
his owner being the only true god. To anyone
but Smith-Jones the soulful earnestness of that
dog’s gaze would have been exasperating.
“ Oh, yes,” agreed Smith-Jones, when one of us
praised the dog, “ he’s a good animal ; but you
would never believe the trouble he gives one. One
has a p ‘sentiment that he will inadvertently do
someth,ng dreadful one of these days.”
A suggestion that Smith-Jones should leave
Carlo at home, tied up, was not taken He
objected to cruelty to dumb animals. All the
same we had our suspicions that the dog's open
admiration several times a day was almost indis-
pensable to its owner. So, though the character-
istics of eacli grew further and further apart, the
dapper civil-servant and his dog went to the office
in company as usual.
When passing street shop-fronts the painful
anxiety depicted on Smith-Jones’ face was a
caution to Carlo, and if the poor brute even
looked at another dog his master’s remonstrances
and entreaties and hurried surreptitious threaten-
ings were irritating to everybody within hearing.
In his spare time, Smith-Jones was given to
airing his figure on a certain street corner. There,
with expanding chest, and feet planted well apart,
he would discuss “form” and “style” and the
inevitable Carlo, with any A us he could manage
to button-hole.
At these street-corner meetings Carlo developed
the habit of pushing between his master’s legs
from behind, so as to get an uninterrupted view of
the face he so much admired. Meanwhile, his
great bushy tail waved majestically from the cleft
of Smith-Jones’ “street-sweeper.”
The habit was incurable. Nothing could lure
Carlo from his favorite position. Even kicks, or
prods with Smith-Jones’gold-headed malaccacane,
failed to shift him. The more he was kicked and
poked the more intense grew the look of admira-
tion in his big, brown, beseeching eyes. Even-
tually the dog won the day, and was allowed to
worship in peace.
But at last came a change. Somehow —never
mind how, but the reported remark of a girl had a
little to do with it —Smith-Jones was apprised of
the fact that the dog’s tail habitually stuck out
between the coat-tails and perpetually wagged
with a slow and solemn wag. Next day, Smith-
Jones walked down to the office without his com-
panion.
“ I had to get rid of him,” he told us in strictest
confidence, and with a perceptible shudder;
“ people actually conceived the astonishing im-
pression, don’tcherknow, that I was wagging my
own tail ! ”
JOHN DRAYMAN.
“Safrica.” A Bulletin present to the
South African papers.
A Melb. correspondent asks :
Is there any “record” in existence of rapid writing
(literary value apart as long as no verbal blunders)?
September Windsor asserts that Miss Bradd. n can write
about 1500 words an hour. Surely impossible to keep
this up even mechanically. The writing is—of course!
“ beautiful,” “clear,” and all the rest of it.
The fastest writer The Bulletin ever knew could
polish off 2000 words in one hour (not in the next)
—33 words a minute to Miss B.’s 25.
Sec. 27 of Q. Civil Service Act provides
that—
When a vacancy occurs in any Dept, of the Service . . .
it shall be filled by the promotion of either the same class
or that immediately below it.
Sec. 29 provides that —
If, in any special case, it is expedient in the interests of
the Public Service to appoint some person who is not
then in the Service, and to place such person in any class,
the Governor in Council may appoint such person
accordingly without either examination or probation, &c.
The latter provision is now being run for all it is
worth, and barristers, lawyers, and surveyors,
who are unable to earn their living at their'pro-
fessions, are run into the Civil Service under Sec.
29 if their social influence is strong enough. The
Lands Dept, seems the present dumping-ground ;
probably the Stipendiary Magistracy will be next
attacked.
The pretty complete demonstration that
the Australian artesian system doesn’t run through
the Victorian mallee, and so south to the ocean, is
on the whole satisfactory ; at any rate, the chances
are that a still larger area of back country is
watered ; possibly the southern outlet is in the
Australian Bight. Meanwhile there are few
more depressing sights in Central Australia—land
of depressing sights though it be-than the waste
of artesian waters that have been tapped—in the
far north of S.A. especially.

asked for work, and—would you believe it !- when
I offered to put him on the garden at Is. a day the
impudent fellow actually said he couldn’t think of
taking advantage of my generosity, and couldn’t
I make it sixpence/”

“In the year 1890 my ankle broke out, and I went to a doctor who told

St. Vincent’s Hospital, Sydney, and after a month’s treatment there came

four years, until my wife, hearing of your Sarsaparilla, got me a bottle.
She persuaded me to give it a thorough trial. Soon I noticed that the sore
on my ankle began to heal, then the pain diminished, and after a course of
six bottles I became as well as ever I was in my life, and I remain in the
best of health.
Everybody for miles around here knows what tribulation I went through.
